Rhetorical Devices: Tarshīh, Isti'āra, and Tajrīd
The commentator analyzes the rhetorical figures in the line 'بحر المعاني' and offers two interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish between tarshīh, isti'āra bil-kīnāya, isti'āra takhyīliyya, and tajrīd in a given Arabic text.

Core Idea
The 'sea of meanings' is a rich metaphor that unfolds into multiple rhetorical layers.

The commentator offers two ways to understand the phrase 'sea of meanings' (بحر المعاني). In the first, it is a similitudinal genitive that is further amplified by 'depths' and 'basins' (tarshīh). In the second, a whole set of metaphorical figures emerges: the intellect is compared to a ship (isti'āra bil-kīnāya), the sea and depths are imaginary metaphors (isti'āra takhyīliyya), and 'meanings' is an abstraction (tajrīd). This lesson will help you unpack these rhetorical layers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base comparison (tashbīh)
The genitive construction 'sea of meanings' is described as 'idāfat al-mushabbah bihi ilā al-mushabbah' — the thing compared to (sea) is added to the thing compared (meanings). So 'meanings' are like a sea. Note the source text: 'الإضافة في بحر المعاني من إضافة المشبه به إلى المشبه'.
This is a common rhetorical device in Arabic poetry.
2
Recognize tarshīh (amplification)
The additional words 'لجج' (depths) and 'حوض' (basins) are not just extra details; they reinforce the simile by suggesting vastness and the need for a vessel. The commentator calls this 'ترشيح للتشبيه'.
Tarshīh adds details that suit the imaginary comparison.
3
Distinguish the two metaphorical readings
The commentator gives a second interpretation: the intellect (حجا/عقل) is implicitly compared to a ship (استعارة بالكناية — metonymic metaphor) because both are means to reach a goal. Then the sea and depths become imaginary metaphors (استعارة تخييلية) that are not real but support the image.
The first reading takes everything as part of a single simile; the second breaks it into multiple figures.
4
Understand tajrīd (abstraction)
The term 'المعاني' (meanings) is called 'تجريد' — abstraction. This means mentioning something that suits the compared; here, 'meanings' are what the intellect seeks, so they are abstracted from the comparison to the sea.
Tajrīd is a device where you mention a property or object that is appropriate for the thing being compared.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (صَلَّى عَلَيْهِ اللهُ) لفظه لفظ الخبر ومعناه الإنشاء؛ أي: الدُّعاء له بالرحمة على وجه التَّقرُّب مِنَ المولى على الاحتياج لها. فإن قلت: كيف أفرد الصَّلاة عن السَّلام وهو مكروه عند جمهور المحدثين؛ كما نص عليه زروق في شرح الوَغْلِيسِيَّةِ؛ لاقترانهما في الآية الكريمة. الكلام على مقدمة الناظم (٢) أَتَى بِالصَّلَاةِ عَلَى النَّبِيِّ صلى الله عليه وسلم عِنْدَ ذِكْرِ اسْمِهِ الشَّرِيفِ؛ لِحَدِيثِ: «البَخِيلُ كُلُّ البَخِيلِ مَنْ ذُكِرْتُ عِنْدَهُ فَلَمْ يُصَلِّ عَلَيَّ»، وَحَدِيثِ: «رَغِمَ أَنْفُ رَجُلٍ ذُكِرْتُ عِنْدَهُ فَلَمْ يُصَلِّ عَلَيَّ» أَخْرَجَهُ التِّرْمِذِيُّ وَغَيْرُهُ. وَالإِضَافَةُ فِي بَحْرِ المَعَانِي مِنْ إِضَافَةِ المُشَبَّهِ بِهِ إِلَى المُشَبَّهِ؛ أَيْ: يَخُوضُ فِي المَعَانِي الَّتِي هِيَ كَالبَحْرِ فِي اتِّسَاعِ كُلِّ مِنْهُمَا اتِّسَاعاً يَحْتَاجُ فِي سُلُوكِهِ إِلَى آلَةٍ، وَذِكْرُ اللُّجَجِ وَالحَوْضِ تَرْشِيحٌ لِلتَّشْبِيهِ. وَيُحْتَمَلُ أَنْ يَكُونَ شَبَّهَ فِي نَفْسِهِ الحِجَا الَّذِي هُوَ العَقْلُ بِالسَّفِينَةِ فِي التَّوَصُّلِ بِكُلِّ مِنْهُمَا إِلَى المُرَادِ، وَهَذِهِ اسْتِعَارَةٌ بِالكِنَايَةِ، وَذِكْرُ اللُّجَجِ وَالبَحْرِ اسْتِعَارَةٌ تَخْيِيلِيَّةٌ، وَالمَعَانِي تَجْرِيدٌ، وَهُوَ أَنْ تَذْكُرَ مَا يُلائِمُ المُشَبَّهَ. وَاللُّجَجُ جَمْعُ: «لُجَّةِ»، وَهِيَ: مُعْظَمُ المَاءِ. وَمَعْنَى البَيْتِ: تَأْبِيدُ الصَّلَاةِ عَلَى النَّبِيِّ صلى الله عليه وسلم بِدَوَامِ تَفَكَّرِ العَقْلِ فِي مَسَائِلِ العِلْمِ، وَذَلِكَ بِدَوَامِ العُلَمَاءِ، وَدَوَامُهُمْ لِقِيَامِ السَّاعَةِ. قلت: يحتمل أنَّه قد قرن بينهما لفظاً، وأسقط السَّلام خطأ، أو ذهب على أنَّه لا كراهة في ذلك؛ قال الأجهوري: شاع في كتب المتقدمين من أهل مذهبنا وغيرهم قولهم: «قال عليه السَّلام» ونحوه مقتصرين على «السَّلام»، وأخبرني الثقة أنه رآه بخط الباجي كذلك، وإذا لم يكره إفراد السَّلام فالصَّلاة أَوْلى. قوله: (وَحَدِيثِ: «رَغِمَ» ... إلخ) هذا الحديث أخرجه الترمذي عن أبي هريرة رضي الله تعالى عنه، ومعنى «رغم»: ألصق بالرُّغام؛ أي: التُّراب. قوله: (جَمْعُ: «لُجَّةٍ») أي: بضم اللام، وأما بفتحها فهو: الخصومة واختلاط الأصوات؛ قال الشاعر: [من الرجز] في لُجَةٍ أَمْسِكْ فُلاناً عَنْ فُلِ.
His statement: 'May God bless him' — its wording is indicative (reportive) but its meaning is performative (invocative); i.e., supplication for mercy upon him in a manner of seeking nearness to the Lord, with need for it. If you say: How did he single out 'blessing' from 'peace'? And that is disliked by the majority of hadith scholars, as Zarrūq stated in his commentary on al-Waghlīsiyya, due to their conjunction in the noble verse. The discourse on the poet's introduction (2): He mentioned the blessing upon the Prophet, peace be upon him, upon mentioning his noble name, because of the hadith: 'The miser, the complete miser, is he who is mentioned in my presence and does not bless me,' and the hadith: 'May his nose be rubbed in dust, a man who is mentioned in my presence and does not bless me,' recorded by al-Tirmidhi and others. The genitive construction in 'sea of meanings' is of the imagined to the imaginer; i.e., he delves into meanings that are like a sea in their vastness, each requiring an instrument to traverse. And mentioning 'depths' and 'basins' is amplification (tarshīḥ) of the simile. And it is possible that he, in himself, compared the intellect ('aql) to a ship in attaining the desired goal through each, and this is a metonymic metaphor (isti'āra bil-kīnāya), while mentioning 'depths' and 'sea' is an imaginary metaphor (isti'āra takhyīliyya), and 'meanings' is abstraction (tajrīd), which is to mention what suits the compared. And 'lujaj' is the plural of 'lujja', which is the main body of water. And the meaning of the line: the perpetuation of blessing upon the Prophet, peace be upon him, with the perpetuity of the intellect's reflection in the questions of knowledge, and that is with the perpetuity of scholars, and their perpetuity until the establishment of the Hour. I (the commentator) say: It is possible that he combined them verbally, and omitted 'peace' by mistake, or he held that there is no dislike in that; al-Ujhūrī said: It was common in the books of the early scholars of our school and others that they said 'peace be upon him' and similar, restricting themselves to 'peace', and a reliable person informed me that he saw it in al-Bājī's handwriting likewise. And if singling out 'peace' is not disliked, then 'blessing' is more appropriate. His statement: (And the hadith: 'May his nose be rubbed in dust' etc.) — this hadith was recorded by al-Tirmidhi from Abu Hurayra, may God be pleased with him, and the meaning of 'raghima' is: stuck to the dust (turāb). His statement: (plural of 'lujja') i.e., with a damma on the lām, but with a fatḥa it means 'dispute and mixing of voices'; the poet said: [from rajaz] 'In a dispute, restrain so-and-so from so-and-so.'
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharḥ) of the opening line of al-Sullam al-Murunq. The commentator explains each part of the line: 'May God bless him' is analyzed as a performative invocation (inshā'), not a report (khabar). A question arises about the individual mention of 'blessing' without 'peace', which is considered disliked by many hadith scholars because the Quran pairs them. The commentator then discusses the reason the poet included the blessing: to follow the prophetic hadiths that urge blessing upon the Prophet when his name is mentioned. Next, he delves into the rhetorical figures in the line. 'Sea of meanings' is a metaphorical genitive (idāfa tashbīhiyya), comparing meanings to a sea in vastness, requiring an instrument (like a ship) to traverse. The mention of 'depths' (lujaj) and 'basins' (ḥawḍ) is 'tarshīḥ', amplification of the simile. He also proposes another reading: the intellect (ḥijā) is implicitly compared to a ship (isti'āra bil-kīnāya), while 'depths' and 'sea' are 'isti'āra takhyīliyya' (imaginary metaphor), and 'meanings' is 'tajrīd' (abstraction), which is mentioning what suits the thing compared. The collective meaning of the line is the perpetual blessing upon the Prophet as long as the intellect reflects on knowledge, i.e., as long as scholars exist. The commentator then addresses the earlier question about omitting 'peace', suggesting he either combined them verbally and omitted 'peace' by mistake, or he considered it not disliked. He cites al-Ujhūrī's report that early scholars often used 'peace' alone, so using 'blessing' alone is even more acceptable. Finally, he notes the plural 'lujaj' and the linguistic distinction between 'lujja' (water mass) and 'luja' (dispute).

Key Terms
الإنشاء
الترشيح
الاستعارة بالكناية
الاستعارة التخييلية
التجريد
لجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the second interpretation of the line, what is the role of 'meanings' (المعاني)?
A) It is the imagined (المشبه به) in a simile.
B) It is an imaginary metaphor (استعارة تخييلية).
C) It is an abstraction (تجريد) that suits the compared.
D) It is a metonymic metaphor (استعارة بالكناية).

Key Takeaway
The line 'بحر المعاني' can be read as a single amplified simile or as a layered composition of metaphor (isti'āra), imaginary metaphor (isti'āra takhyīliyya), and abstraction (tajrīd).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Identify and analyze the rhetorical devices in the hadith: 'مثل المؤمنين في توادهم وتراحمهم كمثل الجسد الواحد' — find the simile, any amplification, and possible metaphorical elements.
?
2. Choose a line from a classical Arabic poem and identify: (a) the base comparison, (b) any tarshīh, (c) any isti'āra, (d) any tajrīd. Explain your reasoning.

Refutation of the Prohibition of Logic: The Two Proofs and Their Weakness
Author refutes the first opinion that logic is prohibited.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and evaluate the two arguments used to claim that logic is prohibited and the author's refutations.

Core Idea
Logic is innate; the philosophers only added labels. Thus its foreign origin does not make it unlawful.

In this passage, the author confronts the first opinion that studying logic is forbidden. He presents the two proofs adduced by its proponents, then dismantles each one. This is a classic example of critical reasoning in Islamic scholarship, where a discipline is defended by separating its essential core from its historical packaging.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two proofs against logic
Locate the two arguments: (1) logic is from the philosophers who have corrupt beliefs; (2) the Salaf did not study it.
2
Examine the first refutation
The author argues that many beneficial sciences (e.g., astronomy, medicine) came from philosophers but were adopted as obligatory or recommended.
3
Examine the second refutation
The author says logic's core is innate inference from correlatives/opposites, so the Salaf used it without the fancy terms.
4
Assess the strength of the author's critique
Consider whether the examples are convincing and whether the claim of innateness is sufficient to counter the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَالقَوْلُ الأَوَّلُ : بِتَحْرِيمِهِ، عَزَاهُ لِابْنِ الصَّلَاحِ وَالنَّوَوِيِّ، استدل القائل بالمنع بدليلين : الأول : أنَّه من علوم الفلاسفة، وهم من أهل العقائد الفاسدة، يوشك أنَّ مَن استغرق همَّتَه في علومهم أن يسترقوه في بعض العقائد. الثَّاني : أنَّ الصَّحابة ومَن في معناهم مِنَ السَّلف الصَّالح لم يشتغلوا به، ولو كان محتاجاً إليه ما أغفلوه. وكلا الدليلين في غاية السُّقوط : أمَّا الأَوَّل : فإِنَّ كثيراً من علوم الفلاسفة نقلت إلى الإسلام، وأثبتتها الأئمة على سبيل الوجوب أو الندب كـ: التوقيت، والطب، والحساب. والثاني : أنَّ الـمـنـطـق مركوز في الطباع بنقل الإجماع؛ إذ حـاصلـه اسـتـدلال بـوجـود أحـدِ المتلازمين على الآخر، وبعدمه على عدمه، أو استدلال بوجود أحد المتعاندين على عدم الآخر، أو بعدمه على وجوده، وهذا لا ينكره عاقل، وحينئذ فليس للفلاسفة إِلَّا مجرد النسبة والاصطلاح، ولا جرم أنَّ مَن له ذهن سليم لا يحتاج إلى الاصطلاحات المنطقيَّة، كما لا يحتاج العربي إلى تعلم اصطلاحات العربية .
The first opinion: its prohibition, attributed to Ibn al-Salah and al-Nawawi. The one who argues for prohibition adduced two proofs: The first: that it is from the sciences of the philosophers, and they are people of corrupt beliefs; it is likely that whoever devotes his energy to their sciences will be seduced into some of their beliefs. The second: that the Companions and those like them from the righteous predecessors did not occupy themselves with it; if it were necessary, they would not have neglected it. Both proofs are extremely weak: As for the first, indeed many of the sciences of the philosophers were transferred to Islam, and the Imams established them as obligatory or recommended, such as astronomy, medicine, and arithmetic. As for the second, indeed logic is ingrained in human nature by consensus; for its essence is inferring from the existence of one of two correlatives to the other, and from its non-existence to its non-existence, or inferring from the existence of one of two opposites to the non-existence of the other, or from its non-existence to its existence. No rational person denies this. Thus, the philosophers have only the naming and terminology; and indeed one with a sound mind does not need the logical terminologies, just as an Arab does not need to learn the terminologies of Arabic grammar.
Source pages p. 414

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the first opinion that studying logic is prohibited. The author (Bannani, commenting on al-Sullam) presents the opinion attributed to Ibn al-Salah and al-Nawawi, who argued that logic is from the sciences of the philosophers with corrupt beliefs, and that the early Muslims did not study it. The author then dismantles both proofs. For the first proof, he notes that many sciences originally from the philosophers (like astronomy, medicine, mathematics) were adopted by Islam as beneficial or even obligatory. Therefore their origin does not entail corruption. For the second proof, he argues that the core logical reasoning is innate and universal; it is simply inference from the existence or non-existence of correlatives or opposites. No rational person denies this basic reasoning. What the philosophers have added is mere terminology and naming conventions. Therefore, a sound mind does not need their technical jargon, just as a native Arabic speaker does not need formal grammar rules to communicate. This defense positions logic as a natural tool, not a foreign import that corrupts faith.

Key Terms
المنطق
الفلاسفة
المتلازمان
المتعاندان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the second proof (that the Salaf did not study logic)?
A) He argues that the Salaf were not rational.
B) He states that logic is innate and its core reasoning is naturally used, so they practiced it without the technical terms.
C) He claims the Salaf actually studied logic secretly.
D) He says the second proof is weak because it is based on personal opinion.

Key Takeaway
The author defends logic by distinguishing its innate core from its technical terminology, arguing that its foreign origin does not make it prohibited and that the Salaf used it intuitively.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the two proofs against logic in the passage and summarize the author's refutation in your own words.
?
Evaluate the strength of the first refutation: Do you think it fully addresses the concern about corrupt beliefs? Why or why not?

The Definition of Created Knowledge as the Form of a Thing in the Mind
Sharh defines created knowledge and clarifies 'thing' linguistically.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components of the logical definition of created knowledge and explain the significance of the linguistic sense of 'thing' for its scope.

Core Idea
The definition of knowledge as the form of a thing in the mind hinges on the broad linguistic sense of 'thing' to include both existent and non-existent.

This passage provides a precise definition of created knowledge (al-ʿilm al-ḥādith) as the acquisition of the form of a thing in the mind. The commentator carefully clarifies that 'thing' (al-shayʾ) here is used in its linguistic sense, not the restricted technical sense of the theologians. This subtle distinction has major implications for what counts as 'knowledge' in logic and epistemology, as it includes both true and false beliefs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find the definiendum in the Arabic: 'al-ʿilm al-ḥādith' (created knowledge). Note the qualification 'al-ḥādith' to distinguish from Allah's eternal knowledge.
The passage explains that merely mentioning 'types' already excludes eternal knowledge.
2
Extract the genus and differentia
The definition is 'ḥuṣūl ṣūrat al-shayʾ fī al-ʿaql' (acquisition of the form of a thing in the mind). The genus is 'acquisition' (ḥuṣūl) and the differentia is 'the form of a thing in the mind' (ṣūrat al-shayʾ fī al-ʿaql).
This is a real definition (hadd haqiqi) aiming to capture the essence of created knowledge.
3
Analyze the scope of 'thing'
The commentator stresses that 'thing' here is used in the linguistic sense, applying to existent and non-existent, simple and relative. This broadens the definition to include all mental representations.
The Mu'tazila consider non-existents as 'things' with fixed essences, which the verifying theologians reject.
4
Evaluate what the definition includes
The passage notes that this definition covers opinion (ẓann), compound ignorance (jahl murakkab), imitation (taqlīd), and even insanity and illusion. The commentator acknowledges that calling these 'knowledge' contradicts language and custom but accepts it as a technical term.
This shows that the logical definition of knowledge is broader than the everyday or religious meaning.

أَنْوَاعُ العِلْمِ الحَادِثِ (وَصْفُهُ العِلْمَ بـ «الحَادِثِ» ؛ لِزِيَادَةِ البَيَانِ، وَإِلَّا فَذِكْرُ الأَنْوَاعِ يُخْرِجُ العِلْمَ القَدِيمَ ؛ لأَنَّهُ وَاحِدٌ لَا تَعَدُّدَ لَهُ عَلَى الصَّحِيح عِنْدَ الجُمْهُورِ مِنْ أَهْلِ السُّنَّةِ. وَالعِلْمُ الحَادِثُ عِنْدَ القَوْمِ هُوَ : «حُصُولُ صُورَةِ الشَّيْء فِي العَقْلِ . وَالشَّيْءُ هُنَا بِالمَعْنَى اللُّغَوِيِّ، فَيَصْدُقُ بِالمُوجُودِ وَالمَعْدُومِ، وَبِالمُفْرَدِ وَالنِّسْبَةِ ؛ مُطَابِقَةٌ لِلْوَاقِعِ وَغَيْرَ مُطَابِقَةٍ. قوله: ( لِزيَادَةِ البَيَانِ) فيه تنكيتُ على مَن يقول : إنَّه لإخراج علم الله تعالى القديم، ووجه الرَّدُّ : ما أشار له الشَّارح مِن أنَّ العلم القديم خرج بذكر «الأنواع» أوَّلاً . قوله : ( عِنْدَ القَوْم) المراد بهم : الحكماء، وهذا التّعريف مبني على الوجود الذهني، ويتناول: الظَّنَّ، والجهل المركَّب، والتَّقليد، بل الشَّلَّ والوهم وتسميتها : عِلْماً» مخالف للغة والعرف العام والشَّرع، لكن لا مشاحة في الاصطلاح. قوله : (وَالشَّيْءُ هُنَا بِالمَعْنَى اللُّغَوِيٌّ) أي : لا بالمعنى المصطلح عليه عند محققي المتكلمين؛ لأنَّ المعدوم عندهم ليس بشيء، ومرادهم: نفي تقرر الذوات في الخارج بدون الـوجـود ردا على المعتزلة، وذلك أنَّ أكثر المعتزلة قالوا : المعدوم الممكن قبل وجوده شيءٌ وذوات ثابتة متقرّرة في نفسها في الخارج ؛ إلَّا أنَّ الممكنات قبل أن تكسى بنور الوجود كأشياء مخبوءة في بيتٍ مظلم، ثمَّ يفيض الله تعالى على من شاء منها نور الوجود، فيبرز للعيان، فالفاعل المختارُ إِنَّما فعل عندهم الوجود لا الذوات . وَصُورَةُ الشَّيْء : مِثَالُهُ المُطَابِقُ لَهُ، المُنْطَبِعُ فِي العَقْلِ انْطِبَاعَ الصُّورِ المُبْصَرَاتِ في المرآة.
(The types of created knowledge) He describes knowledge with 'created' for additional clarification; otherwise mentioning 'types' excludes eternal knowledge because it is one with no multiplicity according to the correct view of the majority of Ahl al-Sunnah. Created knowledge according to the folk is: 'The acquisition of the form of a thing in the mind.' And 'thing' here is in the linguistic sense, so it applies to the existent and the non-existent, to the simple and the relative, conforming to reality or not. His statement: (for additional clarification) contains a subtle criticism of those who say it is to exclude the eternal knowledge of Allah; the response is what the commentator indicated: that eternal knowledge is excluded by mentioning 'types' first. His statement: (according to the folk) means the philosophers, and this definition is based on mental existence, and includes opinion, compound ignorance, and imitation, even insanity and illusion, and calling these 'knowledge' contradicts language, common custom, and revelation, but there is no harm in terminology. His statement: ('thing' here in the linguistic sense) i.e., not in the technical sense of the verifying theologians because the non-existent is not a thing to them; their intention is to deny the determination of essences in the external world without existence, in refutation of the Mu'tazila. Indeed, most Mu'tazila said the possible non-existent before its existence is a thing with fixed essences determined in themselves externally, but possibles before being clothed with the light of existence are like things hidden in a dark room, then Allah causes to emanate upon whomever He wills the light of existence, so they appear to the eye. Thus the free agent only creates existence according to them, not essences. And the form of the thing is its matching likeness, impressed in the mind as the forms of visible objects are impressed in a mirror.
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) on the definition of created knowledge (al-ʿilm al-ḥādith). It begins by justifying why the author of the matn qualified ‘knowledge’ with ‘created’: to provide clarity, since merely mentioning ‘types’ already excludes eternal knowledge—which is one and unchanging according to Ahl al-Sunnah. The passage then gives the core definition: ‘The acquisition of the form of a thing in the mind.’ The commentator explains that ‘thing’ (shayʾ) here is used in its broad linguistic sense, not the narrower technical sense of the theologians (mutakallimūn) who deny that non-existents are ‘things.’ Thus the definition covers both existent and non-existent objects, simple and relative notions, whether they correspond to reality or not. The commentator then addresses possible objections: he notes that this definition includes even false beliefs, opinions, and compound ignorance, which are not really ‘knowledge’ in the linguistic or religious sense, but this is acceptable as a technical term. He then clarifies the position of the verifying theologians (muḥaqqiqūn) who say the non-existent is not a thing, refuting the Muʿtazila who consider possible non-existents to have fixed essences. Finally, he defines ‘form of the thing’ as a matching likeness impressed in the mind, analogizing it to the reflection of visible objects in a mirror. The entire passage thus provides both the definition and the necessary contextual clarifications for understanding what the logicians mean by ‘knowledge.’

الشيء
صورة الشيء

The definition of created knowledge as the acquisition of the form of a thing in the mind uses the broad linguistic sense of 'thing' to include all mental representations, regardless of truth or existence.

Objection to Defining Knowledge as Acquisition and al-Sa'd's Response
Objection and response to the primary definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical flaw in an objection to a definition and evaluate the correctness of the response.

Core Idea
An objection that confuses the definiens with a mere word collapses when the full definiens is restored.

This passage presents a subtle objection to the definition of knowledge as 'the acquisition of the form in the mind.' The objector claims that 'acquisition' describes the form, not the knower. But al-Sa'd al-Taftazani shows that the definiens is the entire phrase, not just one word. Understanding this exchange trains you to spot misdirected objections in Islamic dialectic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original definition and the objection
Find the definition of knowledge being discussed and the exact wording of the objection. The objector says 'acquisition is a description of the form, while knowledge is a description of the knower, so it cannot be interpreted by it.'
The objection is that the definiens (ḥuṣūl) does not apply to the same subject as the definiendum (ʿilm).
2
Find the mismatch claimed by the objector
The objector argues that 'acquisition' (al-ḥuṣūl) is an attribute of the form (ṣūra), not of the knower (ʿālim). Since knowledge is an attribute of the knower, the definition fails.
This is a common type of objection in jadal: the definiens must apply to the same subject as the definiendum.
3
Analyze al-Sa'd's response
Al-Sa'd responds that the definiens is not just 'acquisition' but 'the acquisition of the form in the mind.' The knower is characterized by having the form in his mind, just as he is characterized by knowledge.
By adding 'of the form in the mind', the definiens becomes a description of the knower's state.
4
Evaluate who wins this exchange
The response resolves the objection because it shows the definiens as a whole applies to the knower. The objector mistakenly isolated a part.
This is a successful defense under the rules of jadal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَاعْتُرِضَ تَعْرِيفُ العِلْمِ بِمَا مَرَّ : بِأَنَّ الحُصُولَ وَصْفٌ لِلصُّورَةِ، وَالعِلْمُ وَصْفٌ لِلعَالِمِ، فَلَا يُفَسِّرُ بِهِ . وَأَجَابَ السَّعْدُ : بِأَنَّ المُعَرِّفَ لِلْعِلْم هُوَ حُصُولُ الصُّورَةِ فِي العَقْلِ، لَا مُجَرَّدُ الحُصُولِ، وَالعَالِمُ كَمَا يَتَّصِفُ بِالعِلْم يَتَّصِفُ بِحُصُولِ الصُّورَةِ فِي عَقْلِهِ . اهـ . وَعَرَّفَهُ ابْنُ عَرَفَةَ وَالسَّيِّدُ بِـ : «أَنَّهُ الإِدْرَالُ»، وَهُوَ مُسَاوِ لِلأَوَّلِ . وَمَنْ عَرَّفَ العِلْمَ هَهُنَا بِـ: «أَنَّهُ مَعْرِفَةُ المَعْلُومٍ عَلَى مَا هُوَ بِهِ»، قوله : (بـ: «أَنَّهُ الإِدْرَالُ) فيه : أنَّ الإدراك مجاز عن العلم؛ لأنَّ معناه الحقيقي هو اللُّحُوقُ والوصول، والمجازُ لا يُستعمل في الحدود؛ فإن أجيب : باشتهاره في معنى العلم، قلنا : يلزم عليه إذاً تعريف الشيء بنفسه، فكأنه قيل: هو العلم. عنه . قوله : (وَهُوَ مُسَاوِ لِلأَوَّلِ) أعنى : حصول صورة الشَّيء في العقل، وفيه تسامح؛ لأنَّ الإدراك ليس هو الحصول، بل هو الوصول؛ يقال : أَدْرَكْتُ الشَّيْء» وصلت إليه، والحصول لازم . قوله : (مَعْرِفَةُ المَعْلُومِ) هذا التعريف للقاضي أبي بكر الباقلاني. وأورد عليه : أنَّ فيه دوراً، وأن قوله: «عَلَى مَا هُوَ بِهِ» قيد زائد لا حاجة إليه ؛ إذ المعرفة لا تكون إلا كذلك؛ لأنَّ إدراك الشَّيء على غير ما هو به جهالة، لا معرفة.
And the definition of knowledge as mentioned was objected to: because ‘acquisition’ is a description of the form, while knowledge is a description of the knower, so it cannot be interpreted by it. And al-Sa‘d answered: that the definiens of knowledge is the acquisition of the form in the mind, not merely acquisition, and the knower is characterized by the acquisition of the form in his mind just as he is characterized by knowledge. End. And Ibn ‘Arafa and al-Sayyid defined it as: ‘It is perception,’ and it is equivalent to the first. And whoever defined knowledge here as: ‘It is the cognition of the known as it is,’ his statement: (as ‘it is perception’) in it: that perception is a metaphor for knowledge; because its literal meaning is attaining and reaching, and metaphor is not used in definitions; if it is answered that it is common in the meaning of knowledge, we say: then that necessitates defining a thing by itself, as if it were said: it is knowledge. About it. His statement: (and it is equivalent to the first) i.e., acquisition of the form of a thing in the mind, and there is leniency in it; because perception is not acquisition, but rather reaching; it is said: ‘I perceived the thing’ meaning I reached it, and acquisition is a consequence. His statement: (cognition of the known) this definition is by al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani. And it was objected to him: that there is circularity in it, and that his statement: ‘as it is’ is a redundant qualification not needed; because cognition is only that way; for perceiving a thing other than as it is is ignorance, not cognition.
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on the definition of knowledge. It first presents an objection: the word ‘acquisition’ (al-ḥuṣūl) describes the form, not the knower, so it cannot define knowledge which is an attribute of the knower. Al-Sa‘d al-Taftazani responds by clarifying that the definiens is not the mere word ‘acquisition’ but the entire phrase ‘the acquisition of the form in the mind.’ The knower is indeed characterized by ‘having the form in his mind,’ so the definition is valid. The passage then introduces two alternative definitions. The first, by Ibn ‘Arafa and al-Sayyid al-Sharif al-Jurjani, defines knowledge as ‘perception’ (al-idrāk). The commentator critiques this: ‘perception’ is a metaphor for knowledge, meaning literal ‘attaining,’ and metaphors are not used in definitions. If one argues it is common usage, then it defines knowledge by itself, creating circularity. Moreover, ‘perception’ implies reaching, not the presence of a form, though the latter is a consequence. The second alternative is by al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani: ‘cognition of the known as it is.’ The commentator objects on two grounds: circularity (since ‘known’ implies prior knowledge) and redundancy of the phrase ‘as it is’ because cognition by definition is conforming to reality; otherwise it is ignorance. Thus the passage defends the original definition by criticizing alternatives.

حصول
صورة
عاقل

D) Because the definition uses a metaphor

Key Takeaway
An objection that isolates a part of a definition and claims it does not apply to the definiendum is refuted by considering the full definiens.

Critique of Alternative Definitions of Knowledge: Perception and Cognition
Critique of alternative definitions of knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify circularity and redundancy in definitions of knowledge.

Core Idea
A definition of knowledge must avoid both circularity (presupposing the term) and redundancy (including unnecessary conditions).

After defending the primary definition, the commentator critiques two alternative definitions: knowledge as 'perception' (idrāk) and as 'cognition of the known as it is' (maʿrifa). Both are shown to be flawed: one is metaphorical and circular, the other is redundant. These critiques sharpen your ability to evaluate definitions in the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two alternative definitions
Find the definitions proposed by Ibn 'Arafa/al-Sayyid (knowledge as 'perception') and by al-Baqillani (knowledge as 'cognition of the known as it is').
These are common alternative definitions in Kalam.
2
Analyze the objection to 'perception'
Why is idrāk considered a metaphor? Its literal meaning is 'attaining/reaching,' not intellectual apprehension. If one argues it is commonly used for knowledge, then the definition becomes circular: 'knowledge is knowledge.'
Metaphors are not allowed in proper definitions (ḥudūd).
3
Analyze the objection to 'cognition of the known as it is'
What is circular about this definition? The term 'known' (maʿlūm) already implies knowledge, so defining knowledge by it is circular. Also, the phrase 'as it is' is redundant because cognition by definition is perception in accordance with reality.
Redundancy (al-ziyāda) is a flaw because it adds no information.
4
Compare the flaws
Both definitions fail: one due to metaphor/circularity, the other due to circularity and redundancy. Neither satisfies the conditions of a valid definition.
A valid definition must be literal, non-circular, and include only necessary parts.

The Definition of Knowledge: Form and Presence in the Intellect
Defines the 'form' of a thing and defends the definition of knowledge as its presence in the intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the definition of 'image' (surah) and evaluate al-Sa'd al-Taftazani's defense of the definition of knowledge as the obtainment of the image in the intellect.

Core Idea
Knowledge is the mental presence of an exact likeness of the object – not a physical imprint but an intelligible form.

In this passage, we examine the core definition of knowledge in Islamic logic: the obtainment of the 'image' (surah) in the intellect. An objector claims this conflates the property of the image with the property of the knower. Al-Sa'd al-Taftazani defends the definition, and we will walk through his reasoning to understand how the definition holds.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of 'image' (surah)
The passage defines surah as the exact mental likeness of a thing, imprinted in the intellect. Note the analogy with a mirror: visible images imprint in a mirror, and similarly, mental forms imprint in the mind.
The imprinting is conceptual, not physical.
2
Identify the objection
The objection states that 'obtainment' (husul) is a description of the image, while knowledge is a description of the knower. Therefore, defining knowledge by 'obtainment' seems to misattribute a property of the image to the knower.
This is a common philosophical confusion between the definiens and its parts.
3
Analyze al-Sa'd's response
Al-Sa'd responds that the definer is the entire phrase 'obtainment of the image in the intellect', not just 'obtainment'. So the knower is described by having the image present in his intellect, which is exactly what knowledge is.
The whole is greater than the sum of its parts in definition.
4
Apply the analogy of imprinting
The commentary clarifies that imprinting is not physical; it is conceptual. The soul imprints intelligible forms, whereas a mirror imprints only sensible forms. This distinction preserves the mental nature of knowledge.
This helps avoid the misconception that knowledge is a physical impression.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَصُورَةُ الشَّيْء : مِثَالُهُ المُطَابِقُ لَهُ، المُنْطَبِعُ فِي العَقْلِ انْطِبَاعَ الصُّورِ المُبْصَرَاتِ في المرآة.
وَاعْتُرِضَ تَعْرِيفُ العِلْمِ بِمَا مَرَّ : بِأَنَّ الحُصُولَ وَصْفٌ لِلصُورَةِ، وَالعِلْمُ وَصْفٌ لِلعَالِمِ، فَلَا يُفَسِّرُ بِهِ .
وَأَجَابَ السَّعْدُ : بِأَنَّ المُعَرِّفَ لِلْعِلْم هُوَ حُصُولُ الصُورَةِ فِي العَقْلِ، لَا مُجَرَّدُ الحُصُولِ، وَالعَالِمُ كَمَا يَتَّصِفُ بِالعِلْم يَتَّصِفُ بِحُصُولِ الصُورَةِ فِي عَقْلِهِ . اهـ
قوله : (المُنْطَبَعُ فِي العَقْلِ) أي: بأن ترسم صورته في العقل، فمعنى «تصور الإنسان»: ارتسام صورة منه في العقل، بها يمتاز الإنسان عن غيره، كما تثبت صورة الشَّيء في المرآة؛ إلَّا أنَّ المرآة تثبت فيها مثل المحسوسات، والنفس تنطبع فيها مثل المعقولات.
قوله : (وَأَجَابَ السَّعْدُ) أي: في شرح الشَّمسيّة».
The image of a thing: its exact likeness, imprinted in the intellect as the images of visible things are imprinted in a mirror. The definition of knowledge by what passed was objected to: that 'obtainment' (hushul) is a description of the image, while knowledge is a description of the knower, so it is not to be interpreted by it. And al-Sa'd answered: that the definer of knowledge is the obtainment of the image in the intellect, not mere obtainment, and the knower, just as he is described by knowledge, is described by the obtainment of the image in his intellect. End. His statement: 'imprinted in the intellect' meaning that its image is drawn in the intellect, so the meaning of 'conceiving of man' is the imprinting of an image of him in the intellect by which man is distinguished from other things, just as the image of a thing is established in a mirror, except that the mirror establishes similar things to sensibles, and the soul imprints in it similar things to intelligibles. His statement: 'and al-Sa'd answered' meaning in his commentary on al-Shamsiyya.
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This passage defines the technical term 'image' (surah) as the exact mental likeness of a thing, imprinted in the intellect like visible images in a mirror. It then addresses an objection to the earlier definition of knowledge as 'the obtainment of the image in the intellect'. The objection is that 'obtainment' is a property of the image, not of the knower, so it cannot define knowledge, which is a property of the knower. Al-Sa'd al-Taftazani responds: the definer is the entire phrase 'obtainment of the image in the intellect', not mere 'obtainment'. So the knower is indeed described by having the image present in his intellect, just as he is described by knowledge. The commentary explains the imprinting analogy: in intellectual conception (tasawwur), the form of the object is drawn in the mind, distinguishing it from others, like a mirror reflecting images—but mirrors reflect only physical forms, while the soul imprints intelligible forms.

Key Terms
الصورة
الحصول
العلم

Checkpoint
According to al-Sa'd, why does the objection fail?
A) Because the definer is the whole phrase 'obtainment of the image in the intellect', not just 'obtainment'.
B) Because knowledge is not a property of the knower.
C) Because the image is identical to the intellect.
D) Because the objection confuses sensation with conception.

Key Takeaway
The definition of knowledge as obtainment of the image in the intellect survives the objection because it describes the knower's state of having the image present, not a mere property of the image.

Critique of al-Baqillani's Definition and Circularity Resolution
Critique of al-Baqillani's definition and circularity resolution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the circularity in al-Baqillani's definition of knowledge and explain the resolution using the distinction between essence and attributive description.

Core Idea
The circularity in 'knowledge of the known' dissolves when 'the known' means the essence, not the known qua known.

Al-Baqillani defined knowledge as 'knowledge of the known as it is'. This definition appears circular. The commentator resolves it by clarifying that 'the known' refers to the essence of the known thing, not to its being known. Understanding this resolution refines your ability to spot and fix circular definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and definiens
Find the definition given by al-Baqillani: knowledge is 'knowledge of the known as it is'. Note the apparent circularity.
2
Recognize the two objections
The passage mentions a circularity (dawr) and a redundancy in 'as it is' because knowledge is always true. Understand why 'as it is' is unnecessary.
3
Analyze the circularity objection in detail
The term 'al-ma'lum' is a passive participle derived from 'ilm. Knowing the derivative depends on knowing the root, leading to a vicious circle.
The passage explicitly explains the derivation.
4
Study the resolution
The commentator resolves by saying 'al-ma'lum' refers to the essence (dhat) of the thing, not to the thing under the attribute of being known.
The description (wasf) can be taken as the essence or as the meaning.
5
Test the resolution
If 'al-ma'lum' means the essence, then no circularity because the essence is prior to any knowledge. Check if this resolves the problem.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعرفه ابن عرفة والسيد بـ: «أنه الإدراك»، وهو مساو للأول. ومن عرف العلم ههنا بـ: «أنه معرفة المعلوم على ما هو به»،
قوله : (وهو مساو للأول) أي: لحصول صورة الشيء في العقل، وفيه تسامح؛ لأن الإدراك ليس هو الحصول، بل هو الوصول؛ يقال: أدركت الشيء وصلت إليه، والحصول لازم.
قوله : (معرفة المعلوم) هذا التعريف للقاضي أبي بكر الباقلاني. وأورد عليه: أن فيه دوراً، وأن قوله: «على ما هو به» قيد زائد لا حاجة إليه؛ إذ المعرفة لا تكون إلا كذلك؛ لأن إدراك الشيء على غير ما هو به جهالة، لا معرفة.

أنواع العلم الحادث
1
كما لبعض الشراح، فقد خرج عن مصطلح القوم إلى مصطلح الأصول، ولزمه فساد العكس بخروج: الظن والاعتقاد الفاسد، مع أنهما من أفراد المعرف هنا. وأكثر منه فساداً تعريف من عرفه بـ: «حكم الذهن الجازم المطابق لموجب»؛ لزيادته على ما قبله بخروج: الاعتقاد الصحيح وجميع التصورات؛ ذكره بعض المحققين، وهو واضح.

والدور الذي أوردوه على تعريفه بـ: «أنه معرفة المعلوم»، الحق في الجواب عنه: أن المراد بـ «المعلوم: ذاته لا بقيد وصفه العنواني الذي هو العلم.

قوله : (كما لبعض الشراح) هو سيدي سعيد، فإنه قال: والمختار قول القاضي: أنه معرفة المعلوم.
قوله : (ولزمه فساد العكس) قال شيخنا اليازغي: هذا هو وجه خروجه عن مصطلح القوم لا شيء زائد عليه فصواب العبارة إذاً: فقد خرج عن مصطلح القوم إلى مصطلح الأصوليين؛ لأن العلم عند المناطقة شامل للظن وللاعتقاد الفاسد، وهما خارجان عن هذا الحد، وعند الأصوليين خاص باليقين.

قوله : (وأكثر منه فساداً تعريف من عرفه بـ: حكم الذهن... إلخ) فقوله: حكم الذهن مخرج لجميع التصورات؛ إذ لا حكم فيها، و: «الجازم» مخرج للظن، وقد كان خرج عن التعريف قبله و: «المطابق» مخرج للفاسد وقد خرج أيضاً عن التعريف قبله، و: «الموجب مخرج للاعتقاد الصحيح، فقد اتضح زيادة فساد هذا على ما قبله وممن عرف العلم هنا بهذا التعريف الشيخ عبد اللطيف أيضاً.

قوله : (والدور الذي أوردوه) بيانه: أن «المعلوم» اسم مفعول مشتق من العلم الذي هو المصدر، ومعرفة المشتق متوقفة على معرفة المشتق منه، فآل الأمر إلى أن العلم توقف على معرفة المعلوم، ومعرفة المعلوم توقفت على العلم، وهذا عين الدور.

قوله : (أن المراد بـ «المعلوم: ذاته ... إلخ) اعلم: أن الوصف يراد منه تارة الذات والمعنى.
And Ibn Arafa and al-Sayyid defined it as: 'It is perception (idrak)', and it is equal to the first. And whoever defined knowledge here as: 'It is knowledge (ma'rifa) of the known thing as it is'. His statement: 'and it is equal to the first' meaning to the obtainment of the image of the thing in the intellect, and there is leniency in it because perception is not obtainment, but rather arrival; it is said: 'I perceived the thing' meaning I arrived at it, and obtainment is a concomitant. His statement: 'knowledge of the known thing' this definition is for al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani. And it was objected against him: that it contains circularity, and that his statement 'as it is' is an extra condition with no need for it, because knowledge is only thus; since perception of a thing other than as it is ignorance, not knowledge.

Types of acquired knowledge
1
As for some commentators, it departed from the terminology of the people [logicians] to the terminology of usul [al-fiqh], and it entailed corruption of the converse by excluding doubt (zann) and false belief, while they are individuals of the definiendum here. And more corrupt than it is the definition of whoever defined it as: 'The decisive judgment of the mind that corresponds to a cause'; because it adds to what preceded it by excluding correct belief and all concepts (tasawwurat). Some verifiers mentioned it, and it is clear.

And the circularity which they objected against his definition 'that it is knowledge of the known thing', the truth in the answer to it: that what is meant by 'the known thing' (al-ma'lum) is its essence, not by the restriction of its attributive description which is knowledge.

His statement: 'as for some commentators' he is Sayyidi Sa'id, for he said: the chosen is the statement of the Qadi: that it is knowledge of the known. His statement: 'and it entailed corruption of the converse' our teacher al-Yazghi said: this is the aspect of its departure from the terminology of the people, nothing additional; so the correct wording then is: it departed from the terminology of the people to the terminology of the usuliyyun; because knowledge in the terminology of the logicians includes doubt and false belief, and they are excluded from this definition, while with the usuliyyun it is specific to certainty.

His statement: 'and more corrupt than it is the definition of whoever defined it as: 'the judgment of the mind...' etc.' For his statement: 'judgment of the mind' excludes all concepts, for there is no judgment in them; and 'decisive' excludes doubt, and it was already excluded from the preceding definition; and 'corresponding' excludes the false, and it was also excluded from the preceding definition; and 'to a cause' excludes correct belief. Thus the increased corruption of this over what preceded it is clear. And among those who defined knowledge here with this definition is Sheikh Abd al-Latif as well.

His statement: 'and the circularity which they objected' its explanation: that 'al-ma'lum' (the known) is a passive participle derived from 'ilm (knowledge) which is the infinitive, and knowledge of the derivative depends on knowledge of the derivative root, so the matter comes to that knowledge depends on knowledge of the known, and knowledge of the known depends on knowledge, and this is pure circularity.

His statement: 'that what is meant by 'the known' is its essence... etc.' Know that the description (al-wasf) sometimes refers to the essence and meaning.
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This passage critiques three alternative definitions of knowledge. First, the definition by Ibn Arafa and al-Sayyid al-Sharif that knowledge is 'perception' (idrak). The commentator notes this is lenient because idrak literally means 'arrival', not 'obtainment', though obtainment is a necessary concomitant. Second, al-Baqillani's definition: 'knowledge of the known as it is'. Two objections are raised: (1) circularity because 'the known' derives from 'knowledge', and (2) the phrase 'as it is' is redundant because knowledge is always true. Third, a definition attributed to some commentator: 'the decisive judgment of the mind corresponding to a cause'. This is even more problematic because it excludes concepts (tasawwurat) entirely, and also excludes doubt, false belief, and correct belief, leaving only certainty—but logicians include doubt and false belief under 'ilm. The passage then resolves the circularity objection: 'the known' in Baqillani's definition refers to the essence of the thing known, not to it as known (i.e., not under the attribute of being known). The commentary quotes al-Yazghi clarifying that Baqillani's definition belongs to usul al-fiqh, where 'ilm means certainty, not to logic. The final gloss explains that a description (wasf) can be taken as the essence or as the meaning; here it means the essence, breaking the circularity.

Key Terms
الإدراك
المعرفة
الدور
حكم الذهن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main logical problem with al-Baqillani's definition?
A) It defines knowledge as perception, which is inaccurate.
B) It is circular because 'the known' derives from 'knowledge'.
C) It excludes correct belief.
D) It uses the term 'ma'rifa' which is ambiguous.

Key Takeaway
Circularity in definitions can be resolved by reinterpreting the definiens to refer to essence rather than a derived attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the circularity in the definition: 'Knowledge is the belief that is true because it is knowledge.' Then resolve it using the technique learned in this lesson.
?
Find a definition in a usul al-fiqh text (e.g., 'ijma'' is the agreement of the mujtahidun of the ummah after the death of the Prophet) and check if it contains any circularity.

Critique of the 'Judgment of the Mind' Definition
Critique of the definition 'decisive judgment corresponding to a cause' and terminology shift.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the definition of knowledge as 'decisive judgment of the mind corresponding to a cause' is considered more corrupt than al-Baqillani's definition, and identify which terms it excludes.

Core Idea
A definition that excludes concepts (tasawwurat) and correct belief fails to match the scope of 'ilm in logic and usul al-fiqh.

Some later scholars defined knowledge as 'the decisive judgment of the mind corresponding to a cause'. This lesson examines why this definition is deemed more corrupt than al-Baqillani's. It excludes all concepts (tasawwurat) because concepts are not judgments, and excludes correct belief because it has no 'cause' in the technical sense. The passage also discusses the terminology shift from logic to usul al-fiqh regarding the scope of 'ilm.

Guided Path
1
Identify the components of the definition
The definition is 'hukm al-dhihn al-jazim al-mutabiq li-mujib'. Break it down: judgment, decisive (jazim), corresponding (mutabiq), to a cause (mujib).
2
Analyze what each component excludes
'Judgment' excludes all tasawwurat (concepts). 'Decisive' excludes doubt (zann). 'Corresponding' excludes false belief (i'tiqad fasid). 'To a cause' excludes correct belief (i'tiqad sahih) because it requires a specific cause.
The passage explicitly lists these exclusions.
3
Understand why this is 'more corrupt'
Logicians include doubt and false belief under 'ilm. The definition excludes them, plus it excludes all concepts and even correct belief, leaving only certainty. This is too narrow.
The commentator says it is 'aktharu minhu fasadan' (more corrupt).
4
Recognize the terminology shift
Al-Yazghi clarifies that this definition belongs to usul al-fiqh, where 'ilm is specific to certainty. In logic, 'ilm includes doubt and false belief. The definition fails to cover the logical scope.
The passage quotes al-Yazghi: 'it departed from the terminology of the logicians to the terminology of the usuliyyun'.
5
Evaluate the definition's adequacy
Determine if the definition could work in usul al-fiqh if it only aims to define certainty. But the text notes that even in usul, excluding correct belief is problematic.

His statement: 'and the circularity which they objected' its explanation: that 'al-ma'lum' (the known) is a passive participle derived from 'ilm (knowledge) which is the infinitive, and knowledge of the derivative depends on knowledge of the derivative root, so the matter comes to that knowledge depends on knowledge of the known, and knowledge of the known depends on knowledge, and this is pure circularity.

His statement: 'that what is meant by 'the known' is its essence... etc.' Know that the description (al-wasf) sometimes refers to the essence and meaning.
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This passage critiques three alternative definitions of knowledge. First, the definition by Ibn Arafa and al-Sayyid al-Sharif that knowledge is 'perception' (idrak). The commentator notes this is lenient because idrak literally means 'arrival', not 'obtainment', though obtainment is a necessary concomitant. Second, al-Baqillani's definition: 'knowledge of the known as it is'. Two objections are raised: (1) circularity because 'the known' derives from 'knowledge', and (2) the phrase 'as it is' is redundant because knowledge is always true. Third, a definition attributed to some commentator: 'the decisive judgment of the mind corresponding to a cause'. This is even more problematic because it excludes concepts (tasawwurat) entirely, and also excludes doubt, false belief, and correct belief, leaving only certainty—but logicians include doubt and false belief under 'ilm. The passage then resolves the circularity objection: 'the known' in Baqillani's definition refers to the essence of the thing known, not to it as known (i.e., not under the attribute of being known). The commentary quotes al-Yazghi clarifying that Baqillani's definition belongs to usul al-fiqh, where 'ilm means certainty, not to logic. The final gloss explains that a description (wasf) can be taken as the essence or as the meaning; here it means the essence, breaking the circularity.

Key Terms
حكم الذهن
التصورات
الظن
الاعتقاد الفاسد
اليقين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main reason the definition 'hukm al-dhihn al-jazim al-mutabiq li-mujib' is considered more corrupt than the preceding definition?
A) It excludes all concepts (tasawwurat).
B) It includes doubt as knowledge.
C) It does not mention certainty.
D) It is circular.

Key Takeaway
A definition that adds unnecessary conditions may become corrupt by excluding valid instances of the definiendum.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Take the definition: 'Knowledge is the true belief that is justified by evidence.' Identify which components of the logical 'ilm (tasawwur, tasdiq, zann, yaqin) are excluded and explain why this definition would be considered corrupt in logic.

The Two Instruments: Qawl Sharh and Hujja
Author defines instruments for conceptualization and assent with grammatical note.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the explanatory statement (qawl sharh) for conceptualization and the argument (hujja) for assent, and identify which types of definitions or descriptions constitute a direct qawl sharh.

Core Idea
Conceptualization comes through definition or description; assent through argument.

In this lesson, we examine the foundational division in logic: the means by which we acquire new concepts (tasawwur) and new judgments (tasdiq). The author identifies two instruments: the explanatory statement (qawl sharh) and the argument (hujja). We will see what exactly counts as a qawl sharh and how it differs from other cognitive tools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two instruments
Find the two poetic lines: the first mentions 'that by which to conceptualization one arrives' is called 'explanatory statement'; the second mentions 'that by which to assent one arrives' is called 'argument'.
The lines are marked (22) and (23) in the text.
2
Understand the directness condition
The commentary specifies that the qawl sharh leads 'directly' (mubasharatan) to conceptualization. This includes complete and incomplete definitions (hadd) and descriptions (rasm).
Directness excludes mediated tools like universals considered as such.
3
Distinguish from mediated means
The author excludes the universal considered as universal, genus, differentia, and property because they require composition and relation to the defined thing. They are not direct.
For example, 'animal' as genus does not directly give conceptualization of 'human' without further specification.
4
Confirm the role of hujja
The hujja (argument) is the instrument for assent. It typically takes the form of a syllogism (qiyas) and is known among the rational people.
The passage does not elaborate on hujja here, but it is the counterpart to qawl sharh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (۲۲) وَمَا بِهِ إِلَى تَصَوُّرٍ وُصِلْ يُدْعَى بِـ: «قَوْلِ شَارِحٍ فَلْتَبْتَهِلْ (۲۳) وَمَا لِتَصْدِيقِ بِهِ تُوُصِّلَا بِـ: «حُجَّةٍ يُعْرَفُ عِنْدَ العُقَلَا يَعْنِي : أَنَّ المَعْلُومَ الَّذِي يُلاحَظُ لِلتَّوَصُّلِ بِهِ إِلَى مَا جُهِلَ [ب/ ٧] مِنَ التَّصَوُّرِ مُبَاشَرَةٌ قوله : (فِي مَوَادِّ الأَقِيسَةِ) أي : عند قول النَّاظم : مِنْ: أَوَّلِيَّاتٍ ... إلخ». قوله : (وَمَا بِهِ إِلَى تَصَوُّرٍ ... (البيتين لا يقال : وقع في كلام الناظم تقديم النائب عن الفاعل على الفعل، وهو ممنوع في الصناعة النَّحويَّة، وذلك في البيت الأول والثاني. لأنَّا نقول : النَّائب عن الفاعل لم يتقدَّم فيهما ، بل هو ضمير المصدر على ما قيل في قوله تعالى: ﴿وَحِيلَ بينَهُمْ [السبا : ٥٤] ، وقول الشاعر : [من الطويل] فَيَا لَكَ مِنْ ذِي حَاجَةٍ حِيْلَ دُونَهَا ووقع مثل هذا لابن مالك في قوله : وَمَــــا بِـــهِ إِلَــــى عَجُبٍ وُصِلْ وأجيب عنه بمثل ما ذكرنا قوله : (مُبَاشَرَةٌ) أي : مِن حدّ تام أو ناقص، أو رسم تام، أو ناقص . واحترز به عن : الموصل بواسطة كالكلِّي مِن حيث إنَّه كلّيّ، أو مِن حيث إنَّه جنس أو فصل أو خاصَّةٌ ؛ لتوقف ذلك على التّركيب والنسبة إلى المعرف.
His saying: (22) And that by which to conceptualization one arrives is called 'explanatory statement' so let him strive. (23) And that by which to assent one arrives is called 'argument' known among the rational. He means: that the known which is considered for arriving through it to what is unknown from conceptualization directly. His saying: (in the materials of syllogisms) i.e., at the saying of the author: 'From first principles... etc.' His saying: (And that by which to conceptualization... [two lines]) It is not said: In the speech of the author occurred the precedence of the substitute for the agent over the verb, and that is prohibited in grammatical craft, and that is in the first and second lines. Because we say: The substitute for the agent did not precede in them, rather it is the pronoun of the infinitive as was said in the saying of God the Exalted: 'And a barrier was placed between them' [Saba 54], and the saying of the poet: 'Alas for you, O one in need, a barrier was placed before her.' And similar occurred for Ibn Malik in his saying: 'And that by which to astonishment one arrives.' And it was answered with the like of what we mentioned. His saying: (directly) i.e., from a complete definition or incomplete, or a complete description or incomplete. And he guards by it against the conveyer by mediation like the universal insofar as it is universal, or insofar as it is genus or differentia or property; because that depends on composition and relation to the thing defined.
Source pages p. 430

Commentary
This passage explains the difference between conceptualization (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq) and the means to achieve them. The author quotes the matn lines: 'And that by which one arrives at conceptualization is called an explanatory statement (qawl sharh), and that by which one arrives at assent is called an argument (hujja) known among the rational.' He then glosses: the known thing considered for arriving at the unknown conceptualization is directly a definition (hadd) or description (rasm), complete or incomplete. He also notes a grammatical issue: the substitute for the agent (na'ib al-fa'il) appears before the verb in the poetic lines, which is normally prohibited. He defends it by saying it is actually a pronoun of the infinitive, citing Quranic and poetic examples. He then clarifies that 'directly' excludes universals considered as universals, because they require composition and relation to the defined thing.

The instruments of logic are two: explanatory statement for conceptualization and argument for assent, each with specific conditions.

The Explanatory Statement: Defining the Essential Tool for Tasawwur
Commentary defines the term 'explanatory statement' for tasawwur

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the function of al-qawl al-sharih in tasawwur and distinguish it from other statements.

Core Idea
The explanatory statement reveals the essence and separates it from its genus.

In classical logic, not every statement is used for the same purpose. Some statements clarify concepts, while others produce conviction. This lesson focuses on the first type: the explanatory statement (al-qawl al-sharih), which is the tool for achieving conceptual knowledge (tasawwur). The commentary explains why it is called 'explanatory' and what function it serves.

Guided Path
1
Identify the technical term
Locate the phrase 'القول الشارح' in the passage. The commentator explicitly says this is a technical term (اصطلاح).
This distinguishes it from everyday speech.
2
Analyze the function
The commentator says it 'explains the essence and distinguishes it from what shares its genus'. This is the core function for tasawwur.
3
Distinguish from the proof
Notice that this term is contrasted with 'hujja', which is for tasdiq. The explanatory statement is not for producing assent but for clarifying concepts.
This helps keep the branches of logic separate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يُسَمَّى : قَوْلاً شَارِحاً»، كَمَا يُسَمَّى : (۱) مُعَرِّفاً» - بِالكَسْرِ ، وَالَّذِي يُلَاحَظُ لِلتَّوَصُّلِ بِهِ إِلَى مَا جُهِلَ مِنَ التَّصْدِيقِ مُبَاشَرَةٌ يُسَمَّى : «حُجَّةً) وَ: «قياساً» قوله : (يُسَمَّى قَوْلاً شَارِحاً يعني في الاصطلاح؛ لأنَّه يشرح الماهية ويميزها عما يشاركها في الجنسية. قوله : (مُبَاشَرَةٌ) أي : من برهان أو جدل أو خطابة، أو شعر، أو سفسطة. احترز به عن القضيَّة من حيث إنَّها قضيَّةٌ، ونحو ذلك . قوله : (يُسَمَّى : «حُجَّةٌ)) أي : لأنَّ مَن تمسَّك به يحج خصمه؛ أي: يغلبه .
It is called 'explanatory statement', and it is also called 'definer' with a kasra. That which is considered for arriving at what is unknown of assent directly is called 'proof' and 'syllogism'. He said: (It is called 'explanatory statement') meaning in terminology, because it explains the essence and distinguishes it from what shares with it in genus. He said: (Directly) meaning from demonstration, dialectic, rhetoric, poetry, or sophistry. He thereby excludes the proposition as such, etc. He said: (It is called 'proof') because whoever adheres to it refutes his opponent, i.e., overcomes him.
Source pages p. 432

Commentary
This passage defines two key logical terms. The 'explanatory statement' (al-qawl al-sharih) is a term that explains the essence (mahiyya) and distinguishes it from what shares its genus; it is used to arrive at conceptual knowledge (tasawwur). The commentary specifies that this is a technical term, not to be confused with ordinary statements. The 'proof' (al-hujja) is what directly leads to assent (tasdiq); it includes various types of argument: demonstration, dialectic, rhetoric, poetry, or sophistry. The phrase 'directly' (mubasharatan) distinguishes the argument itself from the proposition that merely states a claim. Finally, the term 'hujja' is explained etymologically: it is so called because it enables one to overcome an opponent in debate.

Key Terms

حجة

Key Takeaway
The explanatory statement is the logical tool for clarifying concepts by revealing the essence and distinguishing it from other essences.

The Proof: The Direct Path to Tasdiq
Commentary defines the term 'proof' for tasdiq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-hujja and list its types and distinguish it from mere propositions.

Core Idea
A proof is not just any argument—it must directly produce assent.

After defining the tool for conceptual knowledge, the passage turns to the tool for assent: the proof (al-hujja). This lesson examines what constitutes a proof in logic, the types it includes, and why it is called 'hujja'. Understanding this is essential for evaluating arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find 'حجة' in the passage. The commentator provides a definition: it is what is considered for arriving at assent directly.
2
Understand the condition of directness
The phrase 'مباشرة' (directly) is crucial. The commentator explains it means from one of five types: demonstration, dialectic, rhetoric, poetry, or sophistry. This excludes mere propositions.
A proposition alone, not structured as an argument, is not a hujja.
3
Explore the etymology
The commentator gives the reason for the name: because it enables one to overcome (يحج) an opponent. This highlights the persuasive function.
This etymological insight connects logic to debate (jadal).

The Definition of Dalalah and the First Objection
Commentary defines 'indication' and presents the first objection to its definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the technical definition of dalalah and explain the first objection regarding the confusion between the attribute of the sign and the mental act.

Core Idea
Dalalah is defined as understanding the signified from the sign, but this definition is challenged because it conflates a property of the word with a mental act.

Before studying definitions and proofs, the logician must understand indication (dalalah) because both are expressed in terms. This passage gives the linguistic and technical definitions of dalalah and immediately presents a critical objection that will refine your understanding of what indication truly is.

Guided Path
1
Locate the need for studying dalalah
The passage begins by stating that the logician's concern is confined to definitions and proofs with their principles. Since these involve terms that indicate meanings, we must first understand indication and its divisions.
This frames dalalah as a prerequisite for the rest of logic.
2
Identify the linguistic and technical definitions
Linguistically, dalalah means 'guidance'. Technically, according to the ancients, it is 'the understanding of one thing from another' (fahm amr min amr). The example given is understanding the meaning of 'adult male human' from the word 'rajul'.
Notice that the definition focuses on the act of understanding, not the sign itself.
3
Break down the example
The 'first thing' in the definition (the 'amr awal') is the indicated (madlul), i.e., the meaning. The 'second thing' is the indicator (dal), i.e., the word. The relation between them is dalalah.
This shows that dalalah is a relation, but the definition defines it as an act.
4
Understand the first objection
The objection states that indication (dalalah) is a property of the word (e.g., the word's ability to signify), while understanding (fahm) is a property of the person who understands. Therefore defining dalalah as fahm is incorrect because it attributes a mental act to the sign.
This is a logical complaint about category confusion.
5
Note the commentator's addition
The commentator adds 'mthalan' (for example) to hint that indication can be non-verbal, suggesting the definition might need to cover broader types of indication.
This prepares for later discussions of mental and logical indication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَدْ عُلِمَ أَنَّ نَظَرَ المَنْطِقِيِّ مُنْحَصِرُ فِي أَرْبَعَةِ أَشْيَاءَ : التَّعْرِيفَاتِ وَمَبَادِئِهَا، وَالحُجَحِ وَمَبَادِئِهَا وَلَمَّا كَانَتْ لَهَا أَلْفَاظٌ تَدُلُّ عَلَيْهَا ، وَبِهَا يُتصَرِّفُ فِيهَا احْتِيجَ أَوَّلاً إِلَى مَعْرِفَةِ الدِّلَالَةِ وَأَقْسَامِهَا ، وَمَا يُعْتَبَرُ مِنْهَا فِي الفَنِّ، وَمَا لَا يُعْتَبَرُ . وَالدَّلَالَهُ - بِتَثْلِيثِ الدَّالِ - هِيَ لُغَةٌ : مَصْدَرُ دَلَّهُ عَلَى الشَّيْءِ: هَدَاهُ إِلَيْهِ . وَفِي الاصْطِلَاحِ عِنْدَ الأَقْدَمِينَ هِيَ : فَهُمُ أمرٍ مِنْ أَمْرٍ كَـ: فَهْمِ مَعْنَى الذَّكَرِ البَالِغِ الآدَمِيٌّ مِنْ لَفْظِ «الرَّجُلِ » ، وَالأَمْرُ الأَوَّلُ فِي التَّعْرِيفِ هُوَ المَدْلُولُ، وَالثَّانِي هُوَ الدَّالُ. وَقَدِ اعْتُرِضَ (۲) بِأَوْجُهِ : (١) - مِنْهَا : أَنَّ الدَّلَالَةَ وَصْفٌ لِلَّفْظِ مَثَلاً ، وَالفَهْمُ وَصْفٌ لِلْشَّخْصِ لَا لِلَّفْظِ ، فَلَا يَصِحُ تَفْسِيرُهَا بِهِ. قوله : (مَثَلاً) زاد لفظة: «مَثَلاً»؛ لِمَا سيأتي مِن أنَّ الدَّلالة تكون غير لفظيَّةٍ .
It is known that the consideration of the logician is confined to four things: definitions and their principles, and proofs and their principles. When they have terms that indicate them, and by which one disposes of them, it is necessary first to know indication (dalalah) and its divisions, and what is considered of it in the art and what is not considered. Al-dalalah – with three possible vowelings of the dal – is linguistically the verbal noun of 'he indicated him to something': he guided him to it. In technical terminology according to the ancients, it is: the understanding of one thing from another, e.g., understanding the meaning of an adult male human from the word 'rajul'. The first thing in the definition is the indicated (madlul), and the second is the indicator (dal). It has been objected with several points: (1) Among them: that indication is a property of the word, for example, while understanding is a property of the person, not of the word; thus it is not correct to interpret it by it. He said: ('For example') – he added the word 'for example' because of what will come later that indication can be non-verbal.
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage establishes the need to study indication (dalalah) before definitions and proofs, since both are expressed in terms. It defines dalalah linguistically as guidance, and technically as the understanding (fahm) of one thing from another, using the classic example of understanding the meaning of 'adult male human' from the word 'rajul'. The definition carefully distinguishes the indicator (dal) from the indicated (madlul). The commentator then introduces the first objection: that dalalah is a property of the word (a descriptive attribute of the signifier), while understanding is a mental act of the person. Therefore defining dalalah as 'understanding' confuses the attribute of the sign with the cognitive act of the interpreter. The commentator notes that the word 'mthalan' (for example) was added to hint that indication can also be non-verbal, which will be discussed later. This prepares the reader for a broader conception of indication beyond linguistic signs.

Key Terms
الدلالة
المدلول
الدال

Key Terms from Source Passage

The definition of dalalah as fahm (understanding) is the starting point, but it is immediately challenged because it confuses a property of the sign with a mental act, reminding us that definitions must capture the essence of the thing defined.

Classifying Relative Concepts in Logic
Al-Sayyid's classification of relative concepts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of relative concepts (ma'ani nisiyya) and classify any given relational term into its appropriate category.

Core Idea
Relative concepts differ in how they relate to their relata—some inhere in both, some in one, some in the aggregate.

In this lesson, we examine al-Sayyid's classification of relative concepts, which he uses to argue why 'fahm' (understanding) cannot be a real quality of the utterance. Understanding these three types is essential for correctly attributing relational properties in logical definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types
Read the passage where al-Sayyid categorises relative concepts. Notice the three distinct patterns: (1) subsisting in both relata, (2) subsisting in one and relating to the other, (3) subsisting in the aggregate.
The Arabic terms: ukhuwwa, ubuwwa, tashabuh are examples.
2
Understand the test for each type
For each type, ask: 'Does the attribute describe each relatum individually, only one, or only the pair together?' Brotherhood describes both Zayd and 'Amr; paternity describes only the father; similarity describes the pair together.
This test determines which category a relative concept belongs to.
3
Apply to 'fahm' and 'dalala'
Al-Sayyid states that 'fahm' is like paternity: it subsists in the hearer but relates to the word and meaning. Therefore, 'fahm' can only be a real quality of the hearer, not of the word. The word's relation is merely 'being understood from'.
Derivation (ishtiqaq) confirms this: 'mafhum minhu' is derived from 'fahm', so the word is not itself understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَتَعَقَّبَ السَّيِّدُ هَذَا الجَوَابَ بِمَا حَاصِلُهُ: أَنَّ الفَهْمَ مِنْ حَيْثُ حَقِيقَتُهُ إِنَّمَا هُوَ صِفَةٌ لِلشَّخْصِ قَائِمَةٌ بِهِ، وَلَا يَصِحُ أَنْ يَكُونَ صِفَةٌ لِلَّفْظِ وَلَا لِلْمَعْنَى . وَحَاصِلُ مَا ذَكَرَهُ أَنَّ كَوْنَ الفَهْمِ نِسْبَةً بَيْنَ السَّامِعِ وَالمَعْنَى، وَاللَّفْظِ لَا يَسْتَلْزِمُ أَنْ يَكُونَ صِفَةً حَقِيقِيَّةً لِكُلٍّ مِنْهَا، وَذَلِكَ لِأَنَّ مِنَ المَعَانِي النِّسِيَّةِ : - مَا هُوَ قَائِمٌ بِكُلٍّ مِنَ المُنْتَسِبَيْنِ، فَيُوصَفُ بِهَا كُلٌّ مِنْهُمَا كَـ: الأُخُوَّةِ الَّتِي بَيْنَ زَيْدٍ وَعَمْرٍو، وَالشَّرِكَةِ الَّتِي بَيْنَهُمَا . - وَمِنْهَا مَا هُوَ قَائِمٌ بِأَحَدِهِمَا مُتَعَلِّقٌ بِالآخَرِ، فَيُوصَفُ بِهَا مَنْ قَامَتْ بِهِ، لَا مَنْ تَعَلَّقَتْ بِهِ كَـ: الأُبُوَّةِ، فَإِنَّهَا قَائِمَةٌ بِالأَبِ مُتَعَلِّقَةٌ بِالابْنِ، فَلَا يَصِحُ وَصْفُ الابْنِ بِهَا، وَمِنْ هَذَا القَبِيلِ الفَهْمُ وَالدَّلَالَةُ كَمَا يَشْهَدُ بِهِ الاشْتِقَاقُ، فَلَا يَصِحُ وَصْفُ اللَّفْظِ بِالفَهْمِ حَقِيقَةً، وَإِنَّمَا يَصِحُ وَصْفُهُ بِكَوْنِهِ مَفْهُومًا مِنْهُ . - وَمِنْهَا مَا هُوَ قَائِمٌ بِمَجْمُوعِ المُنْتَسِبَيْنِ فَلَا يُوصَفُ بِهَا أَحَدُهُمَا، بَلْ مَجْمُوعُهُمَا كَ: التَّشَابُهِ؛ فَيُقَالُ: زَيْدٌ وَعَمْرٌو مُتَشَابِهَانِ . نَعَمْ؛ يُفْهَمُ مِنْ تَعَلُّقِهِ بِاللَّفْظِ صِفَةٌ لَهُ هِيَ كَوْنُهُ مَفْهُومًا مِنْهُ المَعْنَى . وَأَجَابَ: بِأَنَّ القَوْمَ وَإِنْ عَبّرُوا عَنِ الدَّلَالَةِ بِـ «الفَهْمِ»، لَكِنْ تَسَامَحُوا فِي التَّعْبِيرِ . وَمُرَادُهُمْ لَازِمُ ذَلِكَ، وَهُوَ كَوْنُ اللَّفْظِ مَفْهُومًا مِنْهُ المَعْنَى، وَاتَّكَلُوا عَلَى ظُهُورِ أَنَّ الدَّلَالَةَ صِفَةٌ لِلَّفْظِ .
And al-Sayyid refuted this answer with the gist: that understanding, in its true nature, is only a quality of the person, subsisting in him, and it is not valid for it to be a quality of the utterance nor of the meaning. And the gist of what he mentioned: that understanding being a relation between the hearer and the meaning, and the utterance, does not entail that it is a real quality of each of them. That is because among relative concepts: (1) What subsists in each of the two relata, so each is described by it, like brotherhood between Zayd and 'Amr, and partnership between them. (2) What subsists in one of them and is related to the other, so it describes the one in whom it subsists, not the one to which it is related, like paternity, for it subsists in the father and is related to the son, so describing the son by it is not valid. Of this kind are understanding and indication, as derivation testifies, so describing the word by understanding is not valid in reality; it is only valid to describe it as being understood from it. (3) What subsists in the aggregate of the two relata, so neither is described by it, but rather their aggregate, like similarity; one says 'Zayd and 'Amr are similar.' Yes; from its relation to the utterance is understood a quality of it, which is its being that from which the meaning is understood. And he answered: that the scholars, even though they expressed indication by 'understanding', they were lenient in expression. Their intended meaning is the necessary consequence of that, namely that the word is that from which the meaning is understood, and they relied on the obviousness that indication is a quality of the utterance.
Source pages p. 434

Commentary
This passage presents al-Sayyid al-Sharif al-Jurjani's refutation of the earlier answer and the final defense. Al-Sayyid argues that understanding, in its true nature, is a quality that subsists in the person (the hearer) only; it cannot be a real quality of the word or the meaning. Therefore the earlier attempt to attribute 'fahm' to the word as 'mafhum minhu' (understood from) is merely metaphorical. The author then classifies relative concepts (ma'ani nisiyya) into three types: (1) those subsisting in both relata, like brotherhood (ukhuwwa) where Zayd and 'Amr are both brothers; (2) those subsisting in one relatum and related to the other, like paternity (ubuwwa) which subsists in the father and relates to the son, so only the father is described as a father; (3) those subsisting in the aggregate of both relata, like similarity (tashabuh), where neither is individually similar but the pair together are similar. Understanding and indication belong to the second type: understanding subsists in the hearer but relates to the word and meaning, so the word is not truly 'understanding' but can be described as 'that from which understanding is derived'. The final answer concedes that the scholars used 'fahm' loosely (tasamuh) for 'dalala'; their real intent was the necessary consequence: the word's property of being understood from it (mafhumiyya), which is equivalent to indication. So the definition is acceptable as a lenient expression.

Key Takeaway
Relative concepts are classified by how they inhere in their relata; understanding subsists in the hearer and only relates to the word, so the word cannot be truly described as 'understanding'.

Refining the Definition of 'Fahm' via Leniency in Expression
The objection and final defense using tasamuh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct al-Sayyid's objection to the definition of 'fahm' and explain the author's defense using the principle of 'tasamuh' (leniency).

Core Idea
Technical definitions may use terms loosely, relying on necessary consequences rather than literal meanings, as in calling indication 'understanding'.

Al-Sayyid's objection forces a refinement: if 'fahm' is a quality of the hearer, how can it define indication (dalala) which is a quality of the word? The author's answer introduces a key principle in logical definitions: tasamuh, or leniency in expression, where the intended meaning is the necessary consequence (lazim) of the term.

Guided Path
1
State al-Sayyid's objection
Al-Sayyid argues that the earlier definition incorrectly attributes 'fahm' to the word. In reality, 'fahm' is a quality subsisting in the hearer. Therefore, defining dalala as 'fahm' is metaphysically inaccurate.
The objection relies on the classification of relative concepts: 'fahm' belongs to the second type.
2
Identify the author's defense
The author concedes that 'fahm' is not literally a quality of the word. However, logicians used 'fahm' loosely to mean its necessary consequence: the word being 'understood from' (mafhumiyya). This is a form of tasamuh.
Tasamuh is justified when the intended meaning is obvious, as 'dalala' is clearly a property of the word.
3
Understand the concept of 'lazim' (necessary consequence)
The logicians meant by 'fahm' not the act of understanding itself, but the word's property of being a vehicle for understanding. This property (mafhumiyya) is a real quality of the word and is equivalent to dalala.
The defense preserves the definition by interpreting it as a shorthand for the real property.
4
Evaluate the acceptability of tasamuh in definitions
Tasamuh is acceptable in logical contexts when the technical usage is well-known and no confusion arises. The author argues that relying on the obviousness of dalala being a word-quality justifies the lenient expression.
This principle appears often in commentaries: technical terms may deviate from literal meanings by common convention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
