The Three Types of Signification: Criteria and Definitions
Author defines the three types of conventional, intellectual, and natural signification for verbal expressions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of signification (dalala) according to the criteria of volition and possibility of detachment, and correctly classify a given example.

Core Idea
Signification is not monolithic: volition and necessity determine whether it is conventional, intellectual, or natural.

Words convey meaning, but the way they do so differs. In Islamic sciences, especially usul al-fiqh and balagha, understanding these types helps analyze how Quranic language functions as evidence. This lesson unpacks the criteria that define conventional (wad'iyya), intellectual ('aqliyya), and natural (tabi'iyya) signification.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two binary criteria: volition and possibility of detachment
The author classifies signification first by whether it is volitional (ikhtiyari) or not. Then, for non-volitional, by whether detachment from the signified is possible (imkan al-takhalluf) or impossible.
These criteria are applied to any signifier-signified relation.
2
Apply the criteria to derive the three types
If volitional → wad'iyya (conventional). If non-volitional and detachment possible → tabi'iyya (natural). If non-volitional and detachment impossible → 'aqliyya (intellectual).
This is a logical division by dichotomy.
3
Review the definitions given in the passage
Wad'iyya is based on choice and stipulation; 'aqliyya is based on rational necessity (e.g., a word necessitates a speaker); tabi'iyya is based on innate nature (e.g., blush indicating shame).
These definitions are from the commentary.
4
Consider the verbal examples provided
For conventional: the word 'lion' for the beast (haqiqa) or a brave man (majaz). For intellectual: any word indicating a speaker (since a word is an accident requiring a substance). For natural: the redness of blush (innate sign of shame).
The passage only gives an abstract definition for natural; the example is inferred from later balagha.
5
Recognize the six-fold division
The passage mentions that signification becomes six divisions when applied to both verbal and non-verbal signifiers. Verbal signification is the focus here.
The missing three are non-verbal equivalents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنواع الدلالة الوضعية: لأن الدلالة إن كانت اختيارية فهي الوضعية، وإلا فإن أمكن تخلفها فهي الطبيعية، وإلا فهي العقلية، فصارت الدلالة ستة أقسام: (1) فدلالة اللفظ الوضعية كدلالة لفظ الأسد على السبع المعروف حقيقة، أو على الرجل الشجاع مجازاً؛ بناء على أن المجاز موضوع بالنوع. ومن ذلك: دلالة ألفاظ القرآن على معانيه الإفرادية، وكذلك التركيبية؛ لأن التحقيق أن دلالة المركب وضعية، لا عقلية. (2) ودلالة اللفظ العقلية كدلالة اللفظ على لافظ يقوم هو به، وهي في جميع الألفاظ؛ لأن اللفظ عرض يستحيل أن يقوم بنفسه. (3) ودلالة اللفظ الطبيعية المستندة إلى الطبع؛ أي: الغريزة التي طبع الخلق عليها.
Types of conventional signification: because signification if it is volitional then it is conventional, otherwise if its being left behind is possible then it is natural, otherwise it is intellectual, so signification becomes six divisions: (1) Conventional signification of a word, like the signification of the word 'lion' for the known beast in reality, or for the brave man metaphorically; based on the view that metaphor is conventionally assigned by type. And among that is the signification of the words of the Qur'an for their individual meanings, and likewise composite meanings; because the established view is that the signification of a composite is conventional, not intellectual. (2) Intellectual signification of a word, like the signification of the word for a speaker who exists through it, and it is in all words because the word is an accident impossible to subsist by itself. (3) Natural signification of a word, which is based on nature, i.e., the innate disposition upon which creation is naturally formed.
Source pages p. 439

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the classification of signification (dalala) into three types based on two criteria: volition (ikhtiyar) and possible detachment (takhalluf). The author is commenting on the matn, explaining that if the signification is volitional, it is wad'iyya (conventional); if not volitional but possible to be detached from the signified, it is tabi'iyya (natural); and if not volitional and impossible to be detached, it is 'aqliyya (intellectual). The author then gives examples for each type with respect to verbal expressions (lafz). For wad'iyya, he gives the example of the word 'asad' denoting the actual beast (haqiqa) or a brave man (majaz), noting that metaphor is also conventional by type (bi al-naw'). He adds that the signification of Quranic words and composites is wad'iyya, not 'aqliyya. For 'aqliyya, he gives the example of the word indicating a speaker who produces it, because a word is an accident that cannot subsist by itself, so its existence necessitates a speaker. For tabi'iyya, he defines it as based on nature, i.e., innate disposition, such as the redness of blush indicating shame, though the specific example is not elaborated here. The classification is intended for verbal signification, with the implication that similar categories apply to non-verbal signification, making six total.

Applying the Classification: Metaphor, Quranic Language, and Intellectual Necessity
Author defines the three types of conventional, intellectual, and natural signification for verbal expressions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify conventional, intellectual, and natural signification in examples from Quranic language and linguistic analysis, and explain why metaphor (majaz) is considered conventional by type (naw').

Core Idea
Even metaphor is a form of conventional signification, and the Quran's composite meanings are conventional, not intellectual.

Building on the criteria from Lesson 1, we now apply them to specific cases: the word 'lion' used literally and metaphorically, the signification of Quranic words and sentences, and the rational necessity linking a word to its speaker. This lesson shows how the classification operates in real Islamic texts.

Guided Path
1
Recall the three types from Lesson 1
Wad'iyya (conventional), 'aqliyya (intellectual), tabi'iyya (natural).
2
Analyze the example of 'lion' (asad)
The word 'lion' conventionally signifies the known beast (haqiqa). When used for a brave man (majaz), it is still conventional, but 'by type' (bi al-naw'). This means that metaphorical usage is part of the language's conventional system, not a separate intellectual or natural signification.
This is a key point: metaphor is not arbitrary but sanctioned by linguistic convention.
3
Examine the claim about Quranic signification
The author says that Quranic words and composites (sentences) have conventional signification. For example, the word 'Allah' conventionally refers to the Creator, and the sentence 'There is no god but Allah' conveys its meaning through convention, not pure reason.
Contrast with intellectual signification: the composite does not by itself necessitate a meaning without convention.
4
Understand intellectual signification in words
Any word (as an accidental sound) intellectually signifies a speaker who produces it. This is a rational necessity: an accident cannot exist without a substance. So hearing a word forces the mind to infer a speaker.
This is not volitional; it is a direct causal relation.
5
Consider natural signification
Natural signification is based on innate disposition (ghariza). For example, a person's facial redness naturally signifies shame. Although the author does not give a verbal example, one could extend this to sounds like crying naturally signifying sadness.
This type is less central in logic but appears in discussions of evidence.

Defense of the Classification: Objection to Natural Signification
Author refutes an objection that natural signification is identical to conventional in requiring divine choice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the equivocation in Ibn Mansur's objection and distinguish the two meanings of 'choice' (ikhtiyar) in conventional vs. natural signification.

Core Idea
The objection collapses because 'choice' means something different in natural signification than in conventional.

In logical classification, precision of terminology is critical. Ibn Mansur's objection attempted to collapse the distinction between natural and conventional signification by arguing both depend on divine choice. The author shows that this argument rests on an equivocation of the term 'choice'. This lesson unpacks that equivocation and demonstrates how the original classification remains sound.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Objection
State Ibn Mansur's claim: both natural and conventional signification require divine 'ja'l' (making), so both involve choice, and thus the classification is invalid.
Ibn Mansur's argument is that the signifier does not necessitate the signified by itself in either case, so both are volitional.
2
Extract the Meaning of 'Choice' in Conventional Signification
According to the author, Yasin defined choice in conventional signification as the wadi's freedom to assign a signifier to any signified or leave it unassigned. This is a voluntary act.
This is the standard meaning in wad'.
3
Extract the Meaning of 'Choice' in Natural Signification (as per Objector)
Ibn Mansur uses 'choice' to mean that the signifier does not necessitate the signified by itself, i.e., the connection is not inherent but depends on God's will.
The author acknowledges this meaning but notes it is different from the conventional sense.
4
Compare the Two Meanings
The author argues that these are distinct: conventional choice involves an agent with options; natural 'choice' is merely the contingency of the connection. The objector conflated them.
The author uses the example of redness following shyness to show human choice is absent in natural signification.
5
Apply to the Classification
Since the meanings differ, the objection fails. The classification remains valid: conventional signification involves the wadi's choice; natural signification does not, even though both depend on God's creative act.
The author also addresses a potential counter-argument about Yasin's statement, clarifying that choice pertains to signification itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (لأن الدلالة إن كانت اختيارية) أشار بما ذكر إلى وجه حصر الدلالتين فيما ذكر، وهو صحيح، واعتراض شيخنا ابن منصور عليه باطل ; ونص كلامه وفيما أشرنا إليه مِن أنَّ الطبيعية لا اختيار فيها نظر؛ إذ لا فرق بينها وبين الوضعيّة في أنَّ الدالَّ لا يقتضي المدلول لذاته، وأنَّه لا يترتب عليه إلَّا بجعل فلولا أنَّ الله تعالى جعل الماء يترتب عليه النبات لم يترتب، وهو سبحان فاعل مختار، ولو شاء لم يجعل الحمرة عقب الخجل . اهـ كلامه، وهو تابع في ذلك للشارح في حاشيته على «المختصر، وزاد الشَّارح : فالحقُ أنَّ العادية اختيارية كالوضعية. وفي اعتراضه تبعاً لمَن ذُكر نظر، وذلك لأنَّ الاختيار في الطبيعية معناه كما فسره به هذا الباحث أنَّ الدَّالَّ لا يقتضي ثبوت المدلول لذاته، وهو مخالف للاختيار في الوضعية؛ إذ معناه كما قال ياسين أنَّ الواضع له أن يضع اللفظ مثلاً لهذا المعنى أو لغيره أو يتركه مهملاً، فاشتبه على هذا الباحث الاختيار في جانب الوضعية بالاختيار الذي يذكره المتكلمون مصححاً للإرادة، ويجعلونه مقابلاً للعلَّة والطبع . وإذا علمت هذا، ظهر أن لا اختيار في الطَّبيعية بالمعنى المراد في الوضعية، فيكون الحصر صحيحاً، وظاهر كلام المحشي حيث قال : لا فرق ... إلخ » أنَّ الاختيار فيهما بمعنى، وليس كذلك، وإن كان الاختيار بالمعنى الذي ذكره في الطبيعيَّة صحيحاً في الوضعية؛ إلَّا أنَّه غير مرادٍ حين إطلاقه في جانب الدَّلالة، وإذا انتفى كونه مراداً عند الإطلاق ثبت المعنى الآخر الذي بيَّنه ياسين، وهو منتف في الطبيعية؛ إذ ليس للإنسان اختيار في جعله الحمرة دالة على الخجل، بل الحمرة تعلوه عند حيائه من غير قصد له فيها، وكذلك قول الإنسان: «أح» مثلاً، فإنَّ الَّذي ألجأه إلى التلفظ بها إنَّما هو الوجع، وقد يتخلَّفُ هذا المدلول كمدلول الحمرة، فظهر من هذا كله بطلان إلخ . لا يقال : كلام الشيخ ياسين إنَّما هو في بيان الاختيار في الوضع لا في الدَّلالة . لأنَّا نقول : معنى قوله : «له أن يضع اللفظ لهذا المعنى له أن يضعه ليكون دالا على هذا المعنى، أو على غيره من المعاني، أو لا يدلُّ على شيءٍ أصلاً، فإنَّ الغرض مِنَ الوضع هو الدَّلالة، فينبغي أن يرجع الاختيار إليها .
His statement: 'because signification if it is volitional...' he pointed to the aspect of confining the two significations to what was mentioned, and it is correct. The objection of our master Ibn Mansur against it is false; the text of his words: 'And in what we pointed to of that natural signification has no choice, there is consideration: for there is no difference between it and conventional signification in that the signifier does not necessitate the signified by itself, and that nothing results from it except by a making. So if God had not made water result in plants, it would not result, and He is a free agent, and if He wished He would not have made redness follow after shyness.' End of his words. And he follows in that the commentator in his hashia on al-Mukhtasar, and the commentator added: 'The truth is that natural signification is volitional like conventional.' And in his objection following those mentioned there is a problem, because the meaning of choice in natural signification, as this researcher interpreted it, is that the signifier does not necessitate the existence of the signified by itself, and that is different from choice in conventional signification; because its meaning as Yasin said is that the putter (wadi') has the option to place the word, for example, for this meaning or another or leave it neglected. So this researcher confused choice in conventional signification with the choice mentioned by the theologians as making volition sound, and they make it opposite of cause and nature. And if you know this, it becomes clear that there is no choice in natural signification in the sense intended in conventional signification, so the confinement is correct. And it appears from the words of the commentator where he said: 'There is no difference...' etc., that choice in both is the same meaning, but it is not so. Although choice in the sense he mentioned in natural signification is valid in conventional, nevertheless that is not intended when it is used in the context of signification. And if it is not intended upon absolute usage, the other meaning explained by Yasin is established, which is absent in natural signification: because man has no choice in making redness signify shyness; rather, redness overcomes him when he is shy without his intention. And similarly the saying of a person 'Ah', for example, what compelled him to utter it is pain, and this signified may be left behind like the signified of redness. So from all this the falsehood appears, etc.' It cannot be said: 'The words of Shaykh Yasin are only about choice in conventional placement, not in signification.' Because we say: His statement, 'He has the option to place the word for this meaning', means he has the option to make it signify this meaning, or another, or not signify anything at all; because the purpose of placement (wad') is signification, so choice should revert to it.
Source pages p. 439

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement by the author addressing an objection raised by Ibn Mansur against the classification of natural signification as non-volitional. Ibn Mansur argued that both natural and conventional signification require a divine 'ja'l' (making), so both involve choice, making the distinction invalid. The author responds by clarifying that the term 'ikhtiyar' (choice) is used differently in the two contexts. In conventional signification, it refers to the wadi's freedom to assign a signifier to any signified or leave it unassigned. In natural signification, 'choice' refers to the fact that the signifier does not necessitate the signified by itself; this is a different meaning. The author credits Yasin (likely al-Hariri) for this distinction. The author then illustrates with the examples of the redness of shyness and the interjection 'Ah' as natural signs that are not subject to human choice. He concludes that the original classification is sound, despite the commentator's earlier claim that natural signification is volitional like conventional. The author also addresses a potential counter-objection about Yasin's discussion being only about the placement, not signification, and argues that placement aims at signification, so choice pertains to signification as well. This passage demonstrates the precision required in logical terminology and the importance of avoiding equivocation.

Key Terms
اختيار (ikhtiyar): Choice: in conventional signification, the wadi's freedom to assign; in natural signification, the lack of necessitation by itself. The author distinguishes two meanings. [Note: In usul al-fiqh, ikhtiyar often relates to moral agency; here it is nuanced for logical classification.]
طبيعية (tabi'iyya): Natural signification: non-volitional, based on innate nature, but with possible detachment from the signified.
وضعية (wad'iyya): Conventional signification: volitional, based on the wadi's choice to assign.
جعل (ja'l): Making or divine causation; Ibn Mansur uses it to argue that both types depend on God's action, blurring the distinction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because the term 'choice' has different meanings in each context
C) Because natural signs are impossible to detach
D) Because conventional signification is not subject to divine will

Key Takeaway
The classification of signification into natural and conventional is preserved by recognizing that 'choice' in conventional signification means the putter's freedom to assign, while in natural signification it merely means the signifier does not necessitate the signified; these are distinct.

Types of Signification and the Primacy of the Conventional
Commentary defines three types of signification and justifies logicians' focus on conventional signification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of signification (dalala) and explain why logicians exclusively study conventional linguistic signification.

Core Idea
Only conventional signification is precise and universal—thus the foundation of logical analysis.

In this passage, the commentator distinguishes three types of signification: natural (like blushing indicating shame), rational (based on intellectual inference), and conventional (imposed by human convention). He then explains that logicians restrict their study to the conventional type because it alone is stable, precise (mundabit), and universally beneficial. This classification is fundamental for understanding how language functions in logical reasoning and how definitions and arguments rely on fixed meanings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of signification
The passage lists: natural (dalala tabi'iyya) – e.g., redness of face upon shyness; rational (dalala 'aqliyya) – inference by intellect; and conventional verbal (dalala lafziyya wad'iyya) – meaning assigned by a language's convention.
Natural signification is also illustrated by medical symptoms (e.g., yellow face for fear).
2
Locate the logicians' choice
The text states that logicians (al-qawm) consider only the first division – conventional signification. Find the phrase: 'wa-l-mu'tabar 'inda al-qawm min hadhihi al-aqsam: al-qism al-awwal faqat'.
3
Understand the justification
The commentator gives two reasons: precision (indibat) – conventional meanings do not change like natural or rational ones – and universal benefit (umum al-naf') – all humans can rely on the same linguistic conventions for communication and reasoning.
Rational signification varies with intelligence; natural signification varies with temperaments.
4
Analyze the ihtibak discussion
The commentary addresses a possible objection: the poet omitted the word 'wad'iyya' (conventional) in the line, which might seem like a deletion (ihtibak). The commentator argues it is not a deletion because the conventional sense is understood from the idafa construction 'dalalat al-lafz' (signification of utterance).
Ihtibak is a rhetorical figure; but here the meaning is clear without omission.

(٦) - وَدَلَالَهُ غَيْرِ اللَّفْظِ الطَّبِيعِيَّةُ كَدَلَالَةِ حُمْرَةِ الوَجْهِ عَلَى الخَجَلِ، وَصُفْرَتِهِ عَلَى الوَجَلِ، وَجَمِيعُ مَا يَسْتَدِلُ بِهِ الأَطِبَاءُ عَلَى المَرَضِ مِنْ هَذَا النَّوْعِ . وَالمُعْتَبَرُ عِنْدَ القَوْمِ مِنْ هَذِهِ الأَقْسَام : القِسْمُ الأَوَّلُ فَقَط وَهُوَ دَلَالَةُ اللَّفْظِ الوَضْعِيَّةُ، وَلَهَا تَرْجَمَ النَّاظِمُ ، إِلَّا أَنَّهُ تَرَكَ مِنْهَا وَصْفاً ذَكَرَهُ فِي أَوَّلِ البَيْتِ وَهُوَ : كَوْنُ الدَّلَالَةِ لَفْظِيَّةٌ . وَلَا يُقَالُ : حَذَفَ مِنَ البَيْتِ أَيْضاً قَيْدَ الوَضْعِيَّةِ الَّذِي ذَكَرَهُ فِي التَّرْجَمَةِ، فَيَكُونُ مِنْ نَوْع الاحْتِبَاكِ . لِأَنَّا نَقُولُ : لَمْ يَحْذِفْهُ مِنَ البَيْتِ، بَلْ هُوَ مَأخُوذُ مِنَ العَهْدِ الَّذِي فِي الإِضَافَةِ فِي قَوْلِهِ : دَلَالَةُ اللَّفْظ». وَإِنَّمَا اعْتَبَرُوا مِنَ الدَّلَالَاتِ القِسْمَ الأَوَّلَ فَقَط ؛ لِانْضِبَاطِهِ وَعُمُومِ النَّفْعِ بِهِ. قوله : (فِي العَقْلِيَّاتِ... إلخ) صوابُهُ : في التعلم والتعليم وغيرهما في العقليات والنقليَّات وغيرهما، أي: الطبيعيَّات، وأما عبارته فتقتضي أنَّ التَّعلم والتعليم في غير العقليات والنقليات؛ إذ العطف يقتضي المغايرة. قوله : (بِخِلَافِ غَيْرِهَا ) أي : اللفظيَّة الوضعية، وهما العقلية والطبيعية؛ لاختلاف العقول ذكاء وبلادةً، حتّى أنَّ بعض العقول يناقض بعضاً، والطبيعية لا تنضبط لاختلاف الطبائع، وأما ما وضعه الواضع فلا يتغيَّر ولا يختلف. قوله : (مِنَ الأَلْطَافِ حُدُوتُ ... إلخ) أي : ألطاف الله تعالى فـ «أل عِوَضُ مِنَ المضاف إليه ؛ أي : من الأمور الملطوف بالنَّاس بها . قوله : (اللُّغَوِيَّةِ) لا يختص بلغة العرب، وإن كان الغالب انصراف اللُّغة لها، بل يشمل غيرها . قوله : (لِيُعَبِّرَ عَمَّا فِي الضَّمِيرِ) هذه علَّةٌ لـ «الألطاف» ؛ أي: ليعبر كلٌّ مِنَ النَّاس لغيره عمَّا في نفسه ممَّا يحتاج إليه في معاشه ومعاده، حتّى يعاونه ذلك الغير عليه ؛ لعدم استقلاله بما يحتاج إليه، فإنَّ الإنسان لا يمكن تعيشه إلَّا بمشاركته أبناء جنسه وإعلامهم بما في ضميره من المقاصد والأغراض. قوله : (قَالَ السَّعْدُ) أي : في شرح الشَّمسيَّة». قوله : (وَلِلْقَصْدِ إِلَى إِبْقَائِهَا) أي: الألفاظ.
Like their described objects, and it is apparent. (6) – And the signification of other than utterance, the natural, like the signification of redness of the face upon shyness, and its yellowness upon fear, and all that by which physicians infer illness is of this type. And what is considered by the people [logicians] among these divisions is only the first division, which is the conventional signification of utterance, and the versifier translated it, except that he left out from it a description he mentioned at the beginning of the line, which is: that the signification is verbal. And it is not said: He deleted from the line as well the condition of conventionality which he mentioned in the translation, so it would be of the type of ihtibak. Because we say: He did not delete it from the line, rather it is taken from the covenant that is in the idafa in his saying: 'signification of utterance'. And they only considered from the significations the first division because of its precision and universal benefit. His statement: 'in the rational[s]... etc.' its correct [form] is: in learning and teaching and other than them in the rational and transmitted [sciences] and other than them, i.e., the natural [sciences]. But his wording implies that learning and teaching are other than the rational and transmitted, since conjunction implies difference. His statement: 'unlike other than it' i.e., the conventional verbal [signification], and they are the rational and natural; because of the difference of minds in intelligence and dullness, so much so that some minds contradict others, and the natural does not have precision due to difference of natures. But what the positer posited does not change nor differ. His statement: 'from the kindnesses is the occurrence... etc.' i.e., the kindnesses of Allah – so 'al' is a substitute for the added [noun]; i.e., from the matters by which people are favored. His statement: 'linguistic' is not specific to the Arabic language, although the usage mostly is for it, rather it includes other than it. His statement: 'to express what is in the mind' is a cause for 'kindnesses'; i.e., so that each person may express to another what is in himself of what he needs in his livelihood and his afterlife, so that the other may assist him in it, because of his lack of independence in what he needs. For man cannot live except by participation with his fellow humans and informing them of the purposes and objectives in his mind. His statement: 'al-Sa'd said' i.e., in the commentary on al-Shamsiyya. His statement: 'and for the intent to preserve them' i.e., the utterances.
Source pages p. 442

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) on the matn division of signification (dalala). The commentator begins by distinguishing three types: natural signification (like redness of the face indicating shyness), rational, and conventional. He then states that logicians (al-qawm) only consider the first division—conventional linguistic signification—because it is precise (mundabit) and universally beneficial (umum al-naf'). The next part addresses a poetic line from the matn where the poet omitted the adjective 'conventional' (wad'iyya). Some might think this omission is a case of ihtibak (a rhetorical figure where part of two coordinate phrases are omitted), but the commentator argues it is not a deletion; rather, the term is understood from the idafa construction in 'dalalat al-lafz'. He then explains why conventional signification is preferred: it does not change like rational signification (which varies with intellects) or natural signification (which varies with temperaments). Finally, the commentator discusses the purpose of language: it is a divine kindness (lutf) enabling humans to express their needs for mutual assistance. The passage highlights the centrality of conventional linguistic signification in logic.

دلالة لفظية وضعية
احتباك
انضباط

Key Takeaway
Logicians prioritize conventional signification because it alone is stable, precise, and universally applicable for reasoning and communication.

The Four Types of Existence and the Real vs. Metaphorical Distinction
Commentary explains four types of existence and corrects that only external existence is real.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the four types of existence in Islamic logic and explain which are real and which are metaphorical.

Core Idea
Only existence in external objects is truly real; mental existence is a representation.

In this lesson, we examine a passage from al-Sanusi's commentary that classifies existence into four levels: in external objects, in minds, in expression, and in writing. It clarifies that only the first is genuinely real, a crucial distinction for understanding how concepts relate to reality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four types of existence
Find the four categories listed in the Arabic: وجود في الأعيان, وجود في الأذهان, وجود في العبارة, وجود في الكتابة.
They correspond to existence in external objects, minds, expression, and writing.
2
Distinguish real vs. metaphorical
Note that the text initially says the first two (external and mental) are real (حقيقيان) and the last two (expression and writing) are metaphorical (مجازيان).
3
Understand al-Sanusi's correction
Al-Sanusi, in his Sharh al-Muqaddimat, restricts real existence to only the first (external), saying mental existence is merely a representation. Identify his phrase: أن الأول فقط هو الحقيقي.
This is the verified view (التحقيق).
4
Apply the distinction to an example
Take the concept 'human'. Describe its existence in each category: external (individual humans), mental (the universal concept), expression (the word 'insan'), and writing (the written letters). Determine which are real according to al-Sanusi.
Only external existence is real; the others are metaphorical.

فِي الأَعْيَانِ، وَوُجُودٌ فِي الأَذْهَانِ، وَوُجُودٌ فِي العِبَارَةِ، وَوُجُودٌ فِي الكِتَابَةِ، وَالأَوَّلَانِ حَقِيقِيَّانِ وَالأَخِيرَانِ مَجَازِيَّانِ . وَالتَّحْقِيقُ عِنْدَ السَّنُوسِيِّ فِي شَرْح المُقَدِّمَاتِ : أَنَّ الأَوَّلَ فَقَدْ هُوَ الحَقِيقِيُّ، وَهُوَ الظَّاهِرُ.
In external objects, and existence in minds, and existence in expression, and existence in writing; the first two are real, and the last two are metaphorical. And the verification according to al-Sanusi in his commentary on al-Muqaddimat is that only the first is real, and that is apparent.
Source pages p. 444

Commentary
This passage classifies existence into four categories: in external objects (a'yan), in minds (adhhan), in expression (ibara), and in writing (kitaba). The first two are considered real (haqiqiyan) while the last two are metaphorical (majaziyan). It then notes that al-Sanusi in his Sharh al-Muqaddimat restricts the real to only the first, external existence, and says that is the apparent view. The commentary clarifies that mental existence is only a representation, not the thing itself. This distinction is foundational for understanding how concepts exist in the mind versus the world, relevant to logic's treatment of universals.

Resolving the Objection of Change in Divine Speech
Author defends divine speech from change by interpreting 'nisba' as 'possessor of a relation'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the objection that divine speech as a relation entails change, and explain how the Ash'ari theologians resolve it by interpreting 'nisba' as 'possessor of a relation'.

Core Idea
Divine speech is not a relation itself but possesses a relation.

In theology, defining divine speech (al-kalam al-nafsi) as a relation (nisba) seems to imply that it changes when the related terms change. This passage presents that objection and the resolution offered by Ash'ari commentators: the word 'nisba' is shorthand for 'possessor of a relation' (dhu nisba). This move preserves the eternity of divine speech while acknowledging its relation to contingent commands and prohibitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Find the clause where the objector says that if divine speech is a relation, it necessitates change: 'أَنَّ كَوْنَهُ نِسْبَةٌ يَقْتَضِي التَّغَيُّرَ'. The objector argues that because relations change when their terms change (as in the examples Zaid is standing, Amr is standing), divine speech would be subject to change.
2
Understand why change is problematic
Note the assertion that divine speech is eternal and unchanging: 'وَكَلَامُهُ تَعَالَى قَدِيمٌ لا يَلْحَقُهُ التَّغَيُّر'. This is a foundational Ash'ari commitment.
3
Locate the resolution
The commentator states that Ibn al-Hajib's statement must be interpreted with an implied annexation: 'ذُو نِسْبَةٍ' (possessor of a relation). This is the key hermeneutic move. The text says: 'فَقَدْ تَأوَّلَهُ المُحَقِّقُونَ ... بِأَنَّهُ لَا بُدَّ فِيهِ مِنْ تَقْدِيرِ مُضَافٍ؛ أَيْ : ذُو نِسْبَةٍ'.
The term 'mudaf' (annexed) is a grammatical term; here it means a genitive construction.
4
Confirm the corrected definition
The author of al-Marasad explicitly defines internal speech as a meaning-attribute that is 'ذُو نِسْبَةٍ', not the relation itself. This avoids the problem: the attribute does not change; only its relation to different objects changes.
The conclusion: 'فَتَحَصَّلَ مِنْ هَذَا : أَنَّ الكَلَامَ القَدِيمَ لَيْسَ هُوَ نَفْسَ النِّسْبَةِ , بَلْ هُوَ صِفَةُ مَعْنَى مُتَعَلِّقٌ بِالنِّسْبَةِ لا عَيْنَهَا'.
5
Summarize the logical move
The resolution uses a linguistic shift: interpreting an ambiguous term in a way that removes a contradiction. This is a common tool in Islamic theology (ta'wil). Here, 'nisba' is interpreted as 'dhu nisba' to preserve divine immutability while maintaining that speech has a relational aspect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمِنْهَا : أَنَّ كَوْنَهُ نِسْبَةٌ يَقْتَضِي التَّغَيُّرَ - أَيْ : بِتَغَيرِ الطَّرَفَيْنِ , وَكَلَامُهُ تَعَالَى قَدِيمٌ لا يَلْحَقُهُ التَّغَيُّر وَأَمَّا كَلَامُ ابْنِ الحَاجِبِ فَقَدْ تَأوَّلَهُ المُحَقِّقُونَ مِنْ شُرَّاحِهِ وَغَيْرِهِمْ: بِأَنَّهُ لَا بُدَّ فِيهِ مِنْ تَقْدِيرِ مُضَافٍ؛ أَيْ : ذُو نِسْبَةٍ . (قصارة). قوله : (أَيْ: بِتَغَيُّرِ الطَّرَفَيْنِ مثلاً قولك : زَيْدُ قَائِمٌ» نسبة، و: «عَمْرُو قَائِمُ» نسبةٌ أخرى، والطرفان متغايران، فيحصل التغاير في النسبة بسبب تغير الطرفين. قوله : (وَأَمَّا كَلَامُ ابْنِ الحَاجِبِ فَقَدْ تَأَوَّلَهُ ... إلخ) ممَّن تأوَّله ابن زكري في محصل المقاصد ونصه : قول ابن الحاجب الكلام النَّفسي نسبة يجب ردُّه إن لم يرد أنَّه ذو نسبة. اهـ. وممَّن صرَّح بهذا المضاف صاحب «المراصد» ونصه : وَقُلْ مُعَرِّفُ الكَلَامِ النَّفْسِي وَهُوَ المُضَافُ لِلْجَنَابِ القُدْسِي صِفَةٌ مَعْنَى قَائِمٌ بِالذَّات ذُو نِسْبَةٍ مُنَافِضُ الصفات فتحصل من هذا : أنَّ الكلام القديم ليس هو نفس النسبة , بل هو صفة معنى متعلّق بالنسبة لا عينها .
Among them: that its being a relation necessitates change — i.e., due to change of the two terms. And His speech — exalted is He — is eternal, change does not affect it. As for the statement of Ibn al-Hajib, the verifiers among his commentators and others have interpreted it: that it must involve an implied annexation; i.e., 'possessor of a relation'. (Qasara?) His statement: (i.e., due to change of the two terms, for example your saying 'Zaid is standing' is a relation, and 'Amr is standing' is another relation, and the two terms are different, so the variation in the relation occurs due to change of the two terms.) His statement: (As for the statement of Ibn al-Hajib, it has been interpreted by...etc.) Among those who interpreted it is Ibn Zakari in Muhsal al-Maqasid, and his text: 'The statement of Ibn al-Hajib that the internal speech is a relation must be rejected unless he means it is possessor of a relation.' End. And among those who explicitly stated this addition is the author of al-Marasad, and his text: 'And say the definer of internal speech, and it is the annexed to the sacred presence, a meaning-attribute subsisting by the essence, possessor of a relation, contradicting the attributes.' So it is concluded from this: that the eternal speech is not the relation itself, but rather it is a meaning-attribute attached to the relation, not identical to it.
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its resolution. The objection is that if divine speech (kalām nafsī) is described as a relation (nisba), that would entail change because relations change when their terms change. But divine speech is eternal and unchanging. The resolution is that the term 'nisba' should not be taken literally; rather, it is an abbreviated expression meaning 'possessor of a relation' (dhū nisba). In other words, divine speech is a quality of the Divine Essence that has a relation to the commands, prohibitions, etc., but it is not the relation itself. The commentator supports this by citing Ibn Zakari and the author of al-Marasad, who explicitly define internal speech as a meaning-attribute that is a 'possessor of relation', not a relation. Thus, the change that occurs in the expressions does not affect the eternal attribute.

D) It applies only to created speech.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari resolution to the objection that divine speech as a relation entails change is to interpret 'nisba' as shorthand for 'possessor of a relation', thus preserving divine immutability.

Ibn 'Arḍūn's Rational Signification of the Qur'an
Presents Ibn 'Arḍūn's view and its justification by al-Qasṭīīnī.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn 'Arḍūn's claim that the Qur'an's signification on eternal speech is rational and distinguish it from conventional signification.

Core Idea
Rational signification: the intellect infers the speaker's inner state, not the word's assigned meaning.

In this passage, Ibn 'Arḍūn proposes that the Qur'an's indication of the eternal attribute of speech is not based on linguistic convention (waḍʻ) but on a rational inference (dalāla 'aqliyya). He uses the example of 'Give me water' to show that the listener instantly knows the speaker's inner request. This lesson unpacks his argument and its justification by al-Qasṭīīnī.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
Find Ibn 'Arḍūn's statement that the signification of the Qur'an upon the eternal speech is rational (ʻaqliyya). Note that he contrasts it with conventional signification.
The claim is in the first line of the Arabic passage.
2
Understand the example
Ibn 'Arḍūn uses the phrase 'اسقني الماء' (give me water) to illustrate. The intellect infers that the speaker has an inner request for water, even though the word 'water' itself conventionally refers to the external substance.
The example shows the distinction between external reference and inner state.
3
Analyze al-Qasṭīīnī's justification
Al-Qasṭīīnī explains that the coiner (wāḍiʻ) only assigned words to external objects; the inference to the speaker's inner state is not part of the coinage but is a rational judgment based on inner intuition (wijdān).
This clarifies that rational signification does not rely on a pre-existing conventional link to inner meanings.
4
Distinguish from necessary concomitance
Note that al-Qasṭīīnī explicitly states that rational signification does not mean that words are necessarily indicative (malzūma 'aqlan) of inner meanings. It is not a logical necessity but an intellectual inference that can be negated.
This distinction is crucial for understanding the subsequent critique.

وَاخْتَارَ ابْنُ عَرْضُونَ فِي شَرْحِ الحَفِيدَةِ : أَنَّ دَلَالَةَ الْقُرْآنِ عَلَى الْكَلَامِ الْقَدِيمِ عَقْلِيَّةٌ ; قَالَ: كَدَلَالَةِ اسْقِنِي الْمَاءَ ; عَلَى أَنَّ الْمُتَكَلَّمَ بِهِ مُقْتَضِ فِي نَفْسِهِ لِلْمَاءِ، وَأَنَّهُ مُتَحَدِّثُ فِي ضَمِيرِهِ بِذَلِكَ. قوله : (فِي شَرْحِ الْحَفِيدَةِ) هِيَ لِلسَّنُوسِي . وَجَزَمَ الْعَلَّامَةُ الْقَسْمَطِينِيُّ بِأَنَّهَا عَقْلِيَّةٌ كَمَا عِنْدَ ابْنِ عَرْضُونَ، وَوَجْهُهُ: بِأَنَّ قَوْلَ الْقَائِلِ: «اسْقِنِي الْمَاءَ» يَدُلُّ عَلَى قِيَامَ الطَّلَبِ بِنَفْسِهِ، وَلَيْسَتْ دَلَالَتُهُ عَلَيْهِ بِالْوَضْعِ؛ لِأَنَّ الْوَاضِعَ إِنَّمَا وَضَعَ الْأَلْفَاظَ لِتَدُلَّ عَلَى الْمُسَمَّيَاتِ الْكَائِنَةِ فِي الْخَارِجِ، وَأَمَّا أَنَّ مِنْ تَكَلَّمَ بِهَا قَدْ قَامَتْ مَعَانٍ بِبَاطِنِهِ، فَهَذَا الْقَدْرُ لَا تَعَرُّضَ لِلْوَاضِعِ إِلَيْهِ ، وَإِنَّمَا هُوَ أَمْرٌ يَحْكُمُ بِهِ الْعَقْلُ مِنْ جِهَةِ الْوِجْدَانِ، وَهَذَا الْمُرَادُ بِالدَّلَالَةِ الْعَقْلِيَّةِ، وَلَيْسَ الْمُرَادُ بِهَا أَنَّ الْأَلْفَاظَ مَلْزُومَةٌ عَقْلًا لِقِيَامِ مَعَانٍ بِنَفْسِ الْمُتَكَلِّمِ بِهَا . اهـ . ثُمَّ قَالَ: وَهُوَ مَرْدُودٌ ; بِأَنَّ الْأَلْفَاظَ لَيْسَتْ مَلْزُومَةٌ عَقْلًا ; لِقِيَامٍ مَعَانٍ بِنَفْسِ الْمُتَكَلِّمِ بِهَا . قوله : (لَيْسَتْ مَلْزُومَةٌ عَقْلًا) أَيِ : اللُّزُومُ الذِّهْنِيُّ بِحَيْثُ كُلَّمَا وُجِدَ اللَّفْظُ دَلَّ عَلَى قِيَامِ مَعْنًى بِنَفْسِ الْمُتَكَلِّمِ بِهِ ; إِذْ قَدْ يَتَكَلَّمُ بِأَلْفَاظٍ لَا تُقْصَدُ مَعَانِيهَا أَصْلًا، وَحِينَئِذٍ فَالدَّلَالَةُ لَيْسَتْ عَقْلِيَّةً، وَقَدْ عَلِمْتَ مِنْ كَلَامِ الْقَسْمَطِينِيِّ الْمُتَقَدَّمِ أَنَّ هَذَا الِاعْتِرَاضَ غَيْرَ وَارِدٍ عَلَى مَنْ يَقُولُ: إِنَّهَا عَقْلِيَّةٌ ; لِأَنَّهُ لَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ قَوْلِهِ : (إِنَّهَا عَقْلِيَّةٌ) أَنْ يَكُونَ اللُّزُومُ ذِهْنِيًّا حَتَّى يُعْتَرَضَ عَلَيْهِ : «بِأَنَّ الْأَلْفَاظَ لَيْسَتْ مَلْزُومَةً . . . إِلَخْ»، فَكَانَ مِنْ حَقِّ هَذَا الشَّارِحِ أَنْ لَوِ اعْتَرَضَ عَلَى الْمُجِيبِ بِفَسَادِ الْقِيَاسِ؛ إِذْ مِنْ شَرْطِهِ الْمُسَاوَاةُ، وَهِيَ مُنْتَفِيَةٌ هُنَا ; إِذْ لَا مُنَاسَبَةَ بَيْنَ نِسْبَةِ الْكَلَامِ النَّفْسَانِيِّ إِلَى الْمَخْلُوقِ وَنِسْبَتِهِ إِلَى الْبَارِي تَعَالَى، فَيَقْتَضِي كَلَامُهُ أَنَّ كُلَّ مَا يَلْغُو بِهِ كُلُّ مَخْلُوقٍ مِنْ بَاطِلٍ أَوْ غَيْرِهِ دَالٍّ عَلَى أَنَّ تِلْكَ الْمَعَانِيَ قَائِمَةٌ بِالْبَارِي، وَهُوَ مُتَكَلِّمٌ بِهَا ; لِأَنَّهُ الَّذِي أَوْجَدَ جَمِيعَ الْحُرُوفِ الْجَارِيَةِ عَلَى لِسَانِ الْخَلْقِ، فَتَأَمَّلْ.
And Ibn 'Arḍūn chose in his commentary on al-Hafida that the signification of the Qur'an upon the eternal speech is rational. He said: Like the signification of the phrase 'Give me water' upon the fact that the speaker intends water in his soul and that he is speaking about it in his conscience. ... His statement: (In the commentary on al-Hafida) it is by al-Sanūsī. And al-'Allāma al-Qasṭīīnī affirmed that it is rational, as according to Ibn 'Arḍūn. Its justification: that the speaker's saying 'Give me water' indicates the presence of the request in his soul, and its indication is not by convention, because the coiner only coined words to signify external objects. As for the fact that the speaker has meanings in his inner self, this is not addressed by the coiner; rather it is something judged by the intellect by way of inner intuition (wijdān). This is what is meant by rational signification; it is not meant that the words are necessarily indicative (malzūma 'aqlan) of the presence of meanings in the speaker's soul. End. Then he said: And it is rejected, because words are not necessarily indicative rationally of the presence of meanings in the speaker's soul. His statement: (not necessarily indicative rationally) i.e., mental necessity such that whenever a word is found, it indicates the presence of a meaning in the speaker's soul; for one may utter words without intending their meanings at all. In that case, the signification is not rational. And you have known from the previous statement of al-Qasṭīīnī that this objection does not apply to those who say it is rational, because their saying 'it is rational' does not entail that the necessity is mental such that it could be objected that 'words are not necessarily indicative...' So it would have been appropriate for this commentator to have objected to the respondent by pointing out the invalidity of the analogy, because one of its conditions is equivalence (musāwā), and that is absent here; for there is no proportion between the relation of internal speech to a creature and its relation to the Exalted Creator. His statement would imply that everything that any creature utters, whether false or otherwise, indicates that those meanings are present in the Creator and that He speaks them, because He is the one who created all the letters that flow on the tongues of creatures. So reflect.
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This passage discusses the type of signification (dalāla) of the Qur'an. Ibn 'Arḍūn proposes that the Qur'an's signification on the eternal attribute of speech is rational ('aqlī), not conventional (waḍ'ī). He gives the example: when someone says 'Give me water', the listener intuitively knows that the speaker has an inner request for water. This is not due to linguistic convention but to the intellect's inference from the external utterance to the internal state. al-Qasṭīīnī agrees and explains that the coiner only defined words for external referents, not for inner states. However, the commentator (al-Bannani) rejects this view, arguing that words are not necessarily linked to inner meanings: one can utter words without intending them, as in joking, lying, or sleep. Therefore, the signification cannot be rational. The commentator further notes that the objection could be directed not at the notion of rationality itself but at the invalid analogy between human and divine speech, since there is no equivalence between the relation of speech to a creature and to God. This is a sophisticated critique of the rational signification theory.

Key Terms

وضع
لزوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main reason al-Qasṭīīnī gives for why the signification of 'Give me water' on the speaker's inner request is not conventional?
A) Because the coiner (wāḍiʻ) did not assign words to inner meanings.
B) Because the word 'water' is a universal term.
C) Because the listener has no access to the speaker's inner self.
D) Because the phrase is an imperative, not an indicative.

Key Takeaway
Rational signification (dalāla 'aqliyya) is an intellectual inference from utterance to inner state, distinct from conventional (waḍʻī) signification that only covers external referents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify an example from Islamic theology where the signification of a revealed text is considered rational rather than conventional. Explain why it fits the definition of dalāla 'aqliyya as presented by Ibn 'Arḍūn.

15 min
Critique of Ibn 'Arḍūn's Rational Signification
Al-Bannani's critique of Ibn 'Arḍūn's rational signification and the invalid analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct al-Bannani's objection to Ibn 'Arḍūn's claim and evaluate his argument against the analogy between human and divine speech.

Core Idea
Words are not necessarily linked to inner states; the analogy between human and divine speech fails due to lack of equivalence.

In this part of the passage, al-Bannani rejects Ibn 'Arḍūn's rational signification. He argues that words can be uttered without intending their meanings (e.g., joking, lying), so there is no necessary link. He also points out that the analogy between human speech indicating inner states and the Qur'an indicating God's speech is invalid because of a lack of proportion (musāwā) between creature and Creator.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rejection
Al-Bannani says 'وھو مردود' (it is rejected). Locate this phrase and note the immediate reason given: words are not necessarily indicative (malzūma 'aqlan) of inner meanings.
The rejection targets the claim that rational signification is a necessary link.
2
Understand the counterexample
Al-Bannani gives the example of uttering words without intending their meanings at all. This shows that the link between utterance and inner state is not necessary, so the signification cannot be purely rational.
This is a strong empirical objection: people often speak without meaning what they say.
3
Analyze the commentator's response to al-Qasṭīīnī
Al-Bannani notes that al-Qasṭīīnī had already clarified that rational signification does not mean logical necessity. Therefore, the objection based on lack of necessary relation does not refute al-Qasṭīīnī's position. The real issue is the invalid analogy between human and divine speech.
This shows a nuanced critique: the objection should have been to the analogy, not to the concept of rationality itself.
4
Examine the invalidity of the analogy
Al-Bannani points out that the analogy (qiyās) between human speech indicating inner states and the Qur'an indicating God's speech fails because there is no equivalence (musāwā) between the relation of speech to a creature and its relation to the Creator. Whatever a creature says (even falsehood) would, by analogy, indicate that God has those meanings, which is absurd.
This is a powerful a fortiori argument: the analogy leads to unacceptable consequences.

بِأَنَّ الْأَلْفَاظَ لَيْسَتْ مَلْزُومَةٌ عَقْلًا ; لِقِيَامٍ مَعَانٍ بِنَفْسِ الْمُتَكَلِّمِ بِهَا . قوله : (لَيْسَتْ مَلْزُومَةٌ عَقْلًا) أَيِ : اللُّزُومُ الذِّهْنِيُّ بِحَيْثُ كُلَّمَا وُجِدَ اللَّفْظُ دَلَّ عَلَى قِيَامِ مَعْنًى بِنَفْسِ الْمُتَكَلِّمِ بِهِ ; إِذْ قَدْ يَتَكَلَّمُ بِأَلْفَاظٍ لَا تُقْصَدُ مَعَانِيهَا أَصْلًا، وَحِينَئِذٍ فَالدَّلَالَةُ لَيْسَتْ عَقْلِيَّةً، وَقَدْ عَلِمْتَ مِنْ كَلَامِ الْقَسْمَطِينِيِّ الْمُتَقَدَّمِ أَنَّ هَذَا الِاعْتِرَاضَ غَيْرَ وَارِدٍ عَلَى مَنْ يَقُولُ: إِنَّهَا عَقْلِيَّةٌ ; لِأَنَّهُ لَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ قَوْلِهِ : (إِنَّهَا عَقْلِيَّةٌ) أَنْ يَكُونَ اللُّزُومُ ذِهْنِيًّا حَتَّى يُعْتَرَضَ عَلَيْهِ : «بِأَنَّ الْأَلْفَاظَ لَيْسَتْ مَلْزُومَةً . . . إِلَخْ»، فَكَانَ مِنْ حَقِّ هَذَا الشَّارِحِ أَنْ لَوِ اعْتَرَضَ عَلَى الْمُجِيبِ بِفَسَادِ الْقِيَاسِ؛ إِذْ مِنْ شَرْطِهِ الْمُسَاوَاةُ، وَهِيَ مُنْتَفِيَةٌ هُنَا ; إِذْ لَا مُنَاسَبَةَ بَيْنَ نِسْبَةِ الْكَلَامِ النَّفْسَانِيِّ إِلَى الْمَخْلُوقِ وَنِسْبَتِهِ إِلَى الْبَارِي تَعَالَى، فَيَقْتَضِي كَلَامُهُ أَنَّ كُلَّ مَا يَلْغُو بِهِ كُلُّ مَخْلُوقٍ مِنْ بَاطِلٍ أَوْ غَيْرِهِ دَالٍّ عَلَى أَنَّ تِلْكَ الْمَعَانِيَ قَائِمَةٌ بِالْبَارِي، وَهُوَ مُتَكَلِّمٌ بِهَا ; لِأَنَّهُ الَّذِي أَوْجَدَ جَمِيعَ الْحُرُوفِ الْجَارِيَةِ عَلَى لِسَانِ الْخَلْقِ، فَتَأَمَّلْ.
And Ibn 'Arḍūn chose in his commentary on al-Hafida that the signification of the Qur'an upon the eternal speech is rational. He said: Like the signification of the phrase 'Give me water' upon the fact that the speaker intends water in his soul and that he is speaking about it in his conscience. ... His statement: (In the commentary on al-Hafida) it is by al-Sanūsī. And al-'Allāma al-Qasṭīīnī affirmed that it is rational, as according to Ibn 'Arḍūn. Its justification: that the speaker's saying 'Give me water' indicates the presence of the request in his soul, and its indication is not by convention, because the coiner only coined words to signify external objects. As for the fact that the speaker has meanings in his inner self, this is not addressed by the coiner; rather it is something judged by the intellect by way of inner intuition (wijdān). This is what is meant by rational signification; it is not meant that the words are necessarily indicative (malzūma 'aqlan) of the presence of meanings in the speaker's soul. End. Then he said: And it is rejected, because words are not necessarily indicative rationally of the presence of meanings in the speaker's soul. His statement: (not necessarily indicative rationally) i.e., mental necessity such that whenever a word is found, it indicates the presence of a meaning in the speaker's soul; for one may utter words without intending their meanings at all. In that case, the signification is not rational. And you have known from the previous statement of al-Qasṭīīnī that this objection does not apply to those who say it is rational, because their saying 'it is rational' does not entail that the necessity is mental such that it could be objected that 'words are not necessarily indicative...' So it would have been appropriate for this commentator to have objected to the respondent by pointing out the invalidity of the analogy, because one of its conditions is equivalence (musāwā), and that is absent here; for there is no proportion between the relation of internal speech to a creature and its relation to the Exalted Creator. His statement would imply that everything that any creature utters, whether false or otherwise, indicates that those meanings are present in the Creator and that He speaks them, because He is the one who created all the letters that flow on the tongues of creatures. So reflect.

Commentary
This passage discusses the type of signification (dalāla) of the Qur'an. Ibn 'Arḍūn proposes that the Qur'an's signification on the eternal attribute of speech is rational ('aqlī), not conventional (waḍ'ī). He gives the example: when someone says 'Give me water', the listener intuitively knows that the speaker has an inner request for water. This is not due to linguistic convention but to the intellect's inference from the external utterance to the internal state. al-Qasṭīīnī agrees and explains that the coiner only defined words for external referents, not for inner states. However, the commentator (al-Bannani) rejects this view, arguing that words are not necessarily linked to inner meanings: one can utter words without intending them, as in joking, lying, or sleep. Therefore, the signification cannot be rational. The commentator further notes that the objection could be directed not at the notion of rationality itself but at the invalid analogy between human and divine speech, since there is no equivalence between the relation of speech to a creature and to God. This is a sophisticated critique of the rational signification theory.

Checkpoint
What is the main flaw al-Bannani identifies in the analogy between human speech indicating inner states and the Qur'an indicating God's speech?
A) The Qur'an is not speech.

5 min
Al-'Abbādī's Clarification: The Qur'an Denotes Attachments of Divine Speech
Author presents al-'Abbādī's verification regarding the Qur'an's denotation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the attribute of divine speech and its attachments, and explain why the Qur'an's multiplicity does not compromise the oneness of the attribute.

Core Idea
The Qur'an is not the attribute itself but the expression of its attachments.

In Ash'ari theology, the speech of Allah is a single eternal attribute. Yet the Qur'an consists of many verses, commands, and prohibitions. How can one attribute produce multiple expressions? Al-'Abbādī resolves this by distinguishing the attribute from its attachments (muta'allaqāt). This lesson unpacks his verification as transmitted in the glosses on the commentary of al-Ṣughrā.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central claim
Find al-'Abbādī's statement about what the Qur'an denotes. He says it is not the attribute subsisting by His essence, but rather some of the attachments.
The 'attribute' refers to the eternal kalām nafsī.
2
Distinguish attribute from attachments
The attachments (muta'allaqāt) are the specific commands, prohibitions, and news expressed in the Qur'an. The attribute itself is one and simple.
Examples: 'Establish prayer' (command), 'Do not approach adultery' (prohibition), 'Indeed, Allah is with the patient' (news).
3
Understand the problem of multiplicity
If the Qur'an directly denoted the attribute, then the attribute would have to be multiple to account for different verses, conflicting with divine simplicity.
Ash'aris affirm that attributes are eternal and not divisible.
4
See how al-'Abbādī's solution preserves oneness
Multiplicity is only in the attachments (muta'allaqāt), not in the attribute of speech itself. Thus the attribute remains one while its objects are many.
This is a key defense against the Mu'tazilite claim that the Qur'an is created because it is multiple.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالْحَقُّ مَا حَقَّقَهُ الْعَبَّادِيُّ , وَنَقَلَهُ عَنْهُ الشَّيْخُ يَاسِينُ فِي حَوَاشِي شَرْح الصُّغْرَى» مِنْ: أَنَّ مَدْلُولَ الْقُرْآنِ لَيْسَ هُوَ الصِّفَةُ الْقَائِمَةُ بِذَاتِهِ تَعَالَى، بَلْ مَدْلُولُهُ بَعْضُ مُتَعَلَّقَاتِ . قوله : (فِي حَوَاشِي شَرْحِ الصُّغْرَى») عِنْدَ قَوْلِ «الشَّرْحِ»: «وَهُوَ الَّذِي عَبَّرَ عَنْهُ بِالنَّظْمِ الْمُعْجِزِ». قوله : (بَلْ مَدْلُولُهُ) هَذَا الَّذِي يَنسَاقُ إِلَيْهِ الْعَقْلُ، وَقَائِلُهُ هُوَ الشِّهَابُ الْقَاسِمِي . قوله : (بَعْضُ مُتَعَلَّقَاتِ) الْمُتَعَلَّقَاتِ: الْأَمْرُ وَالنَّهْيُ، وَالْخَبَرُ .. وَغَيْرُهَا، فَكَلَامُ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى صِفَةٌ وَاحِدَةٌ لَهَا مُتَعَلَّقَاتٌ تَنقَسِمُ إِلَى أَمْرٍ وَنَهْيٍ وَخَبَرٍ ; فَالْتَكْثِيرُ فِي تِلْكَ الْمُتَعَلَّقَاتِ دُونَهَا .
And the truth is what al-'Abbādī verified, and Shaykh Yāsīn transmitted from him in his glosses on the commentary of al-Ṣughrā: that the denotation of the Qur'an is not the attribute subsisting by His essence—exalted is He—but rather its denotation is some of the attachments. His statement: (in the glosses on the commentary of al-Ṣughrā) at the statement of the commentary: 'And it is that which is expressed by the miraculous arrangement.' His statement: (but its denotation) this is what the mind tends to, and its proponent is al-Shihāb al-Qāsimī. His statement: (some of the attachments) the attachments: command, prohibition, and news... and others. So the speech of Allah—exalted is He—is a single attribute that has attachments that divide into command, prohibition, and news; so the multiplicity is in those attachments, not in it.
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage presents the correct position according to al-'Abbādī. It clarifies that the Qur'an does not directly denote the eternal attribute of speech (kalām nafsī) which is a single attribute in God. Rather, the Qur'an denotes the attachments (muta'allaqāt) of that attribute, i.e., the specific commands, prohibitions, and pieces of news that are expressed in the Qur'anic verses. This resolves the problem of multiplicity: the divine speech is one, but its attachments are many. The commentator notes that this view is transmitted from al-'Abbādī by Shaykh Yāsīn and is also the position of al-Shihāb al-Qāsimī. This is a key definitional point in Islamic theology regarding the nature of the Qur'an.

Key Terms
متعلق (muta'allaq)
كلام الله (kalām Allāh)
صفة (ṣifa)

C) The physical letters and sounds.

Key Takeaway
The Qur'an's multiplicity is due to its expressing the diverse attachments of God's single attribute of speech, not because the attribute itself is multiple.

Refuting Necessary Indication and Defining the Qur'an's Meaning
Refutation of necessary indication and clarification of Qur'an's meaning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the eternal attribute of speech and its muta'allaqat, and explain why the Qur'an's meaning is not the attribute itself.

Core Idea
The Qur'an's meaning is not Allah's eternal speech but its relational consequences.

This passage tackles a critical Ash'ari distinction: how can the Qur'an be the speech of Allah yet differ from other scriptures? The author refutes the notion that words necessarily indicate mental meanings and then presents the correct view: the Qur'an conveys some of the muta'allaqat (entailments) of the eternal attribute, not the attribute itself. Understanding this resolves apparent contradictions between divine unity and scriptural diversity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the refuted claim
Find the claim that words necessarily indicate meanings in the speaker's mind. The author says this is refuted (mardud).
This claim is called al-dalala al-'aqliyya.
2
Understand the refutation
The refutation is that words are not mentally necessary indicators because a speaker can utter words without intending the corresponding meanings. Example: a parrot may utter words without mental concepts.
3
Grasp the correct view from al-'Abbadi
The truth (haqq) is that the meaning (madlul) of the Qur'an is not the eternal attribute (al-sifa al-qadima) subsisting in Allah's essence, but rather some of its muta'allaqat (entailments) such as commands and prohibitions.
Muta'allaqat are relational aspects; the attribute is one, its entailments are many.
4
Apply the distinction to differentiate scriptures
Each heavenly book indicates different muta'allaqat. Thus, the Qur'an's meaning differs from the Gospel's because the rulings in the Qur'an are not in the Gospel.
This preserves the oneness of the attribute while explaining textual diversity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَهُوَ مَرْدُودٌ ; بِأَنَّ الأَلْفَاظَ لَيْسَتْ مَلْزُومَةٌ عَقْلاً ; لِقِيَامٍ مَعَانٍ بِنَفْسِ المُتَكَلِّم بِهَا . (۳) - وَالحَقُّ مَا حَقَّقَهُ العَبَّادِيُّ ، وَنَقَلَهُ عَنْهُ الشَّيْخُ يَاسِينُ فِي حَوَاشِي شَرْح الصُّغْرَى» مِنْ: أَنَّ مَدْلُولَ القُرْآنِ لَيْسَ هُوَ الصِّفَةُ القَائِمَةُ بِذَاتِهِ تَعَالَى، بَلْ مَدْلُولُهُ بَعْضُ مُتَعَلَّقَاتِ الصِّفَةِ القَدِيمَةِ، كَمَا أَنَّ غَيْرَهُ مِنَ الكُتُبِ السَّمَاوِيَّةِ كَذَلِكَ ; قَالَ : وَحِينَئِذٍ يَظْهَرُ أَنَّ مَدْلُولَ القُرْآنِ غَيْرُ مَدْلُولِ الإِنْجِيلِ، .. وَهَكَذَا؛ ضَرُورَةً أَنَّ المُتَعَلَّقَاتِ المَدْلُولَةَ لِلْقُرْآنِ غَيْرُ المَدْلُولَةِ لِغَيْرِهِ، فَإِنَّ فِيهِ مِنَ الْأَحْكَامِ مَا لَيْسَ فِي غَيْرِهِ. اهـ
It is refuted: because words are not necessary indicators mentally, due to the existence of meanings in the speaker's mind. And the truth is what al-'Abbadi established, and Shaykh Yasin transmitted from him in his glosses on the commentary of al-Sughra: that the meaning of the Qur'an is not the attribute subsisting in His essence, but rather its meaning is some of the entailments (muta'allaqat) of the eternal attribute, just as other heavenly books are likewise. He said: Then it becomes apparent that the meaning of the Qur'an is different from the meaning of the Gospel, and so on; necessarily because the entailments indicated by the Qur'an are different from those indicated by other books, for the Qur'an contains rulings that are not in others. End.
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage begins by refuting an earlier claim that words necessarily indicate meanings in the speaker's mind (dalala 'aqliyya). The author argues that words are not mentally necessary indicators because a speaker can utter words without intending their corresponding meanings. The correct view, attributed to al-'Abbadi and transmitted by Shaykh Yasin, is that the meaning of the Qur'an is not the eternal attribute of speech subsisting in Allah's essence. Rather, the Qur'an's meaning is some of the muta'allaqat (entailments or consequences) of that attribute, such as commands, prohibitions, and reports. These entailments are relational aspects of the single attribute. The author then notes that this applies to all revealed books, each having its own set of entailments. Thus, the Qur'an's meaning is distinct from the Gospel's because the rulings in the Qur'an are not found in the Gospel. This distinguishes the scriptures while preserving the oneness of the divine attribute.

الصفة القديمة
المتعلقات

Key Takeaway
Words are not necessary mental indicators, and the Qur'an's meaning is a subset of the eternal attribute's entailments, not the attribute itself.

Haqiqa vs Majaz in 'Kalam Allah'
Classification of 'Kalam Allah' as haqiqi or majaz

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between haqiqa and majaz in the application of 'Kalam Allah' to the Qur'an based on the author's criteria.

Core Idea
The Qur'an is literally God's speech as revealed, but metaphorically as an indication of His eternal attribute.

In this passage, the author explains that calling the Qur'an 'the speech of Allah' is not a simple matter. Depending on what aspect we consider, the designation can be either literal (haqiqa) or metaphorical (majaz). Understanding this distinction is crucial for proper theological reasoning about the Qur'an's nature, especially in debates about its createdness and ontological status.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two classifications
The author presents two possibilities: haqiqa (literal) and majaz (metaphorical). Note the conjunction 'إِمَّا ... وَإِمَّا' indicating an exclusive alternative.
2
Extract the justification for haqiqa
The literal designation is justified because the Qur'an is sent down from Allah, not composed by humans, and not under their acquisition. The attribution is one of honor (إضافة تشريف) similar to calling Paradise 'the house of Allah'.
The honorific attribution does not change the literal nature.
3
Extract the justification for majaz
The metaphorical designation is justified because through the Qur'an we intend to indicate some of the meanings of the eternal attribute of speech. It is like a translation being called the sultan's speech, or stories of prophets in the Qur'an being called their speech even though they are translations.
The analogy shows that the indirect reference makes it metaphorical.
4
Compare the two perspectives
The same entity (the Qur'an) can be called 'Kalam Allah' literally when focusing on its origin as revealed text, and metaphorically when focusing on its relation to the eternal attribute. The author leaves both as valid depending on context.
5
Apply the distinction
In theological discussions, recognize when scholars are using the term literally or metaphorically. For example, saying 'The Qur'an is Kalam Allah' in the context of its recitation is literal; saying it to affirm the eternal attribute is metaphorical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَعَلَيْهِ فَتَسْمِيَتُهُ بِكَلَامِ اللهِ تَعَالَى : إِمَّا حَقِيقِيَّةٌ ; لِكَوْنِهِ مُنَزَّلاً مِنَ اللهِ تَعَالَى لَيْسَ مِنْ تَألِيفِ الخَلْقِ، وَلَا دَاخِلاً تَحْتَ كَسْبِهِمْ، فَتُكُونُ إِضَافَتُهُ إِلَيْهِ مِنْ إِضَافَةِ المَخْلُوقِ لِلْخَالِقِ تَشْرِيفاً ; كَمَا يُقَالُ لِلْجَنَّةِ : دَارُ اللهِ تَعَالَى. - وَإِمَّا مَجَازٌ ; لِكَوْنِهِ قُصِدَتْ بِهِ الدَّلَالَةُ عَلَى بَعْضٍ مَدْلُولِ الصِّفَةِ القَدِيمَةِ، كَمَا يُقَالُ لِلْكَلَام المُتَرْجَمِ بِهِ عَنْ كَلَامِ السُّلْطَانِ - وَاللَّهِ تَعَالَى المَثَلُ الأَعْلَى -: «هَذَا كَلَامُ السُّلْطَانِ»، وَكَمَا نَقُولُ لِلْمَحْكِي فِي القُرْآنِ عَنِ الأَنْبِيَاءِ وَأُمَمِهِمْ الأَعْجَمِيِّينَ: «هَذَا كَلَامُهُمْ»، مَعَ أَنَّهُ لَيْسَ هُوَ كَلَامُهُمْ، بَلْ جُعِلَ تَرْجَمَةٌ عَنْ كَلَامِهِمْ.
And accordingly, its being called 'the speech of Allah' is either literal, because it is sent down from Allah not composed by creation and not under their acquisition, so its attribution to Him is from the attribution of the created to the Creator as honor, like saying of Paradise: 'the house of Allah Most High'; or metaphorical, because by it is intended the indication of some of the meanings of the eternal attribute, as is said of the speech translated from the speech of the sultan—and Allah is the highest example—'this is the speech of the sultan,' and as we say of what is narrated in the Qur'an from the prophets and their non-Arab nations: 'this is their speech,' while it is not their speech, but it is made a translation of their speech.
Source pages p. 448

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of the Qur'an's status. The author explains that calling the Qur'an 'the speech of Allah' can be understood in two ways. First, as a literal designation (haqiqa) because the Qur'an is sent down from Allah, not authored by humans, so the attribution is one of honor, similar to calling Paradise 'the house of Allah'. Second, as a metaphorical designation (majaz) because the Qur'an indicates some of the entailments of the eternal attribute. The author gives analogies: a translation of the sultan's speech is called the sultan's speech, and the stories of prophets in the Qur'an are called their speech even though they are translations. This shows that the term 'Kalam Allah' applied to the Qur'an can be either literal or metaphorical depending on the perspective.

حقيقة
مجاز
إضافة تشريف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The designation of the Qur'an as 'Kalam Allah' can be literal (as revealed text) or metaphorical (as indication of the eternal attribute), and the distinction depends on the aspect considered.

Three Types of Conventional Signification: Correspondence, Inclusion, Implication
Author defines the three types of conventional signification and explains the basis for their limitation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of conventional signification (dalalah wad'iyyah) and explain why the division is inductive, not rational.

Core Idea
Words signify meanings in three ways: whole, part, or necessary entailment.

How does a word relate to its meaning? In Islamic logic, conventional signification (dalalah wad'iyyah) is the foundation. This lesson unpacks the three types—correspondence, inclusion, and implication—and reveals why the logicians limit them to three.

Guided Path
1
Define correspondence (mutaabaqah)
Correspondence is when the word signifies the entire meaning it was placed for. For example, the word 'human' signifies the whole composite meaning of 'animal rational'.
2
Define inclusion (tadammun)
Inclusion is when the word signifies a part of that meaning. For example, 'human' signifies 'animal' or 'rational' individually, as these are parts of the whole meaning.
3
Define implication (iltizaam)
Implication is when the word signifies a meaning that necessarily accompanies the original but is not part of it. For example, 'human' signifies 'capable of knowledge' as a necessary consequence of rationality.
The entailment must be by reason (bi al-'aql).
4
Understand the basis for the threefold division
The relation between word and meaning is either direct placement (correspondence) or mediated by entailment. Entailment is either internal (part of the meaning, inclusion) or external (necessary concomitant, implication). The author notes this confinement is inductive, not rational, because other combinations exist (e.g., word signifying both whole and part).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دَلَالَةُ اللَّفْظِ عَلَى مَا وَافَقَهُ يَدْعُونَهَا: «دَلَالَةَ المُطَابَقَهُ» وَجُزْئِهِ : «تَضَمُّناً»، وَمَا لَزِمْ فَهُوَ الْتِزَامَ، إِنْ بِعَقْلِ الْتَزَمُ وَلَمَّا كَانَتْ دَلَالَةُ اللَّفْظِ الوَضْعِيَّةُ - وَهِيَ المُعْتَبَرَةُ عِنْدَهُمْ كَمَا تَقَدَّمَ - تَنْقَسِمُ إِلَى ثَلَاثَةِ أَقْسَامٍ : دَلَالَةِ مُطَابَقَةٍ، وَدَلَالَةِ تَضَمُّنٍ، وَدَلَالَةِ الْتِزَامِ: أَشَارَ النَّاظِمُ إِلَى القِسْمِ الأَوَّلِ بِقَوْلِهِ : دَلَالَةُ اللَّفْظِ عَلَى مَا وَافَقَهُ) أَيْ: عَلَى المَعْنَى الَّذِي وَافَقَ مَا وُضِعَ لَهُ اللَّفْظُ ; أَيْ : مِنْ حَيْثُ إِنَّهُ وُضِعَ لَهُ.

قوله : (تَنْقَسِمُ إِلَى ثَلَاثَةِ أَقْسَامِ) وجه الحصر في الثلاثة : أنَّه لا يلزم مِن حُضُور اللفظ في الذهن حضور المعنى إلا لعلاقة بينهما، وهي إما كون اللفظ موضوعاً للمعنى كما في المطابقة، أو لأمرٍ يلزمه ذلك المعنى . ثمَّ هذا اللازم : إمَّا داخل في ملزومه كما في التَّضمن، وإمَّا خارج كما في الالتزام، وهذا الحصر استقرائي لا عقليّ؛ لأنَّه بقي دلالة اللفظ على مجموع الثلاثة، أو على الكلِّ والجزء، أو على الكلِّ واللازم، أو على الجزء واللازم .

قوله : (مَا وُضِعَ لَهُ اللَّفْظُ) صوابُهُ : «على المعنى الذي وافق اللفظ»، وأما عبارته ففيها قلق; لأن معناها حينئذ : على المعنى الذي وافق المعنى الذي وُضِعَ له اللفظ، والموافق غير الموافق، فيلزم أن يكون اللفظ غير موضوع للمعنى بنفسه، بل للمعنى الموافق للمعنى الذي وضع له اللفظ، وهو فاسد.
The signification of a word for what it agrees with they call 'signification of correspondence'; for its part, 'inclusion'; and what is entailed, it is 'implication', if by reason it is entailed. When conventional signification of the word—and it is the one considered by them as previously—is divided into three types: signification of correspondence, signification of inclusion, and signification of implication, the versifier indicated the first type by his saying: signification of a word for what it agrees with, i.e., for the meaning that agrees with what the word is placed for, i.e., in respect that it is placed for it.

His saying: 'It is divided into three types'—the reason for confining it to three is that it is not necessary from the presence of the word in the mind that the meaning be present except due to a relation between them. That relation is either that the word is placed for the meaning, as in correspondence; or it is placed for something that entails that meaning. Then this entailment: either it is inside the entailed object, as in inclusion; or outside, as in implication. And this confinement is inductive, not rational, because there remain: the signification of the word for the sum of the three, or for the whole and the part, or for the whole and the necessary, or for the part and the necessary.

His saying: 'what the word is placed for'—its correct form is: 'for the meaning that agrees with the word'. But his expression is problematic, because its meaning then would be: 'for the meaning that agrees with the meaning for which the word is placed', and the agreeing is not the agreed, so it would necessitate that the word is not placed for the meaning by itself, but rather for a meaning that agrees with the meaning for which the word is placed, and that is corrupt.
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This passage defines the three types of conventional signification (dalalah wad'iyyah) and explains why they are limited to three. The text begins by stating that when a word signifies what it is placed for (the whole meaning), this is called correspondence (mutaabaqah). When it signifies a part of that meaning, it is inclusion (tadammun). When it signifies something necessarily entailed by the meaning, it is implication (iltizaam), provided the entailment is by reason. The author then notes that conventional signification is divided into these three types. The commentary explains the reason for the confinement: the connection between word and meaning requires a relation. Either the word is directly placed for the meaning (correspondence), or it is placed for something that entails the meaning (inclusion or implication depending on whether the entailment is inside or outside). The commentary clarifies that this inductive enumeration is not exhaustive by rational necessity, as there could be other combinations (e.g., word signifying both whole and part, etc.), but the three basic types are the foundational ones. A textual correction follows: the phrase 'what the word is placed for' should be understood as 'the meaning that agrees with the word' to avoid a circular definition. The author of the gloss points out a problematic wording in the original and corrects it to preserve logical precision.

Key Terms
دلالة المطابقة
دلالة التضمن
دلالة الالتزام
دلالة وضعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the division of conventional signification into three types considered inductive (istiqra'i) rather than rational (aqlī)?
A) Because the confinement is derived from empirical observation of language, and other combinations are possible.
B) Because the author copied it from earlier logicians without proof.
C) Because the three types are the only ones logically conceivable.
D) Because the versifier said so and it cannot be questioned.

Key Takeaway
Conventional signification is of three fundamental types—correspondence, inclusion, implication—but this division is based on induction, not logical necessity, leaving room for other possible derivations.

Metaphor as Conventional Signification: Its Impact on Signification Types
Commentator explains that metaphorical usage is conventional by type, affecting signification types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the type of signification (mutabaqa, tadamun, iltizam) for a metaphorical usage based on the view that metaphor is conventional by type.

Core Idea
Metaphor is conventional by type, making metaphorical signification conformity (mutabaqa) not implication.

In logic and rhetoric, a key question is whether metaphorical usages (majaz) are considered conventional (wad'i) or not. The preponderant view holds that the original linguistic convention established not only literal meanings but also the types of relationships that allow metaphorical transfer. This has direct consequences for how we classify the signification of a metaphorical word: when a word is used metaphorically with a context (qarinah), its signification of the metaphorical meaning is conformity (mutabaqa), not inclusion (tadamun) or implication (iltizam). Understanding this distinction is essential for proper interpretation of texts in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two views on metaphor's conventionality
The passage states the preponderant view (metaphor is conventional by type) and the opposing view (not conventional at all). Note which scholars held each view (Sakkaki, Jurjani vs. al-Talwih author).
2
Understand the implication of the preponderant view
If metaphor is conventional by type, then when a word is used metaphorically for a part of its literal meaning or its necessary accompaniment, the direct signification of the metaphorical meaning is mutabaqa (conformity), not tadamun or iltizam. The context makes the metaphorical meaning the intended object.
3
Distinguish the types of signification for metaphorical and literal usages
For the metaphorical meaning itself, it's mutabaqa. For a part of that metaphorical meaning, it's tadamun. For something outside the metaphorical meaning but associated, it could be iltizam. This mirrors the classification for literal meanings, but the base is the metaphorical meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (بِنَاءً عَلَى أَنَّ المَجَازَ مَوْضُوعٌ) أي : بالنَّوع ؛ لأنَّ الواضع اعتبر العلاقات بالنوع، وهذا هو الراجح كما هو مبسوط في «التلويح». وقيل : غير موضوع أصلاً، وبه صرَّح صاحب «المفتاح»، وجزم به السيد في حواشي المطول . فينبني على الأوّل الرَّاجح : أنَّه إذا استعمل اللفظ في جزء ما وضع له، أو لازمه مجازاً بقرينة، لم تكن دلالته عليه تضمُّناً ولا التزاماً، بل مطابقة، ودلالته على جزء ذلك المعنى المجازي تضمن،
His saying: 'Based on the view that the figurative is conventional' i.e., by type; because the linguistic convention considered the relationships by type, and this is the preponderant view as is expanded in 'al-Talwih'. And it was said: it is not conventional at all, and the author of 'al-Miftah' stated that, and al-Sayyid in his glosses on 'al-Mutawwal' affirmed it. So it will follow from the first preponderant view that if the word is used for a part of what it was placed for, or its necessary accompaniment, metaphorically with a context, its indication of it is not inclusion nor implication but conformity, and its indication of part of that metaphorical meaning is inclusion.
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage addresses the question of whether metaphorical usage (مجاز) is considered conventional (وضعي). The commentator says the preponderant view is that it is conventional 'by type' (بالنوع), meaning that the original linguistic convention established not only literal meanings but also the types of relationships (like analogy, causality, etc.) that allow words to be used metaphorically. This view is supported by the work 'al-Talwih'. The opposing view, held by al-Sakkaki (author of 'al-Miftah') and al-Sayyid al-Sharif al-Jurjani, is that metaphor is not conventional at all. Based on the preponderant view, if a word is used metaphorically to signify a part of its literal meaning (e.g., 'lion' for 'bravery' part of its meaning) or a necessary accompaniment (e.g., 'lion' for 'roar'?), then that signification is not تضمن or التزام but rather مطابقة, because the metaphorical meaning has become the intended meaning via the context. However, the word's indication of a part of that metaphorical meaning (e.g., 'lion' for 'man' being part of 'brave man') would be تضمن. This shows the impact of viewing metaphor as conventional: it shifts the type of signification from implications to conformity.

Key Terms
مجاز
وضع
دلالة مطابقة
دلالة تضمن
دلالة التزام

Checkpoint

A) مطابقة
B) تضمن
C) التزام
D) Not conventional

Key Takeaway
Metaphorical language is considered conventional by type, and therefore its signification is conformity (mutabaqa) when used with a context, not inclusion or implication.
