The Three Types of Conventional Indication
Defines three types of conventional indication and their scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of conventional indication (mutabaqa, tadammun, iltizam) and explain how they apply to both literal and metaphorical usage.

Core Idea
The three indications are the key to understanding how words convey meaning beyond mere naming.

In this passage, the commentator outlines the threefold division of conventional indication that is foundational in Islamic logic and legal theory. Understanding these types—whole, part, and necessary concomitant—is essential for analyzing definitions and arguments. We will walk through each type and see that they apply equally to literal and metaphorical language, a point of debate among scholars.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types named
Find the Arabic terms: المطابقة, التضمن, الالتزام. What does each indicate?
These are the three types of dalala wad'iyya.
2
Understand the relation to wad'
Recognize that these indications are based on the initial assignment of word to meaning (wad').
3
Note the application to majaz
The passage states that these divisions apply to metaphorical meanings as well, citing authorities like al-Sa'd and al-Sanusi.
This is a debated point.
4
Contrast with the rhetoricians' view
The passage notes that rhetoricians restrict the division, especially mutabaqa, in metaphor. Understand why they claim mutabaqa does not apply.
Their view is based on the absence of wad' in majaz.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنواع الدلالة الوضعية
بِنَفْسِهِ»؛ لِأَنَّ احْتِيَاجَهُ لِلْقَرِينَةِ لَيْسَ لِتَحْصِيلِ الدَّلَالَةِ، بَلْ لِدَفْعِ مَا عُرِضَ لَهَا مِنَ الإِيْهَامِ بِسَبَبِ تَزَاحُمِ الأَوْضَاعِ، وَيَدْخُلُ المَجَازُ بِقَوْلِهِمْ: «أَوْ بِقَرِينَةٍ».
وعلى لازمه التزام، فتكون أقسام الدَّلالة في المعنى المجازي كالحقيقي، وهو الذي حققه السَّعد في «المطوّل»، وصوَّبه السَّنوسي في شرح إيساغوجي، وجرى عليه في «المختصر، وكذا الشارح هنا؛ إلَّا أنَّ اصطلاح البيانيين إنَّما هو على الثَّاني المرجوح، وذلك أنهم قالوا: إنَّ اختلاف العبارة في وضوح الدَّلالة وخفائها على المعنى الواحد لا يتأتّى بدلالة المطابقة، وجعلوا المجاز بأقسامه ممَّا يقع به الاختلاف، فلزم أن يكون المعنى المجازي غير مدلول عليه بالمطابقة، وبنوا هذا على أنَّ المجاز غير موضوع، وأنَّ المطابقة لا تكون إلا عن وضع، فإذا أطلق اللفظ وأريد جزء معناه، أو لازمه مجازاً بقرينة، فإنَّ السَّامع العالم بالوضع يفهم مِنَ اللفظ معناه الأصلي مطابقة، وجزأه في ضمنه تضمُّناً، أو لازمه التزاماً، ثمَّ بواسطة القرينة يدرك أنَّ المراد إنَّما هو الجزء، أو اللازم مجازاً، فالجزء مفهوم في ضمن الكلِّ لكنَّه مراد لا في ضمنه، وبين فهم الجزء في ضمن الكل وإرادته لا في ضمنه بون بعيد.
والأول هو دلالة التّضمن دون الثَّاني، وليس هذا الفهم الثَّاني واحداً مِنَ الدَّلالات الثَّلاث؛ بالقرينة لا بالوضع، فالدَّلالات الثَّلاث حينئذ مستندة للوضع فقط، وكون اللفظ حقيقة أو مجازاً مستند للاستعمال فقط.
وذكر السَّيِّد في حواشي العضد: أنَّ الخلاف في المجاز هل موضوع أم لا لفظي منشؤه؟ هل الوضع تعيين اللفظ بنفسه للمعنى؟ وعليه لا وضع في المجاز أصلاً؛ لأنَّ الواضع لم يعين اللفظ بنفسه للمعنى المجازي، بل بالقرينة، فاستعماله فيه بالمناسبة لا بالوضع، أو الوضع تعيين اللفظ بإزاء المعنى، وعلى هذا ففي المجاز وضع نوعي قطعاً؛ إذ لا بدَّ مِنَ العلاقة المعتبر نوعُهَا عند الواضع، وعلى هذا الثَّاني دَرَج في «الشَّرح»، وبنحو ما عرف به الشارح «الوضع» عرَّفَهُ في جمع الجوامع.
Types of conventional indication. By itself; because its need for a context (qarina) is not to produce indication, but to remove the ambiguity that occurred to it due to the conflict of positions. And metaphor (majaz) is included by their saying: 'or with a context.' And with its necessary [implication] as iltizam, so the divisions of indication in the metaphorical meaning are like the literal, which al-Sa'd in al-Mutawwal verified, and al-Sanusi corrected in his commentary on Isaghuji, and proceeded upon in al-Mukhtasar, and likewise the commentator here; except that the terminology of the rhetoricians is only the second, weaker [view]. That is because they said: the difference of expression in clarity and obscurity of indication of one meaning cannot be achieved by mutabaqa indication, and they made metaphor with its divisions among that in which difference occurs, so it follows that the metaphorical meaning is not indicated by mutabaqa. And they built this on that metaphor is not positioned, and that mutabaqa only occurs from position. So when the word is uttered and part of its meaning or its necessary implication is intended metaphorically with a context, then the hearer who knows the position understands from the word its original meaning by mutabaqa, and its part within it by tadammun, or its necessary implication by iltizam; then by means of the context he realizes that what is intended is only the part or the necessary implication metaphorically. So the part is understood within the whole but is intended not in it, and between understanding the part within the whole and intending it not in it is a great gap. The first is tadammun indication, not the second. And this second understanding is not one of the three indications; it is by context, not by position. So the three indications are then based only on position, and the word being literal or metaphorical is based only on usage. And al-Sayyid mentioned in his Hashiya on al-‘Adud: that the dispute over whether metaphor is positioned or not is verbal, its origin being: is position the assignment of the word itself to the meaning? On this, there is no position in metaphor at all, because the positer did not assign the word itself to the metaphorical meaning, but by context, so its usage is by suitability not by position. Or is position the assignment of the word to the meaning? On this, there is generic position in metaphor absolutely, because a connection whose type is considered by the positer is necessary. And on this second [view] the Sharh proceeds, and according to what the commentator defined 'position', he defined it in Jam' al-Jawami'.
Source pages p. 451

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) that defines the three types of conventional indication (dalala wad'iyya) and addresses how they apply to both literal (haqiqa) and metaphorical (majaz) usage. The author begins by stating the types: mutabaqa (whole meaning), tadammun (part of meaning), and iltizam (necessary implication). He explains that these three divisions apply equally to literal and metaphorical meanings, citing authorities like al-Sa'd, al-Sanusi, and the author of al-Mukhtasar. However, the rhetoricians (bayan scholars) adopt a different view, holding that the threefold division does not apply to mutabaqa in the case of metaphor, because metaphor lacks a direct word-to-meaning assignment (wad'). According to them, when a word is used metaphorically, the hearer first understands the literal meaning by mutabaqa, then by context (qarina) realizes the intended metaphorical meaning. Thus the second understanding (the metaphorical one) is not considered one of the three indications; it is produced by context, not by the original wad'. The passage then clarifies that the three indications are solely based on wad', while the distinction between literal and metaphorical depends on usage (isti'mal). Finally, it presents al-Sayyid's resolution of a verbal dispute: whether 'wad'' means assignment of the word itself (in which case metaphor has no wad') or assignment of the word to the meaning (in which case there is a generic wad' for metaphor). On the second view, the Sharh proceeds, and the commentator defines 'wad'' accordingly.

Key Terms
الدلالة الوضعية
المطابقة
التضمن
الالتزام
المجاز

Checkpoint

A) They apply only to literal meanings.
B) They apply to both literal and metaphorical meanings according to the author.
C) They are all based on usage, not wad'.
D) Iltizam does not apply to metaphor.

Key Takeaway
The three conventional indications—mutabaqa, tadammun, iltizam—are based on wad' and apply to both literal and metaphorical meanings, though some scholars restrict them.

Does Majaz Have a Wad'? The Debate and Resolution
Discusses whether majaz has a wad' and resolution of the debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two positions on whether majaz has a wad' and how al-Sayyid resolves the verbal dispute.

Core Idea
The debate over majaz's wad' is a verbal dispute about the definition of wad' itself.

This passage delves into a technical debate among scholars: Is metaphor (majaz) based on a position (wad'), or does it rely solely on context? The commentator presents two views and al-Sayyid's resolution, showing that the disagreement is verbal. We will unpack this debate and its implications for understanding linguistic indication.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two views on wad' in majaz
One view: wad' is assignment of the word itself to the meaning, so no wad' in majaz. The other: wad' is assignment of the word to the meaning via a generic relationship, so there is generic wad'.
These correspond to the two definitions of wad'.
2
Understand the rhetoricians' objection
They say mutabaqa does not apply to metaphor because it lacks direct wad'. So the three indications only apply to the literal meaning.
3
See how al-Sayyid resolves the dispute as verbal
He notes that the disagreement hinges on the definition of wad'. If wad' means assigning the word itself, then majaz has no wad'; if it means assigning to the meaning, then there is a generic wad'.
This makes the dispute about the term, not the substance.
4
Recognize the commentator's stance
The commentator adopts the second view, as seen in his definition of wad' in Jam' al-Jawami'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

وذكر السَّيِّد في حواشي العضد: أنَّ الخلاف في المجاز هل موضوع أم لا لفظي منشؤه؟ هل الوضع تعيين اللفظ بنفسه للمعنى؟ وعليه لا وضع في المجاز أصلاً؛ لأنَّ الواضع لم يعين اللفظ بنفسه للمعنى المجازي، بل بالقرينة، فاستعماله فيه بالمناسبة لا بالوضع، أو الوضع تعيين اللفظ بإزاء المعنى، وعلى هذا ففي المجاز وضع نوعي قطعاً؛ إذ لا بدَّ مِنَ العلاقة المعتبر نوعُهَا عند الواضع، وعلى هذا الثَّاني دَرَج في «الشَّرح»، وبنحو ما عرف به الشارح «الوضع» عرَّفَهُ في جمع الجوامع.

And they built this on that metaphor is not positioned, and that mutabaqa only occurs from position. And al-Sayyid mentioned in his Hashiya on al-‘Adud: that the dispute over whether metaphor is positioned or not is verbal, its origin being: is position the assignment of the word itself to the meaning? On this, there is no position in metaphor at all, because the positer did not assign the word itself to the metaphorical meaning, but by context, so its usage is by suitability not by position. Or is position the assignment of the word to the meaning? On this, there is generic position in metaphor absolutely, because a connection whose type is considered by the positer is necessary. And on this second [view] the Sharh proceeds, and according to what the commentator defined 'position', he defined it in Jam' al-Jawami'.
Source pages p. 451

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) that defines the three types of conventional indication (dalala wad'iyya) and addresses how they apply to both literal (haqiqa) and metaphorical (majaz) usage. The author begins by stating the types: mutabaqa (whole meaning), tadammun (part of meaning), and iltizam (necessary implication). He explains that these three divisions apply equally to literal and metaphorical meanings, citing authorities like al-Sa'd, al-Sanusi, and the author of al-Mukhtasar. However, the rhetoricians (bayan scholars) adopt a different view, holding that the threefold division does not apply to mutabaqa in the case of metaphor, because metaphor lacks a direct word-to-meaning assignment (wad'). According to them, when a word is used metaphorically, the hearer first understands the literal meaning by mutabaqa, then by context (qarina) realizes the intended metaphorical meaning. Thus the second understanding (the metaphorical one) is not considered one of the three indications; it is produced by context, not by the original wad'. The passage then clarifies that the three indications are solely based on wad', while the distinction between literal and metaphorical depends on usage (isti'mal). Finally, it presents al-Sayyid's resolution of a verbal dispute: whether 'wad'' means assignment of the word itself (in which case metaphor has no wad') or assignment of the word to the meaning (in which case there is a generic wad' for metaphor). On the second view, the Sharh proceeds, and the commentator defines 'wad'' accordingly.

المجاز
الحقيقة
القرينة
الخلاف اللفظي

Implicative Denotation and the Three Opinions
Author defines implicative denotation and critiques three views on mental transition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three opinions on implicative denotation and evaluate the logical critique of the first opinion.

Core Idea
Implicative denotation is one understanding seen from two angles, not a mental transition.

In this passage, the commentator defines taḍammun as a word's indication of a part of its meaning and dives into a critical debate: does understanding the part require a mental transition from the whole? He presents three opinions, critiques the majority view with a logical argument, and endorses the minority view that there is no separate transition. Mastering this debate sharpens your grasp of how words convey meaning in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of taḍammun
Find where the author defines implicative denotation. Note the examples: 'four' indicating 'one', 'human' indicating 'animal'. This shows taḍammun is always a part-whole relation.
The definition is in the first few lines of the Arabic.
2
Distinguish taḍammun from muṭābaqa
The same word can indicate the whole meaning (muṭābaqa) and also a part (taḍammun). According to the author's preferred view, these are not two separate understandings but one that is called differently depending on what it is compared to.
This distinction is crucial for the second opinion.
3
Extract the three opinions
List them: (1) transition from whole to part (majority), (2) no transition at all, single understanding (author's choice), (3) transition from part to whole (dismissed). Identify the authorities cited for each.
The third opinion is not mentioned by the commentator, but the author includes it for completeness.
4
Analyze the critique of the first opinion
Follow the logical argument: if the first opinion were true, then correspondence (muṭābaqa) would not entail inclusion, but scholars state it does. This refutes the first opinion. Understand the modus tollens structure.
The argument hinges on the principle that understanding the whole does not always lead to detailed understanding of the part.
5
Synthesize the preferred opinion
The second opinion says there is no transition: the same act of understanding is called muṭābaqa when related to the whole and taḍammun when related to a part. This preserves the entailment and avoids the logical flaw of the first opinion.
This opinion is endorsed by major logicians like al-Āmidī, Ibn al-Ḥājib, and al-‘Aḍud.

وَأَشَارَ إِلَى الثَّانِي بِقَوْلِهِ : (وَجُزْئِهِ : تَضَمُّناً) أَيْ: وَدَلَالَةَ اللَّفْظِ عَلَى جُزْءِ المَعْنَى الَّذِي وُضِعَ هُوَ لَهُ؛ أَيْ: مِنْ حَيْثُ إِنَّهُ جُزْءٌ يُسَمُّونَهَا : تَضَمُّناً؛ أَيْ: دَلَالَةَ تَضَمُّنٍ كَدَلَالَةِ لَفْظِ الأَرْبَعَةِ عَلَى الوَاحِدِ رُبْعِهَا، أَوِ الاِثْنَيْنِ نِصْفِهَا، أَوِ الثَّلَاثَةِ ثَلَاثَةِ أَرْبَاعِهَا، وَكَدَلَالَةِ لَفْظِ الإِنْسَانِ عَلَى الحَيَوَانِ فَقَط، أَوْ عَلَى النَّاطِقِ فَقَطْ. وَاخْتُلِفَ: هَلْ فِيهَا انْتِقَالُ مِنْ فَهْمِ الكُلِّ إِلَى فَهْمِ الجُزْءِ وَعَلَيْهِ الجُمْهُورُ، أَوْ لَا انْتِقَالَ أَصْلاً، بَلْ لَيْسَ هُنَاكَ إِلَّا فَهُمْ وَاحِدٌ: إِنْ قِيْسَ إِلَى المَجْمُوعِ كَانَ مُطَابَقَةٌ، وَإِنْ قِيْسَ إِلَى بَعْضِ الأَجْزَاءِ كَانَ تَضَمُّناً، وَهُوَ الصَّوَابُ. قوله: (القرينةُ عينت إحدى تلك الأوضاع، فلم تكن إذن القرينة في المشترك لإيجاد تلك الدلالة؛ بمعنى: أنَّها لم تكن ثمَّ كانت، بل لتعينها بعد وجودها. وأما القرينة في المجاز فهي لإيجاد الدَّلالة في اللفظ لفقدها عند الإطلاق.) قوله: (يُسَمُّونَهَا: تَضَمُّناً) أشار به إلى أنَّ «تضمناً» في كلام الناظم عطف مفعول بفعل محذوف يدلُّ عليه «يَدْعُونَهَا». وحينئذ لا يقال: إنَّ في كلام النَّاظم عطف معمولي عاملين مختلفي اللفظ والمعنى، وهما على عطف «جُزْئِهِ» على «ما» تَضَمُّناً و في «التّسهيل»، كما توهم. قوله: (وَاخْتُلِفَ: هَلْ فِيهَا انْتِقَال) فى هذه المسألة ثلاثة أقوال: الأوّل: أنَّ فيها انتقالاً من فهم الكلِّ إلى فهم الجزء، فيكون فهم الكلِّ سابقاً وفهم الجزء متأخراً عنه، وعليه الجمهور كما في «الشَّرح»، وهو الذي في «التلخيص» و«جمع الجوامع»، والسعد في «مطوَّله»، ومعناه: أنَّ اللَّفظ إذا أطلق على الكل يفهم الكلُّ منه من غير ملاحظة أجزاء على الانفراد، وإحضار لها بالبال ثمَّ يلتفتُ الذَّهن إلى الأجزاء مفصلةً، وإِنَّما يتحقق التّضمُّن بهذا الالتفات الثاني فقط. والحاصل أن أصحاب هذا القول قالوا: إنَّ دلالة التَّضمُّن فهم الجزء بعد فهم الكل، وهذا القول ليس بصواب؛ لأنَّ القوم صرَّحوا باستلزام المطابقة في المركَّبات للتضمن، ولا مرية أنَّه لا استلزام على هذا القول؛ لأنَّه يكون المعنى هكذا: كلَّما أطلق اللفظ فهم معناه إجمالاً، وكلَّما فهم معناه إجمالاً فهم جزؤه تفصيلاً، ولا خفاء في كذب الكبرى حينئذ؛ إذ لا يلزم من فهم الكلِّ إجمالاً فهم الجزء تفصيلاً؛ لأنَّ النَّفس قد تلتفتُ إلى تفصيل الجزء بعد فهم الكل، وقد لا تلتفت، وما أجيب به عن هذا الإيراد لا ينهض. القول الثاني: أنَّ دلالة التَّضمن لا انتقال فيها أصلاً، وليس للجزء فهم يخصُّهُ، وإِنَّما هناك فهم واحد، إن قيس إلـى الـمـجـمـوع كـان مـطـابـقـة، وإن قيس إلـى أحـاد الأجزاء كـان تـضـمـنـاً، فالدَّلاله على الكلِّ لا تُغايرُ الدَّلالة على الجزء مغايرةً بالذات، بل بالإضافة والاعتبار، فهي بالنسبة إلى كلِّ المعنى مطابقةً، وبالنسبة إلى جزئه تضمُّناً، فدلالةُ التَّضمُّن على هذا القول: فهم الجزء في ضمن الكل، وحينئذ تحقق استلزام المطابقة للتضمن، وعلى هذا القول: الآمدي وابن الحاجب والعضد، والسَّعد في «حاشيته»، والسَّيِّد في «حاشية المطوّل»، وابن أبي شريف، وغيرهم، وصوّبه الشارح هنا، ودرج عليه شيخنا سيدي حمدون في «خريدته» وهو الحق ونصه: وَحُزْنُهُ تَضَمُّناً وَلَا انْتِقَال. القولُ الثَّالث: يقول بتعدد الفهم أيضاً كالأوَّل؛ إلَّا أنَّ فهم الجزء مِنَ اللَّفظ سابق على فهم الكلِّ منه، فيكون الانتقال مِنَ اللَّفظ إلى الجزء، ومِنَ الجزء إلى الكلِّ عكس القول الأوَّل، وهذا القول باطل؛ إذ لا يلزم من إطلاق اللفظ فهم جزء المعنى؛ لعدم وضعه له، ولا من فهم الجزء فهم الكلِّ؛ لأنَّ الجزء أعم، ولا يلزمُ مِن فهم الأعم فهم الأخص، ولبطلانه لم يتعرض له الشارح رحمه الله تعالى أصلاً.
And [the author] pointed to the second by his saying: 'and its part: by inclusion (taḍammun)', i.e., the word's indication of a part of the meaning for which it was coined; i.e., in terms of it being a part; they call it 'taḍammun', i.e., implicative indication, like the word 'four' indicating the one (a quarter of it), or the two (half of it), or the three (three quarters of it), and like the word 'human' indicating only 'animal' or only 'rational'. And there is disagreement: is there in it a transition from understanding the whole to understanding the part? The majority are upon it, or is there no transition at all? Rather, there is only one understanding: if measured to the whole, it is correspondence (muṭābaqa), and if measured to some parts, it is inclusion (taḍammun). And that is correct. His saying: (The contextual indicator specifies one of those coinages, so the contextual indicator in the ambiguous word (mushtarak) is not for producing that indication; meaning: it was not then based, but rather to specify it after its existence. As for the contextual indicator in the figurative (majāz), it is for producing indication in the word due to its absence when unrestricted.) His saying: (They call it 'taḍammun') he pointed by that to the fact that 'taḍammun' in the speech of the author is a direct object of an omitted verb indicated by 'they call it'. And then it is not said: that in the author's speech there is conjoining of two objects of two different verbs in wording and meaning... as in 'al-Tashīl', as was falsely supposed. His saying: (It is disagreed: is there in it a transition?) Concerning this issue there are three opinions: The first: that there is in it a transition from understanding the whole to understanding the part, so understanding the whole is prior and understanding the part subsequent to it. The majority are upon it as in 'al-Sharh', and it is the one in 'al-Talkhīṣ' and 'Jam' al-Jawāmi'', and al-Sa'd in his 'Muṭawwal'. Its meaning: that when the word is uttered upon the whole, the whole is understood from it without noticing the parts individually and bringing them to mind, then the mind turns to the parts in detail, and inclusion is only realized by this second attention only. And the conclusion: the adherents of this opinion said that implicative indication is understanding the part after understanding the whole, and this opinion is not correct; because the scholars explicitly stated that correspondence in compounds entails inclusion, and there is no doubt that there is no entailment upon this opinion; because then the meaning would be like this: whenever the word is uttered, its meaning is understood in summary, and whenever its meaning is understood in summary, its part is understood in detail, and there is no concealment in the falsehood of the major premise then; because it is not necessary from understanding the whole in summary to understand the part in detail; since the soul may turn to the detail of the part after understanding the whole, and may not turn, and what is answered by way of this objection does not suffice. The second opinion: that implicative indication has no transition at all, and the part has no understanding specific to it, but there is only one understanding: if measured to the whole, it is correspondence, and if measured to the individual parts, it is inclusion. So the indication of the whole does not differ from the indication of the part by essential difference, but by relation and consideration. So it is, in relation to the entire meaning, correspondence, and in relation to its part, inclusion. So implicative indication according to this opinion: understanding the part within the whole. And then the entailment of correspondence to inclusion is realized. Upon this opinion are: al-Āmidī, Ibn al-Ḥājib, al-‘Aḍud, al-Sa‘d in his ‘Ḥāshiya’, al-Sayyid in his ‘Ḥāshiyat al-Muṭawwal’, Ibn Abī Sharīf, and others; and the commentator approved it here, and our teacher Sayyidī Ḥamdūn followed it in his ‘Kharīda’, and it is the truth, and its text: 'and its part by inclusion and no transition.' The third opinion: says multiple understandings too like the first, except that understanding the part from the word is prior to understanding the whole from it, so the transition is from the word to the part, and from the part to the whole, opposite of the first opinion. And this opinion is false; because it is not necessary from uttering the word to understand part of the meaning due to lack of coinage for it, nor from understanding the part to understand the whole because the part is more general, and understanding the more general does not entail understanding the more specific. And due to its falsehood, the commentator (may Allah have mercy on him) did not mention it at all.
Source pages p. 452

Commentary
This passage is an extended commentary on the definition of implicative denotation (dalālatu t-taḍammun). The author begins by defining it: a word indicates a part of its meaning (e.g., 'four' implies 'one', 'human' implies 'animal'). Then he raises a dispute: in such indication, is there a mental transition from understanding the whole to understanding the part? The majority say yes, but the author sides with the minority view that there is no transition, only one act of understanding considered in two ways: as correspondence when related to the whole, and as inclusion when related to a part. He then unfolds three opinions. The first opinion (majority) holds that the whole is understood first, then the part subsequently; the author critiques it because it would entail that whenever the whole is understood, the part must be understood—which is false, as one may understand 'human' without thinking of 'animal'. The second opinion holds that there is no separate understanding of the part; the same understanding is called correspondence or inclusion depending on what it is compared to. The author endorses this view, citing many authorities. The third opinion (that part is understood before the whole) is dismissed as false. The key takeaway: implicative denotation does not require a mental step-by-step analysis; it is inherent in the single act of understanding the whole.

مُطَابَقَة

Defining Commitment Denotation
Author defines commitment denotation and explains the condition of mental concomitance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify commitment denotation in a given expression and distinguish it from other types of denotation (mutābaqah and tadammun).

Core Idea
Commitment denotation is a mental necessity, not a physical one.

This lesson unpacks the third type of word-meaning relation: commitment denotation (dalālatu l-iltizām). Unlike direct inclusion (tadammun) or full correspondence (mutābaqah), commitment denotation relies on a necessary mental link between the word's primary meaning and an external meaning. A crucial condition is that the connection must be recognized by the mind, not by external causality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the word and its primary meaning
Start with a given word (lafẓ). Ask: What is the meaning for which this word was coined (al-maʿnā al-mawḍūʿ lahu)? This is the primary meaning.
2
Find the secondary meaning that is necessary
Determine if the word also indicates another meaning that is necessarily concomitant (lāzim) to the primary meaning. The secondary meaning must be external to the primary meaning (khārij ʿanhu).
Example: 'roof' implies 'wall' – wall is not part of roof's definition but is mentally necessary.
3
Check if the accompaniment is mental
Ensure that the connection between the two meanings is recognized by the mind (luzūm dhihnī), not based on external causation or chance. Without this condition, the denotation is not commitment.
Mental concomitance means that whenever the mind thinks of the primary meaning, it necessarily thinks of the secondary meaning.
4
Verify that the secondary meaning is not explicitly included
Confirm that the secondary meaning is not part of the primary meaning (otherwise it would be tadammun). Commitment denotation is distinct because the secondary meaning lies outside the word's coinage definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَشَارَ إِلَى الثَّالِثِ بِقَوْلِهِ : (وَمَا لَزِمْ فَهُوَ : الْتِزَامُ) أَيْ : وَدَلَالَةُ اللَّفْظِ عَلَى مَعْنَى لَازِم لِلْمَعْنَى الَّذِي وُضِعَ هُوَ لَهُ خَارِجٌ عَنْهُ؛ أَيْ: مِنْ حَيْثُ إِنَّهُ لَازِمٌ لَهُ هِيَ التِزَامٌ ; أَيْ: دَلَالَةٌ الْتِزَامِ بِشَرْطِ أَنْ يَكُونَ اللُّزُومُ ذِهْنِيَّا، كَمَا أَشَارَ إِلَيْهِ. قوله: (وَدَلَالَةُ اللَّفْظِ عَلَى مَعْنَى لازم ... إلخ) هذا التَّقريرُ أنسبُ بكلام النَّاظم ممَّا قرَّره به أبو مدين ونصه : وإن دل على ما لزم فهو - أي : اللفظ - أي دلالته - التزام؛ أي: دلالة التزام، فيكون الكلام على حذف شرط ومضافين .
And [the author] pointed to the third by his saying: 'And what is necessary (lazima) then it is: commitment (iltizām).' i.e., the word's indication of a meaning necessary (lāzim) for the meaning for which it was coined, external to it; i.e., in terms of it being necessary to it, it is commitment; i.e., commitment indication, on condition that the concomitance be mental, as he pointed to. His saying: (And the word's indication of a necessary meaning... etc.) This formulation is more appropriate to the author's speech than what Abū Madīn formulated for it, and his text: 'And if it indicates what is necessary, then it - i.e., the word - i.e., its indication - is commitment; i.e., commitment indication, so the speech is upon the deletion of a condition and two additions.'
Source pages p. 453

Commentary
This passage defines the third type of denotation: commitment (iltizām). The author explains that it is the word's indication of a meaning that is necessarily concomitant (lāzim) to the meaning for which the word was coined, but that necessary meaning is external to (outside of) the coined meaning. For example, from the word 'roof' one may understand 'wall', which is a necessary concomitant but not part of the defined meaning. A crucial condition is that the concomitance (luzūm) must be mental (dhihnī), meaning the connection is recognized by the mind, not merely an external causal relation. The author then notes that the commentator's formulation is more appropriate to the author's original text than the alternative formulation by Abū Madīn, who supposedly explained it as a deletion of a condition and two additions. This precision ensures that commitment denotation is not confused with mere external association.

Key Terms
الْتِزَام
لَازِم
لُزُوم ذِهْنِيّ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What condition must hold for commitment denotation (دلالة التزام) to be valid?
A) The concomitant must be external and real
B) The concomitance must be mental (ذهني)
C) The word must be unambiguous
D) The part must be understood after the whole

Key Takeaway
Commitment denotation requires a mental necessity between the primary meaning and an external concomitant, not a causal or accidental link.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Condition of Mental Concomitance
Author emphasizes the condition of mental concomitance for commitment denotation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why mental concomitance is required for commitment denotation and evaluate whether a given relationship qualifies as mental or merely causal.

Core Idea
Mental concomitance separates genuine linguistic implication from accidental correlation.

This lesson delves deeper into the crucial condition that the concomitance must be mental (dhihnī). The author insists on this to prevent confusion between real linguistic implications and mere external causal relationships. For instance, smoke implies fire in reality, but the word 'smoke' does not commit to fire in the same way a word like 'roof' commits to 'wall'.

Guided Path
1
Recall the definition of commitment denotation
Commitment denotation is a word's indication of a meaning that is necessary (lāzim) to its primary meaning and external to it.
2
Understand the meaning of 'mental concomitance'
Mental concomitance (luzūm dhihnī) means the connection is recognized by the intellect as necessary. When you think of the primary meaning, you inevitably think of the secondary meaning.
Example: Thinking of 'father' mentally requires thinking of 'child'.
3
Distinguish mental from real concomitance
Real concomitance (luzūm khārijī) is based on external causation (e.g., smoke and fire). Mental concomitance is based on conceptual necessity, not physical occurrence.
The word 'fire' does not mentally imply 'smoke' in the same necessary way – the mind can think of fire without smoke.
4
Apply the condition to an example
Take the word 'sun' and its meaning 'light'. Is the connection mental? Yes, because the mind necessarily thinks of light when thinking of the sun. But the word 'sun' does not imply 'heat' as a necessary mental accompaniment for all persons? Possibly, but the condition requires universal mental necessity. This helps test whether a given pair qualifies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَشَارَ إِلَى الثَّالِثِ بِقَوْلِهِ : (وَمَا لَزِمْ فَهُوَ : الْتِزَامُ) أَيْ : وَدَلَالَةُ اللَّفْظِ عَلَى مَعْنَى لَازِم لِلْمَعْنَى الَّذِي وُضِعَ هُوَ لَهُ خَارِجٌ عَنْهُ؛ أَيْ: مِنْ حَيْثُ إِنَّهُ لَازِمٌ لَهُ هِيَ التِزَامٌ ; أَيْ: دَلَالَةٌ الْتِزَامِ بِشَرْطِ أَنْ يَكُونَ اللُّزُومُ ذِهْنِيَّا، كَمَا أَشَارَ إِلَيْهِ. قوله: (وَدَلَالَةُ اللَّفْظِ عَلَى مَعْنَى لازم ... إلخ) هذا التَّقريرُ أنسبُ بكلام النَّاظم ممَّا قرَّره به أبو مدين ونصه : وإن دل على ما لزم فهو - أي : اللفظ - أي دلالته - التزام؛ أي: دلالة التزام، فيكون الكلام على حذف شرط ومضافين .
And [the author] pointed to the third by his saying: 'And what is necessary (lazima) then it is: commitment (iltizām).' i.e., the word's indication of a meaning necessary (lāzim) for the meaning for which it was coined, external to it; i.e., in terms of it being necessary to it, it is commitment; i.e., commitment indication, on condition that the concomitance be mental, as he pointed to. His saying: (And the word's indication of a necessary meaning... etc.) This formulation is more appropriate to the author's speech than what Abū Madīn formulated for it, and his text: 'And if it indicates what is necessary, then it - i.e., the word - i.e., its indication - is commitment; i.e., commitment indication, so the speech is upon the deletion of a condition and two additions.'
Source pages p. 453

Commentary
This passage defines the third type of denotation: commitment (iltizām). The author explains that it is the word's indication of a meaning that is necessarily concomitant (lāzim) to the meaning for which the word was coined, but that necessary meaning is external to (outside of) the coined meaning. For example, from the word 'roof' one may understand 'wall', which is a necessary concomitant but not part of the defined meaning. A crucial condition is that the concomitance (luzūm) must be mental (dhihnī), meaning the connection is recognized by the mind, not merely an external causal relation. The author then notes that the commentator's formulation is more appropriate to the author's original text than the alternative formulation by Abū Madīn, who supposedly explained it as a deletion of a condition and two additions. This precision ensures that commitment denotation is not confused with mere external association.

Key Terms
لُزُوم ذِهْنِيّ
لَازِم
الْتِزَام

C) The word 'key' implies 'door' because a key is typically used on a door
D) The word 'book' implies 'reading' because books are meant to be read

Key Takeaway
The condition of mental concomitance ensures that commitment denotation is a matter of conceptual necessity, not empirical contingency.

Two Types of External Implication
Author divides external implication into two types and illustrates with numerical examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between external implications whose negation the mind can conceive and those it cannot, and identify numerical examples of each.

Core Idea
External implication is not always mental; some implications require reasoning to be grasped.

In this passage, the author presents a key distinction within external implication (al-lāzim fī al-khārij faqaṭ). While external implication always holds in reality, the mind's ability to conceive its absence varies. Understanding this division is crucial for distinguishing between implications that are intuitively clear and those that require demonstrative proof. The author also illustrates these types with numerical examples from perfect, deficient, and abundant numbers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two divisions
Read the passage and locate the statement that external implication is divided into two types. Note the criterion: the mind's judgment about the possibility of negating the implication.
2
Distinguish the first division
The mind can conceive the negation of the implication in the external. Example: the implication of whiteness from snow – snow could be black.
3
Distinguish the second division
The mind deems the negation impossible. Example: the implication of an effect from its cause after demonstration.
4
Understand the transition to mental implication
When the implication becomes intuitive (badīhī) so that understanding the subject immediately brings the consequence to mind, then it moves from being purely external to also mental. The author notes this is clear because mental implication is defined as immediate understanding.
5
Analyse numerical examples
The author gives three types of properties that follow necessarily from numbers: completeness (kamil), deficiency (naqis), and excess (zaid). Check the examples: 6 and 28 are perfect numbers; 8 is deficient (1+2+4=7  12). These are external implications because they hold objectively, but they are not always mentally immediate without calculation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَبِهِ تَعْلَمُ : أَنَّ اللَّازِمَ فِي الخَارِجِ فَقَطْ قِسْمَانِ : قِسْمٌ يُجَوِّزُ العَقْلُ انْتِفَاءَ لُزُومِهِ في الخارج أَيْضاً ، وَقِسْمٌ يُحِيلُهُ . نَعَمْ؛ إِذَا صَارَ اللُّزُومُ فِي الثَّانِي بَدِيهِيَّا عِنْدَ العَقْلِ بِحَيْثُ يَصِيرُ كُلَّمَا فُهِمَ المَلْزُومُ فُهِمَ لازِمُهُ، فَإِنَّهُ يَكُونُ ذِهْنِيًّا حِينَئِذٍ ، وَهُوَ ظَاهِرٌ ، وَمِمَّا هُوَ لَازِمٌ فِي الخارج مَا يَلْزَمُ بَعْضَ الأَعْدَادِ مِنَ التَّمَامِ كَالسِّتَّةِ وَكَالثَّمَانِيَةِ وَالعِشْرِينَ، وَمِنَ النُّقْصَانِ كَالثَّمَانِيَةِ وَنَحْوِهَا لِنَقْصٍ أَجْزَائِهَا مِنَ النَّصْفِ فَمَا دُونَهُ عَنْ جُمْلَتِهَا، وَمِنَ الزِّيَادَةِ كَالِاثْنَي عَشَرَ وَنَحْوِهَا لِزِيَادَةِ أَجْزَائِهَا عَلَيْهَا .
And by this you know: that the necessary [implication] in the external only is two divisions: a division which the mind allows the negation of its implication in the external also, and a division which it deems impossible. Yes; when the implication in the second becomes intuitive to the mind such that whenever the subject is understood its necessary consequence is understood, then it becomes mental at that time, and that is clear. And among that which is necessary in the external is what is necessary for some numbers: of completion, like six and twenty-eight; and of deficiency, like eight and the like because its parts from the half and below are less than its total; and of excess, like twelve and the like because its parts exceed it.
Source pages p. 456

Commentary
This passage classifies external implication (al-lāzim fī al-khārij faqaṭ) into two types. The author begins by stating: 'And by this you know: that the necessary implication in the external only is two divisions.' The first division is one where the mind can conceive the non-existence of the implication even in the external world, for example the implication of whiteness from snow or blackness from a crow. The second division is one where the mind deems the negation of the implication impossible, such as the implication of an effect from its cause after demonstration. The author then adds: if the implication in the second type becomes intuitive (badīhī) to the mind so that whenever the subject is understood its necessary consequence is also understood, then at that point it becomes a mental implication. This is clear because mental implication is defined as being understood immediately. Finally, the author gives examples from numbers: perfect numbers like 6 and 28, where the sum of proper parts equals the number; deficient numbers like 8, where the sum of parts is less than the number; and abundant numbers like 12, where the sum of parts exceeds the number. These are external implications because they hold in reality, but the mind may not grasp them without reasoning.

لَازِم
لُزُوم ذهني
تَمَام العدد

Key Takeaway
External implication can be divided into two types: one whose negation the mind can conceive, and one it cannot; when the latter becomes intuitive, it is also mental implication.

Two Types of External Implication
Division of external implication into two types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two types of external implication (lazim fi al-kharij) and identify examples from classical Islamic logic.

Core Idea
External implication is either possibly absent or necessarily present after proof; do not confuse the second with mental implication.

In this passage, the author refines the concept of necessary concomitant (lazim) by restricting it to what is external (khariji) and then dividing it into two kinds. Understanding this division helps you avoid a common logical error: thinking a rational necessity is merely mental. Pay close attention to the examples and the commentator's warning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the division
Find the two types of external implication: one where the mind deems it possible that the implication is absent (even in external reality), and one where the mind deems it impossible after proof.
The Arabic uses 'yujawwizu al-'aql intifā'a luzūmihi' and 'yuḥīluhu'.
2
Examine the first type
The first type allows the mind to imagine the absence of the implication. Example: snow on whiteness (snow could be something else, but whiteness is not necessary). The implication is external but not necessary.
3
Examine the second type
The second type is where after clear evidence, the mind cannot conceive the absence of the implication. Example: effect on its cause (a created thing necessarily implies a creator).
4
Observe the transition to mental implication
If the implication becomes so intuitive (badihi) that understanding the antecedent immediately brings the consequent to mind, it shifts to mental (dhihni) implication. This is still external in origin but becomes mentally immediate.
5
Recognize the common error
The commentator warns against mistaking the second type for mental implication. The error arises from equating 'mental' with 'rational'. The second type is rational but still external; it is only mental when it becomes intuitive without need for proof.
Source: 'قد يغلط فيه، ويعتقد أن القسم الثاني ذهني'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَبِهِ تَعْلَمُ : أَنَّ اللَّازِمَ فِي الخَارِجِ فَقَطْ قِسْمَانِ : قِسْمٌ يُجَوِّزُ العَقْلُ انْتِفَاءَ لُزُومِهِ في الخارج أَيْضاً ، وَقِسْمٌ يُحِيلُهُ . نَعَمْ؛ إِذَا صَارَ اللُّزُومُ فِي الثَّانِي بَدِيهِيَّا عِنْدَ العَقْلِ بِحَيْثُ يَصِيرُ كُلَّمَا فُهِمَ المَلْزُومُ فُهِمَ لازِمُهُ، فَإِنَّهُ يَكُونُ ذِهْنِيًّا حِينَئِذٍ ، وَهُوَ ظَاهِرٌ ، وَمِمَّا هُوَ لَازِمٌ فِي الخارج مَا يَلْزَمُ بَعْضَ الأَعْدَادِ مِنَ التَّمَامِ كَالسِّتَّةِ وَكَالثَّمَانِيَةِ وَالعِشْرِينَ، وَمِنَ النُّقْصَانِ كَالثَّمَانِيَةِ وَنَحْوِهَا لِنَقْصٍ أَجْزَائِهَا مِنِ النَّصْفِ فَمَا دُونَهُ عَنْ جُمْلَتِهَا، وَمِنَ الزِّيَادَةِ كَالِاثْنَي عَشَرَ وَنَحْوِهَا لِزِيَادَةِ أَجْزَائِهَا عَلَيْهَا . فاللازم في الخارج فقط قسمان كما أشار لذلك الشَّارح: 1 - قسم يجوز العقل انتفاء لزومه في الخارج أيضاً . 2 - وقسم يحيله . فتَنَبَّه لذلك، فإنَّه قد يغلط فيه، ويعتقد أنَّ القسم الثاني ذهني ، ومنشأ الغلط: اعتقادُ أنَّ الذهني هو العقلي. قوله: (قِسْمٌ يُجَوِّزُ العَقْلُ انْتِفَاءَ لُزُومِهِ) مثالُهُ ما تقدَّم مِن دلالة الثَّلج على البياض، والغراب على السواد. قوله: (وَقِسْمٌ يُحِيلُهُ) يعني: بعد قيام الدليل والبرهان، وذلك كما تقدم من دلالة الأثر على المؤفِّر ، فإنَّ العقل إذا أقام الدليل يقطعُ باللُّزوم بين الأثر والمؤثر، ويحيل انتفاء اللُّزوم بينهما . قوله : (مَا يَلْزَمُ بَعْضَ الأَعْدَادِ مِنَ التَّمَامِ ... إلخ) تمام العدد: مساواة أجزائه مِنَ النصف والربع والثَّمن والثلث والسُّدس ونحو ذلك لأصله ؛ بحيث إذا نظر إلى العدد وإلى أجزائه وجد أحدهما لا يزيد ولا ينقص كـ «السِّنّة» لها نصفٌ ،وهو ثلاثة وثلث وهو اثنان، وسدس وهو واحد، فإذا ضممت هذه الأجزاء وجدتها مساوية لأصل العدد بحيث لا تزيد ولا تنقص . وكذا الثَّمانية والعشرون لها نصف وهو أربعة عشر، وربع وهو سبعة، وسبع وهو أربع، ونصف سبع وربعه وهذه الأجزاء مساوية لأصل العدد. والعدد الناقص : ما نقصت أجزاؤه عن أصله والزائد : ما زادت عليه . ثمَّ الأجزاء معتبرةٌ في هذه الأوصاف ولو مع الإضافة؛ كما في : الثَّمانية والعشرين ما لم يقع تكرار، فلا يقال : الثَّمانية لها ربع النصف؛ لأنَّه مكرَّرٌ مع الثَّمن، وليس عندهم في الأحاد تام عدا السِّنّة، ولا فى العشرات تام إلَّا الثَّمانية والعشرون .
And by it you know that the necessary concomitant in external reality only is of two types: a type that the mind deems it possible for its implication to be negated in external reality as well, and a type that the mind deems it impossible. Yes, when the implication in the second becomes intuitive in the mind such that whenever the antecedent is understood, its necessary concomitant is understood, then it becomes mental at that time, and that is clear. And among what is a necessary concomitant in external reality is what accompanies some numbers from completion, like six, twenty-eight, and from deficiency, like eight and its like due to deficiency of its parts from half and below from its total, and from excess, like twelve and its like due to excess of its parts over it. So the necessary concomitant in external reality only is two types as the commentator indicated: 1 - A type that the mind deems possible the negation of its implication in external reality as well. 2 - And a type that deems it impossible. So pay attention to that, for one might err in it and believe that the second type is mental, and the source of error is the belief that the mental is the rational. His statement: (A type that the mind deems possible the negation of its implication) its example is what preceded from the signification of snow on whiteness, and the raven on blackness. His statement: (And a type that deems it impossible) he means: after the establishment of evidence and proof, and that is as preceded from the signification of the effect on the cause, for when the mind establishes the proof, it is certain of the implication between the effect and the cause, and deems impossible the negation of the implication between them. His statement: (What accompanies some numbers from completion... etc.) The completion of a number is the equality of its parts from half, quarter, eighth, third, sixth, and the like to its origin; such that when one looks at the number and its parts, he finds that one does not increase or decrease, like 'the six' has a half which is three, a third which is two, and a sixth which is one, so if you combine these parts, you find them equal to the origin of the number, such that they neither increase nor decrease. Likewise, twenty-eight has a half which is fourteen, a quarter which is seven, a seventh which is four, and a half-seventh and its quarter, and these parts are equal to the origin of the number. And the deficient number is what its parts fall short of its origin, and the abundant number is what its parts exceed it. Then the parts are considered in these descriptions even with addition, as in twenty-eight, as long as no repetition occurs, so it is not said: eight has a quarter of a half because it is repeated with the eighth, and according to them there is no complete number in the singles except six, nor in the tens except twenty-eight.
Source pages p. 457

Commentary
This passage defines the division of the necessary concomitant (lazim) that exists only in external reality (fi al-kharij faqat) into two types: one where the mind allows the absence of the implication even externally, and one where the mind deems the absence impossible. The author explains that if the implication becomes intuitive (badihi), it becomes mental, but that is a separate case. Examples: snow on whiteness (deniable) vs effect on cause (necessary after proof). Then he gives numerical examples: complete numbers where proper parts sum exactly to the number (like 6 and 28), deficient numbers (like 8) where parts sum less, abundant numbers (like 12) where parts sum more. These are external implications of numbers. The commentator warns against a common error: thinking the second type is mental when it is actually external, stemming from equating 'mental' with 'rational'. All examples are from arithmetic, illustrating logical concepts.

Key Terms
اللازِم
اللُّزوم
الذهني

Key Takeaway
The external necessary concomitant is divided into two types: one where the mind can conceive its absence, and one where it cannot after proof; this distinction prevents confusion between rational necessity and mental implication.

Are Inclusion and Implication Conventional or Rational?
Author discusses two views on the three significations' basis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two scholarly views on whether tadammun and iltizam are conventional or rational, and explain the reasoning behind each view.

Core Idea
The status of inclusion and implication hinges on whether the cause is wad' or intellect.

In this passage, al-Bajuri presents two positions on the three types of signification. The first holds that all three are conventional, with wad' playing a direct or indirect causal role. The second holds that inclusion and implication are rational, relying on the intellect alone. Understanding this debate is key to grasping how logicians and rhetoricians approach meaning differently.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Read the first line carefully. The author states that wad' is a cause for mutabaqa and a cause of a cause for tadammun and iltizam; then he presents the alternative view that the last two are rational ( 'aqliyyatan ).
The first view treats all three as conventional, the second treats the last two as purely rational.
2
Examine Yusufi's objection
Yusufi objects that wad' cannot be the complete cause for mutabaqa because it depends on knowing the convention and the word occurring in the mind. Find this objection in the Arabic.
The objection targets the causal completeness of wad'.
3
Understand the answer to Yusufi
The author answers that those two conditions (knowledge and mental occurrence) are prerequisites, not part of the causal chain. The detail about causation applies after these conditions are met.
This preserves wad' as the primary cause.
4
Analyze the second objection about 'cause of a cause'
If there is no transmission (intiqal) from the whole to the part, how can wad' be a cause of a cause? The answer: 'cause' here means implication (luzum), not temporal priority.
This resolves the apparent contradiction.
5
Distinguish the disciplinary perspectives
The passage concludes that logicians consider all three conventional because they study pure rational meanings, while rhetoricians consider the last two rational because they study meanings that involve conventional input (like metaphor).
Each field uses terminology advantageously.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وَوَجْهُ نِسْبَةِ الدَّلَالَاتِ الثَّلَاثِ إِلَى الوَضْعِ : أَنَّهُ سَبَبٌ فِي الأُولَى، وَسَبَبُ سَبَبٍ فِي الأَخِيرَتَيْنِ - وَقِيلَ : إِنَّ الأَخِيرَتَيْنِ عَقْلِيَّتَانِ ; نَظَراً إِلَى أَنَّ سَبَبَهُمَا هُوَ فَهُمُ الكُلِّ وَالمَلْزُومِ ; ) بحث فيه اليوسي في حواشي المختصر : بأنَّ الوضع ليس هو السَّببُ التَّامُّ للمطابقة؛ لتوقفها على أمرين آخرين هما : العلم بالوضع، وخطور الفظ بالبال. وأجاب بجوابين أحسنهما : أنَّه لم يعتبرهما لاشتراط الدَّلالات الثَّلَّاث فيهما، فالتفصيل المذكور إذن بعد وجودهما . قوله : (وَسَبَبُ سَبَبٍ... إلخ) هذا لا يـنـاسـب مـخـتـاره مـن أنـه أنـه لا انتقال؛ إذِ السَّبب يتقدَّم على المسبّب، وفهم الكلِّ على أنَّه لا انتقال ليس سابقاً على فهم الجزء ; اللَّهمَّ إلَّا أن يجاب: بأنَّ المراد بالصَّبيَّة اللُّزوم; أي: الوضع سبب في فهم المعنى، وفهم المعنى يلزم فهم الجزء. قوله : (وَقِيلَ : إِنَّ الأَخِيرَتَيْنِ عَقْلِيَّتَانِ على هذا القول دَرج صاحب المفتاح»، وتبعه القزويني في «التلخيص، وكذا السبكي في جمع الجوامع، وعلى القول الأول أكثر المناطقة كما في «زكريا»، وهو مبني على أنَّ المراد بـ اللفظية الوضعيّة : فهم المعنى مِنَ اللفظ الموضوع؛ كان بواسطة أم لا .ووجهُهُ : أنَّ المنطقيين يبحثون عن المعاني العقليَّة الصَّرفة التي لا دخل للوضع فيها، فناسب أن يريدوا بـ «العقليّة»: ما ليس لغير العقل مدخل فيه، وأمَّا البيانيُّون فإنَّما يبحثون عن المعاني من حيث إنَّ للوضع فيها دخلاً كالمعاني المجازيَّة، فناسب أن يريدوا بـ «العقلية»: ما لغير العقل فيه مدخل، فكل اصطلح على ما ناسب منه .
His statement: (And the aspect of attributing the three significations to convention: that it is a cause in the first, and a cause of a cause in the last two - and it was said: the last two are rational, considering that their cause is the understanding of the whole and the antecedent.) Yusufi discussed in his glosses on al-Mukhtasar: that convention is not the complete cause for correspondence (mutabaqa) because it depends on two other things: knowledge of the convention and the occurrence of the word in the mind. He answered with two answers, the better of which is that he did not consider them because the three significations are conditioned by them, so the mentioned detail is after their existence. His statement: (and a cause of a cause) this does not suit his chosen view that there is no transmission; because the cause precedes the effect, and understanding the whole as there is no transmission is not prior to understanding the part; unless it is answered that what is meant by 'causality' is implication (luzum); meaning: convention is a cause for understanding the meaning, and understanding the meaning implies understanding the part. His statement: (And it was said: the last two are rational) According to this opinion, the author of al-Miftah proceeded, followed by al-Qazwini in al-Talkhis, and likewise al-Subki in Jam' al-Jawami'. And according to the first opinion, it is the view of most logicians as in Zakariyya. It is based on that what is meant by 'verbal conventional' is understanding the meaning from the conventional word, whether by means or without. Its justification: that logicians investigate pure rational meanings in which convention has no input, so it is appropriate that they intend by 'rational' what has no input for other than intellect. As for rhetoricians, they investigate meanings insofar as convention has input into them, like metaphorical meanings, so it is appropriate that they intend by 'rational' what has input for other than intellect. So each innovated terminology according to what suits them.
Source pages p. 458

Commentary
This passage discusses the relationship between the three types of signification (mutabaqa, tadammun, iltizam) and wad' (linguistic convention). The matn presents two views: (1) wad' is a cause for the first (mutabaqa) and a cause of a cause for the last two (tadammun and iltizam); (2) the last two are purely rational. The commentator Yusufi objects that wad' is not the complete cause because it depends on knowledge of the convention and the word's occurrence in mind. The answer is that these are prior conditions. Another objection: if there is no 'transmission' (intiqal) from one meaning to another, then wad' cannot be a cause of a cause because that would imply temporal precedence. The answer is that 'cause' here means implication: wad' causes understanding the meaning, and that understanding implies understanding the part. Finally, the passage clarifies that logicians consider all three conventional because they study pure rational meanings, while rhetoricians consider the last two rational because they study meanings with conventional input (like metaphor). Thus each discipline uses terminology appropriately.

C) Because they investigate pure rational meanings.
D) Because they follow the logicians.

Key Takeaway
The attribution of tadammun and iltizam to convention or rationality depends on whether one focuses on the causal role of wad' or the involvement of intellect, with logicians and rhetoricians adopting terminologies that suit their disciplines.

Why Aspect (Haythiyya) Matters in Defining Dalala
Author explains why aspect must be considered to preserve correctness of definitions of three connotation types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a definition of dalala requires specifying the aspect to avoid violating tard or 'aks, using examples from Islamic terminology.

Core Idea
Without specifying the aspect, definitions of dalala become too broad or too narrow.

When we define the three types of connotation (mutabaqa, tadammun, iltizam), we must carefully consider from which perspective the word indicates its meaning. This is because many words in Islamic sciences have multiple related meanings. The author explains that ignoring the aspect corrupts the definition by making it either overinclusive (tard) or underinclusive ('aks). Let's see how.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle
The author states that aspect (haythiyya) must be considered in definitions of the three dalala types. Why? To preserve tard (completeness) and 'aks (exhaustiveness). Underline this in the Arabic.
2
Spot the homonyms
Two examples: 'rak'a' can mean the whole set of pillars or just the bowing; 'shams' can mean the disk or the rays. These are homonyms common in fiqh and natural theology.
Homonymy causes the same word to have multiple placements.
3
Trace the corruption of tard
If we define mutabaqa for 'rak'a' without specifying aspect, then when the word is used for the totality, its connotation to bowing becomes mutabaqa (by the other placement) as well as tadammun. This adds an unintended meaning, breaking tard.
Tard is violated when the definition includes what it shouldn't.
4
Trace the corruption of 'aks
If we define mutabaqa for 'rak'a' indicating just bowing, then the connotation to the whole is only tadammun. The definition would miss the whole connotation, breaking 'aks.
'Aks is violated when the definition excludes what it should include.
5
Apply the solution
To fix, specify the aspect in the definition: e.g., 'mutabaqa is the connotation of the word to the whole meaning for which it was placed (considering that placement).' This isolates the correct perspective.
The aspect is like a lens; it filters out other placements.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثَّانِي : لَا بُدَّ مِنِ اعْتِبَارِ الحَيثِيَّةِ فِي تَعَارِيفِ الدَّلَالَاتِ الثَّلَاثِ؛ لِيَسْلَمَ طَرْدُهَا وَعَكْسُهَا مِنَ الفَسَادِ; لِأَنَّ اللَّفْظَ قَدْ يَكُونُ مُشْتَرَكاً بَيْنَ المَعْنَى وَجُزْئِهِ كَلَفْظِ الرَّكْعَةِ المَوْضُوعِ تَارَةً لِمَجْمُوعِ أَرْكَانِهَا وَتَارَةً لِلانْحِنَاءِ المَخْصُوصِ، أَوْ بَيْنَ المَعْنَى وَلَازِمِهِ كَلَفْظِ الشَّمْسِ المَوْضُوعِ لِلْقُرْصِ تَارَةً وَلِلشُّعَاعِ أُخْرَى. فَإِذَا أُطْلِقَ لَفْظُ الرَّكْعَةِ عَلَى المَجْمُوعِ المَذْكُورِ فَدَلَالَتُهُ عَلَى المَجْمُوعِ مُطَابَقَةٌ، وَعَلَى الانْحِنَاءِ تَضَمُّنٌ. وَلَكِنْ لَوْلَا اعْتِبَارُ الحَيثِيَّةِ لَكَانَتْ دَلَالَتُهُ عَلَى الانْحِنَاءِ أَيْضاً مُطَابَقَةً بِاعْتِبَارِ الوَضْعِ الآخَرِ، فَيَفْسُدُ الطَّرْدُ. وَكَذَا لَوْ أُطْلِقَ عَلَى الانْحِنَاءِ فَقَطْ مُطَابَقَةً، فَعَلَى المَجْمُوعِ تَضَمُّنٌ، فَيَفْسُدُ العَكْسُ، فَلَا يَكُونُ التَّعْرِيفُ جَامِعاً.
The second: It is necessary to consider the aspect in the definitions of the three types of connotation so that their completeness and exhaustiveness are safe from corruption. Because the word may be homonymous between the meaning and its part, like the word 'rak'a' which is sometimes placed for the totality of its pillars and sometimes for the specific bowing; or between the meaning and its necessary concomitant, like the word 'sun' which is placed for the disk sometimes and for the rays another. So if the word 'rak'a' is used for the mentioned totality, then its connotation to the totality is by mutabaqa, and to the bowing by tadammun. But if the aspect were not considered, its connotation to the bowing would also be by mutabaqa due to the other placement, so the completeness (tard) is corrupted. And similarly if it is used for the bowing only by mutabaqa, then its connotation to the totality is by tadammun, so the exhaustiveness ('aks) is corrupted, and the definition would not be comprehensive.
Source pages p. 462

Commentary
This passage presents the second principle: in defining the three types of dalala (mutabaqa, tadammun, iltizam), one must specify the aspect (haythiyya) from which the word is considered. Without this, definitions may be too broad (tard is violated) or too narrow ('aks is violated). The author provides examples of homonymous words: 'rak'a' (prayer unit) can mean either the whole set of its pillars or just the bowing; 'shams' (sun) can mean the celestial body or its rays. If one defines mutabaqa for 'rak'a' without specifying the aspect, then when the word is used for the whole, its indication of bowing would be both tadammun (by the whole) and mutabaqa (by the separate meaning for bowing), causing the definition to include something unintended (tard corruption). Conversely, if used only for bowing, then its indication of the whole is only tadammun, while the definition of mutabaqa would miss the whole, causing 'aks corruption. Hence, the aspect must be explicitly considered to produce a sound definition.

الطرد
العكس

Necessity of the Aspect in Connotation Definitions
Author explains why the aspect (haythiyya) must be considered in definitions of the three types of connotation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the aspect (haythiyya) must be considered when distinguishing the three types of connotation (mutabaqa, tadamun, iltizam) in the definitions of terms used in Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
The aspect (haythiyya) prevents overlap by specifying from which perspective a word's connotation is considered.

When defining the three types of connotation—match, inclusion, and implication—it is crucial to specify the aspect (haythiyya). Without it, a word could fit more than one connotation, making the definitions useless. This lesson shows how classic examples from hadith illustrate the problem and why the correct view is that connotation depends on understanding, not intention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of connotation
Recall that a word's connotation can be: mutabaqa (to the entire meaning), tadamun (to a part), or iltizam (to a necessary concomitant). The author insists that their definitions require specifying the aspect to avoid overlap.
2
Understand the problem of ambiguous words
A word may be ambiguous between a whole and its part (e.g., 'rak'a': unit of prayer vs. bowing) or between a meaning and its concomitant (e.g., 'shams': sun disc vs. sunlight). The author gives hadith examples to show how these ambiguities cause confusion in connotation.
The hadith 'So bow a rak'a...' uses 'rak'a' for the entire unit, but understanding the bowing within it is by inclusion, not match.
3
See how the aspect resolves ambiguity
By specifying the aspect, we clarify that the word is considered in terms of its full meaning, its part, or its concomitant. For example, 'rak'a' when meaning the whole unit gives match connotation for the whole and inclusion for the bowing, depending on the aspect.
4
Know the refutation of the intent-based view
Some argue that connotation depends on speaker intention, so a word can only have one connotation per utterance. The author refutes this: connotation depends on understanding (fahm), so a word can simultaneously have two connotations, even if the speaker intends only one.
This is a key critical refinement in the tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثَّانِي : لَا بُدَّ مِنِ اعْتِبَارِ الحَيثِيَّةِ فِي تَعَارِيفِ الدَّلَالَاتِ الثَّلَاثِ ؛ لِيَسْلَمَ طَرْدُهَا وَعَكْسُهَا مِنَ الفَسَادِ؛ لِأَنَّ اللَّفْظَ قَدْ يَكُونُ مُشْتَرَكاً : بَيْنَ المَعْنَى وَجُزْئِهِ كَلَفْظِ الرَّكْعَةِ المَوْضُوعِ تَارَةً لِمَجْمُوعِ أَرْكَانِهَا ، وَتَارَةً لِلانْحِنَاءِ المَخْصُوص. أَوْ بَيْنَ المَعْنَى وَلَازِمِهِ كَلَفْظِ الشَّمْسِ المَوْضُوعِ لِلْقُرْصِ تَارَةً، وَلِلشُّعَاعِ أُخْرَى. فَإِذَا أُطْلِقَ لَفْظُ الرَّكْعَةِ عَلَى المَجْمُوعِ المَذْكُورِ؛ كَحَدِيثِ: «فَارْكَعْ رَكْعَةً تُوتِرُ لَكَ مَا قَدْ صَلَّيْتَ»، فَفَهِمَ الانْحِنَاءَ فِي ضِمْنِهِ دَلَالَةُ تَضَمُّنٍ، لَا مُطَابَقَةٍ؛ لِأَنَّهُ وَإِنْ صَدَقَ عَلَيْهِ فَهُمُ المَعْنَى مِنْ لَفْظِ مَوْضُوعِ لَهُ، لَكِنْ لَمْ يُفْهَمْ مِنْهُ مِنْ حَيْثُ إِنَّهُ مَوْضُوعٌ لَهُ بَلْ مِنْ حَيْثُ إِنَّهُ مَوْضُوعٌ لِلْمُرَكَّبِ المُنْدَرِجِ هُوَ فِيهِ. وَكَذَا إِذَا أُطْلِقَ لَفْظُ الشَّمْسِ عَلَى القُرْصِ؛ كَحَدِيثِ: «تَدْنُو الشَّمْسُ مِنْ رَبِّهَا»، وَفُهِمَ مِنْهُ لَازِمُهُ الَّذِي هُوَ الشُّعَاعُ، فَفَهُمُهُ مِنْهُ الْتِزَامٌ لَا مُطَابَقَةٌ، إِذْ لَمْ يُفْهَمْ مِنْهُ مِنْ حَيْثُ إِنَّهُ مَوْضُوعٌ لَهُ، بَلْ مِنْ حَيْثُ وَضْعُهُ لِمَلْزُومِهِ. لَا يُقَالُ : دَلَالَةُ اللَّفْظِ عَلَى المَعْنَى المِطَابَقِيِّ مَوْقُوفَةٌ عَلَى إِرَادَةِ المُتَكَلِّمِ، فَإِذَا أُطْلِقَ اللَّفْظُ المُشْتَرَكُ عَلَى الكُلِّ لَمْ يَدُلَّ عَلَى الجُزْءِ بِالمُطَابَقَةِ؛ لِعَدَمِ كَوْنِهِ مُرَاداً، بَلِ التَّضَمُّنِ فَقَطْ، وَقِسْ عَلَى ذَلِكَ اللَّفْظُ المُشْتَرَكُ بَيْنَ المَعْنَى وَجُزْئِهِ، وَبَيْنَ المَلْزُومِ وَلَازِمِهِ. لِأَنَّا نَقُولُ : كَوْنُ الدَّلَالَةِ تَابِعَةً لِلْإِرَادَةِ خِلَافُ التَّحْقِيقِ وَالحَقُّ أَنَّ الدَّلَالَةَ تَابِعَةٌ لِلْفَهْمِ، لَا لِلْإِرَادَةِ، فَجَازَ أَنْ يَكُونَ لِلَّفْظِ دَلَالَتَانِ بِاعْتِبَارِ كَوْنِهِ مَفْهُوماً مِنْهُ المَعْنَى؛ عَلَى أَنَّهُ تَمَامُ المَوْضُوعِ لَهُ تَارَةً، وَجُزْءاً مِنَ المَوْضُوعِ لَهُ أُخْرَى، وَإِنْ أَرَادَ المُتَكَلِّمُ أَحَدَهُمَا مُعَيَّناً، بَلْ وَإِنْ لَمْ يُرِدْ شَيْئاً.
The second point: It is necessary to consider the aspect (haythiyya) in the definitions of the three connotations, so that their universality (tard) and exclusivity ('aks) remain sound; because a word may be ambiguous: between a meaning and its part, like the word 'rak'a' which is sometimes posited for the totality of its pillars and sometimes for the specific bowing. Or between a meaning and its necessary concomitant, like the word 'shams' which is sometimes posited for the disc and sometimes for the ray. So if the word 'rak'a' is applied to the aforementioned totality, as in the hadith: 'So bow a rak'a to make odd what you have prayed,' and one understands the bowing within it, that is a connotation of inclusion (tadamun), not of match (mutabaqa); because even though it is true that one understands the meaning from the word that is posited for it, nevertheless it is not understood from it inasmuch as it is posited for it, but rather inasmuch as it is posited for the composite in which it is included. Similarly, if the word 'shams' is applied to the disc, as in the hadith: 'The sun draws near to its Lord,' and one understands from it its necessary concomitant which is the ray, then understanding it from it is a connotation of implication (iltizam), not of match; since it is not understood from it inasmuch as it is posited for it, but rather inasmuch as it is posited for its necessary precondition (malzum). It cannot be said: The connotation of a word to its matched meaning is dependent on the intention of the speaker; so if the ambiguous word is applied to the whole, it does not indicate the part by match because it is not intended, but only by inclusion. And the same applies to the ambiguous word between the meaning and its part, and between the necessary precondition and its concomitant. Because we say: The connotation being dependent on intention is contrary to verification; the truth is that connotation is dependent on understanding, not on intention. So it is possible for a word to have two connotations by virtue of the meaning being understood from it: sometimes as the complete intended meaning, and sometimes as a part of the intended meaning, even if the speaker intends one of them specifically, and even if he does not intend anything.
Source pages p. 462

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hashiya) from the commentary of al-Bajuri or al-Bannani on the definition of the three types of connotation (dalala). The author argues that in defining the three types—mutabaqa (match), tadamun (inclusion), and iltizam (implication)—it is necessary to specify the aspect (haythiyya) from which the connotation is considered. This is to ensure that the definitions are mutually exclusive and comprehensive (tard and 'aks). The need arises because words can be ambiguous between a whole and its part (like 'rak'a' meaning either the entire unit of prayer or just the bowing) or between a meaning and its necessary concomitant (like 'shams' meaning either the solar disc or the sunlight). The author illustrates with a hadith: 'So bow a rak'a...' where the word 'rak'a' is used for the entire unit, but understanding the bowing within it is by inclusion (tadamun), not by match, because the word is not originally set for just the bowing. Similarly, 'shams' in the hadith 'The sun draws near...' leads to understanding the sunlight by implication (iltizam), not by match. The author then addresses an objection: one might say that connotation depends on speaker intention, so if the speaker intends the whole, only inclusion is valid, not match. The refutation states that connotation actually depends on understanding (fahm), not intention. Therefore, a word can simultaneously have two connotations—as match for one meaning and as inclusion for another—regardless of speaker intent. This ensures the definitions remain sound when the aspect is considered.

الدلالة المطابقية

Checkpoint
Why must the aspect (haythiyya) be considered in defining the three types of connotation?
A) To ensure that definitions include all possible meanings of a word.
B) To prevent the definitions from overlapping and failing to be mutually exclusive.
C) Because the Arabic language requires it for grammatical correctness.
D) To distinguish between literal and figurative speech.

Key Takeaway
Considering the aspect (haythiyya) ensures that definitions of the three connotations are mutually exclusive and comprehensive, preventing ambiguity from overlapping types.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In the hadith 'The sun draws near to its Lord' (Muslim), identify the type of connotation when one understands sunlight from the word 'shams'. Explain why the aspect (haythiyya) is necessary to distinguish this from match connotation.
?
Consider the word 'rak'a' in a fiqh text: if it is used to mean the entire unit of prayer, but you infer the bowing, what type of connotation is that? Justify your answer using the principle that connotation depends on understanding, not intention.
