Hadd and Rasm
intermediate
10 min
Types of Conventional Signification: Mutabaqah, Tadammun, Iltizam
Defines three types of conventional signification with hadith examples and refutes intention-dependence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish between correspondence, inclusion, and concomitance signification in Islamic textual examples and explain why dalalah does not depend on speaker intention.

Core Idea
A word signifies its meaning in three ways: exactly, by part, or by necessary concomitant.

Conventional signification (al-dalalah al-wad'iyyah) is the foundation of understanding revelation and legal discourse. This lesson introduces the three types—mutabaqah (correspondence), tadammun (inclusion), and iltizam (concomitance)—using authentic hadith examples. It also establishes the crucial principle that signification is independent of the speaker's intention, a point that safeguards the objectivity of textual interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of signification
From the passage, extract the definitions of mutabaqah, tadammun, and iltizam. Note that each type describes a distinct relationship between the word (lafz) and the meaning (ma'na) based on the conventional assignment (wad').
Mutabaqah: the meaning is exactly what the word is placed for. Tadammun: a part of that meaning. Iltizam: a necessary concomitant of that meaning.
2
Analyze the hadith examples
Read the hadith 'فَارْكَعْ رَكْعَةً تُوتِرُ لَكَ مَا قَدْ صَلَّيْتَ' and the one about the sun. For each, determine why the understanding is classified as tadammun or iltizam rather than mutabaqah.
In the first, 'rak'ah' is placed for the whole unit; bowing is a part – thus tadammun. In the second, 'sun' is placed for the celestial body; the ray is a necessary concomitant – thus iltizam.
3
Examine the argument against dependence on intention
Identify the claim that dalalah is dependent on the speaker's intention and the author's refutation. The author argues that signification is based on understanding (fahm) from knowledge of wad', not on intention.
The author cites authority from al-Mutawwal and Sharh al-Mutala' to support this.
4
Understand multiple significations from a single word
When a word is mushtarak (shared) between a part and a whole or between a necessitating thing and its necessary concomitant, the mind can have multiple transitions. The author details how a word can signify both by correspondence and by inclusion/concomitance from different aspects.
This is possible because signification is not bringing the meaning into existence but merely the turning of the psyche (iltifat al-nafs).
5
Apply the concepts to another hadith
The final part gives an example: 'مَنْ أَدْرَكَ الرَّكْعَةَ فَقَدْ أَدْرَكَ السَّجْدَةَ'. Determine whether 'rak'ah' here signifies by correspondence or inclusion and why.
Here 'rak'ah' may be used for the whole unit including sajdah, so understanding the sajdah from it could be tadammun or even mutabaqah if the word is placed for the entire unit including sujud.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنواع الدلالة الوضعية كَحَدِيثِ: «فَارْكَعْ رَكْعَةً تُوتِرُ لَكَ مَا قَدْ صَلَّيْتَ» ، فَفَهُمُ الانْحِنَاءِ فِي ضِمْنِهِ دَلَالَةُ تَضَمُّنٍ ، لَا مُطَابَقَةٍ ؛ لِأَنَّهُ وَإِنْ صَدَقَ عَلَيْهِ فَهُمُ المَعْنَى مِنْ لَفْظِ مَوْضُوعِ لَهُ، لَكِنْ لَمْ يُفْهَمْ مِنْهُ مِنْ حَيْثُ إِنَّهُ مَوْضُوعٌ لَهُ بَلْ مِنْ حَيْثُ إِنَّهُ مَوْضُوعٌ لِلْمُرَكَّبِ المُنْدَرجِ هُوَ فِيهِ . وَكَذَا إِذَا أُطْلِقَ لَفْظُ الشَّمْسِ عَلَى القُرْضِ كَحَدِيثِ: «تَدْنُو الشَّمْسُ مِنْ رَبِّهَا» ، وَفُهِمَ مِنْهُ لَازِمُهُ الَّذِي هُوَ الشُّعَاعُ، فَفَهُمُهُ مِنْهُ الْتِزَامٌ لَا مُطَابَقَةٌ ، إِذْ لَمْ يُفْهَمْ مِنْهُ مِنْ حَيْثُ إِنَّهُ مَوْضُوعٌ لَهُ، بَلْ مِنْ حَيْثُ وَضْعُهُ لِمَلْزُومِهِ.
قوله: «فَارْكَعْ رَكْعَةٌ» هذا الحديث صحيح متفق عليه، أخرجه الشيخان عن ابن عمر رضي الله تعالى عنهما: أنَّ النَّبِيَّ صلى الله عليه وسلم قال: صَلَاةُ اللَّيْلِ مَثْنَى مَثْنَى، وَإِذَا أَرَدْتَ أَنْ تَنْصَرِفَ فَارْكَعْ رَكْعَةٌ تُوتِرُ لَكَ مَا قَدْ صَلَّيْتَ.
قوله: (فَفَهُمُ الانْحِنَاءِ فِي ضِمْنِهِ دَلَالَةٌ تَضَمُّنٍ، لَا مُطَابَقَةٍ) معناه: أنَّ ذلك الفهم تضمن، وإن صدق عليه أنَّه فهم تمام المعنى مِنَ اللَّفظ الموضوع له؛ إذ لم يفهم من تلك الحيثية؛ مثلاً: إذا فرضنا اللفظ مشتركاً بين الشَّيء ولازمه والمجموع المركَّب مِنَ اللَّازم والملزوم كلفظ الشَّمس للجرم، والشُّعاع والمجموع المركب منهما، فإذا ذكر لفظ «الشَّمس، ففهم منه الشُّعاع»، فذاك الفهم مطابقة إن كان مِن حيث إنَّ الشُّعاع تمام الموضوع له اللفظ، وإن صدق عليه أنَّه فهم الجزء ممَّا وضع للكلِّ أو اللازم ممَّا وضع للملزوم؛ إذ لم يفهم من تلك الحيثيَّة.
لا يقال: دلالة اللفظ على المعنى المطابقي موقوفة على إرادة المتكلم، فإذا أطلق اللفظ المشترك على الكلِّ لم يدلَّ على الجزء بالمطابقة؛ لعدم كونه مراداً، بل التَّضمن فقط، وقس على ذلك اللفظ المشترك بين المعنى وجزئه، وبين الملزوم ولازمه.
لأنَّا نقول: كون الدَّلالة تابعة للإرادة خلاف التحقيق والحقُّ أنَّ الدَّلالة تابعة للفهم، لا للإرادة، فجاز أن يكون للفظ دلالتان باعتبار كونه مفهوماً منه المعنى؛ على أنَّه تمام الموضوع له تارة، وجزءاً مِنَ الموضوع له أخرى، وإن أراد المتكلم أحدهما معيَّناً، بل وإن لم يرد شيئاً، فإنَّا قاطعون بأنَّا إذا سمعنا اللفظ وكنَّا عالمين بالوضع، نتعقل معناه؛ سواء أراده اللافظ أم لا، ولا نعني بـ «الدَّلالة» سوى هذا، فالقول بأنَّ الدَّلالة تابعة للإرادة باطل، كما في «المطوّل» تبعاً لشرح المطالع.
والحاصلُ: أَنَّ اللَّفظ إذا كان مشتركاً بين الجزء والكل، وأطلق على الكلِّ، انتقل الذهن إلى الجزء لكونه موضوعاً له، وإلى الكلِّ أيضاً، لكن انتقاله إلى الكلِّ متضمن انتقاله إلى الجزء إجمالاً، فله إلى الجزء انتقالان تفصيلي قصدي بسبب كونه موضوعاً له، وإجمالي ضمني بسبب كونه جزءاً من الموضوع له، فله عليه دلالتان.
وكذا في اللفظ المشترك بين اللازم والملزوم ينتقل الذهن منه إلى اللازم ابتداء لكونه موضوعاً له، وبتوسط الملزوم أيضاً، وكذا في التَّضمُّن والالتزام إذا أطلق المشترك على الجزء دلّ عليه مطابقةً، وتضمُّناً، أو على اللازم دلَّ عليه مطابقة والتزاماً، هذا ملخص ما حققه السَّيِّد في حواشي شرح المطالع.
فإن قيل: يلزم أن يدلّ اللفظ على الجزء أو اللازم في حالةٍ واحدةٍ دلالتين من جهتين مختلفتين.
قلنا: لا امتناع في ذلك؛ إلا لو كان معنى دلالة اللفظ على المعنى تحصيله وإيجاده، فيلزم من ذلك تحصيل المحصل وإيجاد الموجد، وهو محال، لكنَّا لا نقول بذلك، بل معناها التفات النفس إلى المعنى عند إطلاق اللفظ أو تخيله، وإذا علم أنَّ اللفظ موضوع لمعان متعدّدة، وكانت تلك المعاني مرتسمة في العقل، فإذا أطلق هذا اللفظ انتقل الذهن منه إلى جميع تلك المعاني، ولاحظ كلَّ واحدٍ منها.
قوله: (وَإِذَا أُطْلِقَ لَفْظُ الرَّكْعَةِ) من الأوّل: قوله عليه الصَّلاة والسَّلام: «مَنْ أَدْرَكَ الرَّكْعَةَ فَقَدْ أَدْرَكَ السَّجْدَةَ»
Types of conventional signification: Like the hadith: 'Then bow a rak'ah to make what you have prayed odd-numbered.' So understanding the bowing within it is a signification of inclusion, not correspondence; because even if it is true that it is understanding the meaning from the word that is placed for it, it is not understood from it in terms of being placed for it, but rather in terms of being placed for the compound in which it is included. Likewise, when the word 'sun' is applied to the disk, as in the hadith: 'The sun draws near to its Lord,' and its necessary concomitant, which is the ray, is understood from it, then understanding it from it is concomitance, not correspondence, since it is not understood from it in terms of being placed for it, but rather in terms of its being placed for its necessary concomitant.
His statement: 'Then bow a rak'ah' — this hadith is authentic and agreed upon, narrated by the two shaykhs from Ibn 'Umar, may Allah be pleased with them both: that the Prophet, peace be upon him, said: 'Night prayer is two by two, and when you wish to leave, then bow a rak'ah to make what you have prayed odd-numbered.'
His statement: (So understanding the bowing within it is a signification of inclusion, not correspondence) — its meaning is that that understanding is inclusion, even if it is true that it is understanding the complete meaning from the word placed for it; since it is not understood from that perspective. For example: if we suppose the word is shared between the thing and its necessary concomitant and the composite compound of the necessary concomitant and the necessitating thing, like the word 'sun' for the body, the ray, and the composite of both, then if the word 'sun' is mentioned and the ray is understood from it, that understanding is correspondence if it is from the perspective that the ray is the complete meaning placed for the word, even if it is true that it is understanding the part of what is placed for the whole or the necessary concomitant of what is placed for the necessitating thing; since it is not understood from that perspective.
It cannot be said: The signification of the word for the meaning by correspondence is dependent on the intention of the speaker. So if the shared word is applied to the whole, it does not signify the part by correspondence due to its not being intended, but only by inclusion. And analogize to that the word shared between a meaning and its part, and between the necessitating thing and its necessary concomitant.
Because we say: The fact that signification is dependent on intention is contrary to verification. The truth is that signification is dependent on understanding, not on intention. So it is permissible for the word to have two significations by virtue of the meaning being understood from it: sometimes as the complete subject matter, and sometimes as a part of the subject matter, even if the speaker intends one of them specifically, and even if he does not intend anything. For we are certain that when we hear the word and are knowledgeable of the conventional usage, we conceive its meaning, whether the speaker intends it or not. And we do not mean by 'signification' anything other than this. So the statement that signification is dependent on intention is false, as in al-Mutawwal, following Sharh al-Mutala'.
The conclusion: When the word is shared between the part and the whole, and is applied to the whole, the mind moves to the part because it is placed for it, and also to the whole, but its transition to the whole includes its transition to the part in summary. So it has two transitions to the part: a detailed intentional one because of being placed for it, and a summary implicit one because of being part of the subject matter. So it has two significations upon it.
Similarly, in the word shared between the necessary concomitant and the necessitating thing, the mind moves from it to the necessary concomitant initially because it is placed for it, and also by mediation of the necessitating thing. Similarly in inclusion and concomitance, if the shared word is applied to the part, it signifies it by correspondence and inclusion, or if applied to the necessary concomitant, it signifies it by correspondence and concomitance. This is the summary of what al-Sayyid verified in his glosses on Sharh al-Mutala'.
If it is said: It would necessitate that the word signifies the part or the necessary concomitant in one state with two significations from two different aspects.
We say: There is no impossibility in that; unless the meaning of the word's signification of the meaning is its obtaining and bringing it into existence, then that would necessitate obtaining the obtainer and bringing the bringer into existence, which is impossible. But we do not say that; rather its meaning is the turning of the psyche toward the meaning when the word is uttered or imagined. And if it is known that the word is placed for multiple meanings, and those meanings are imprinted in the mind, then when this word is uttered, the mind moves from it to all those meanings and regards each one of them.
His statement: (And when the word 'rak'ah' is applied) — from the first: his statement, peace and blessings be upon him: 'Whoever catches the rak'ah has caught the sajdah.'
Source pages p. 463

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) explaining the three types of conventional signification (al-dalalah al-wad'iyyah): correspondence (mutabaqah), inclusion (tadammun), and concomitance (iltizam). The author begins by defining each type with examples from hadith. The hadith 'Fark'a' rak'ah' (then bow a rak'ah) is used to illustrate inclusion: the word 'rak'ah' signifies the entire unit of prayer, but understanding the bowing (inḥinā') from it is inclusion, not correspondence, because the bowing is a part of what the word is placed for. Similarly, the hadith 'The sun draws near to its Lord' illustrates concomitance: the word 'sun' is placed for the celestial body, but understanding the ray from it is concomitance, because the ray is a necessary concomitant of the sun, not the direct subject matter.

The author then clarifies that the same word can have multiple significations depending on perspective. He rejects the claim that signification depends on the speaker's intention. Instead, he asserts that signification depends on understanding (fahm) derived from knowledge of the conventional usage (wad'). This is verified by the fact that when we hear a word and know its meaning, we conceive it regardless of the speaker's intent. He cites the authority of al-Mutawwal and Sharh al-Mutala'.

The discussion continues with the case of a shared word (mushtarak) that is placed for both a part and a whole or a necessary concomitant and its necessitating thing. In such cases, the mind has multiple transitions: a detailed intentional transition to the part because it is directly placed for it, and a summary implicit transition because the part is included in the whole. Thus, the word can have two significations simultaneously from different aspects.

Finally, the author addresses an objection: that a word cannot signify two meanings in one state. He responds that this is only impossible if signification were the act of bringing the meaning into existence; but signification is merely the turning of the psyche (iltifat al-nafs) toward the meaning. When the word is known to be placed for multiple meanings, the mind moves to all of them. He then provides an additional example: the hadith 'Whoever catches the rak'ah has caught the sajdah,' where 'rak'ah' is used to signify the entire unit including the sajdah, thus combining inclusion and correspondence.

Checkpoint
In the hadith 'فَارْكَعْ رَكْعَةً تُوتِرُ لَكَ مَا قَدْ صَلَّيْتَ' (then bow a rak'ah to make what you have prayed odd-numbered), understanding the bowing (الانْحِنَاءِ) from the word 'رَكْعَةً' is an example of which type of conventional signification?
A) مطابقة (Correspondence)
B) تضمن (Inclusion)
C) التزام (Concomitance)
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
Conventional signification has three types—correspondence, inclusion, and concomitance—and it is based on understanding from the conventional assignment, not on the speaker's intention.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the type of signification for the word 'salat' in the hadith: 'الصلاة عماد الدين' (prayer is the pillar of religion). Is it mutabaqah, tadammun, or iltizam? Explain your reasoning.
?
Analyze the phrase 'يد الله فوق أيديهم' (the hand of Allah is above their hands). Determine whether the signification of 'hand' is mutabaqah (literal hand) or iltizam (metaphorical power and authority), and argue why this distinction matters in 'aqidah.

Division of Words and the Purpose of Verbal Investigations
Bannani opens the section on expressions, explaining the division of words and its purpose.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a word is compound or single according to Islamic logic, and explain why this division is made.

Core Idea
The division of words into compound and single is the first step toward understanding universal meanings in definitions.

In this opening of the section on verbal investigations (mabahith al-alfaz), al-Bannani reminds us that the only signifier considered in logic is the word that signifies by convention. He then introduces the fundamental division of these words into compound and single. This division is not arbitrary—it serves a specific purpose: to lead us into the discussion of universals (kulliyat), which are the building blocks of definitions. Understanding this division is essential for any student of logic, because it sets the stage for analyzing the structure of terms in propositions and syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scope of the division
The passage states that the division applies only to words that signify by convention (lafz dal bil-wad'). Why is this restriction important? What types of words are excluded?
Natural signs like smoke indicating fire are not included.
2
Extract the two categories
The passage gives two categories: compound (murakkab) and single (mufrad). What is the defining characteristic of each? Compound words have parts that signify something (e.g., 'abdullah' where 'abd' and 'Allah' signify). Single words have parts that do not signify anything (e.g., 'rajul' where 'r', 'j', 'l' alone have no meaning).
3
Understand the purpose
Why does the author make this division? The passage says it is to lead to the discussion of universals, the principles of definitions. How does this division help in that progression? By first understanding the building blocks of terms (words), we can then analyze their meanings (universals) and finally how they combine to form definitions.
This is a standard logical progression from words to meanings.
4
Recognize the debate over the number of categories
The passage mentions that the correct view is binary, but some propose a ternary division. What would the third category be? Possibly a 'single word that can be broken down'? The passage defines single as that whose part does not signify, so a ternary view might add a category like 'composite' between compound and single.
This debate shows the nuance in logical classifications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ فِي مَبَاحِثِ الأَلْفَاظِ
شَرْحُ البَنَانِيِّ عَلَى السَّلَّمِ المُنَوَّقِ
قَدْ تَقَدَّمَ أَنَّ المُعْتَبَرَ عِنْدَهُمْ مِنْ أَقْسَامِ الدَّالّ إِنَّمَا هُوَ اللَّفْظُ الدَّالُ بِالوَضْعِ، وَأَرَادَ فِي هَذَا الفَصْلِ ذِكْرَ مَبَاحِثِهِ مِنْ حَيْثُ كَوْنُهُ مُرَكَّبَاً أَوْ مُفْرَداً ، وَكَوْنُ المُفْرَدِ كُلِّيَّا أَو جُزْئِيًّا ، أَوْ غَيْرُ ذَلِكَ ; لِيَنْسَاقَ بِذَلِكَ إِلَى الكَلَامِ عَلَى الكُلِّيَّاتِ الَّتِي هِيَ مَبَادِئُ التَّعْرِيفَاتِ .
وَمَبَاحِثُ جَمْعُ: «مَبْحَث»، اسْمُ مَصْدَرٍ بِمَعْنَى : «البَحْثِ»، وَهُوَ: «التَّفْتِيسُ وَالِاسْتِقْصَاءُ .
قَالَ :
(٢٦) مُسْتَعْمَلُ الأَلْفَاظِ حَيْثُ يُوجَدُ إِمَّا مُرَكَّبٌ، وَإِمَّا مُفْرَدُ
قَسَّمَ اللَّفْظَ إِلَى قِسْمَيْنِ : مُرَكَّبٍ، وَمُفْرَدٍ ; وَمُرَادُهُ : تَقْسِيمُ اللَّفْظِ الدَّالّ بِالوَضْعِ، فَـ «أَلْ» فِي الأَلْفَاظِ لِلْعَهْدِ، وَالمَعْهُودُ : اللَّفْظُ الدَّالُ بِالوَضْعِ.
قوله : (اسْمُ مَصْدَرٍ ) أي : لأنَّه مبدوءٌ بميم زائدة لغير المفاعلة، وما كان كذلك فهو اسم مصدر، ويصح أن يكون اسم مكان ك: «مَقْعَدٍ، وَمَقَاعِد»; أي: محلَّ البحث.
ثمَّ إنَّه كان من حقِّ النَّاظم أن يقدّم هذه التَّرجمة ويأتي بها محل الباب السابق، ويستغني عن أنواع الدَّلالة، ويعبر بـ (باب) فيقولُ: بَابٌ فِي مَبَاحِثِ اللَّفْظِ ; لأنَّ الباب السابق - أعني : «أَنْوَاعُ الدَّلَالَةِ» - مِن جملة المباحث اللفظية، وقد أشار لما ذكرنا الشيخ قدورة. قوله : (إِمَّا مُرَكَّبٌ، وَإِمَّا مُفْرَدُ) دَرَجَ النَّاظِمُ فِي تَقْسِيمِهِ اللَّفْظَ المُسْتَعْمَلَ : إِلَى مُفْرَدٍ وَإِلَى مُرَكَّبٍ عَلَى مَذْهَبِ مَنْ يَقُولُ : إِنَّ القِسْمَةَ ثُنَائِيَّةٌ ، وَهُوَ الصَّحِيحُ، قَالَ صَاحِبُ القَادِرِيَّةِ : اللَّفْظُ قِسْمَانِ لَدَيْهِمْ يُعْرَفُ مُفْرَدٌ أَوْ مُرَكَّبٌ مُؤَلَّفُ
وَمِنْهُمْ مَنْ جَعَلَ القِسْمَةَ ثَلَاثِيَّةً :
١ - مُفْرَد، وَهُوَ: «الَّذِي لا يَدُلُّ جُزْؤُهُ عَلَى شَيْءٍ».
Chapter on the Investigations of Expressions. [This is] al-Bannani's commentary on al-Sullam al-Munawwaq. It has preceded that what is considered by them among the divisions of the signifier is only the word signifying by convention. And he intended in this chapter to mention its investigations regarding its being compound or single, and the single being universal or particular, or other than that; in order to lead by that to the discussion of the universals which are the principles of definitions. 'Mabahith' is the plural of 'mabhath', a noun of source meaning 'research', which is 'search and thorough investigation'. He said: (26) 'The words in use, wherever found, are either compound or single.' He divided the word into two divisions: compound and single; and his intent is the division of the word signifying by convention, so 'al-' in 'al-alfaz' is for the known [previous mention], and the known is the word signifying by convention. His statement: (a noun of source) i.e., because it begins with an extra meem not for mutual verbal action, and whatever is like that is a noun of source, and it is permissible for it to be a noun of place like 'maq'ad' and 'maqa'ad'; i.e., place of research. Then, it was the right of the versifier to put this translation in place of the previous chapter and dispense with the types of signification, and to express with 'chapter' saying 'Chapter on the Investigations of the Word', because the previous chapter - I mean 'Types of Signification' - is among the verbal investigations, and al-Shaykh Qadura indicated what we mentioned. His statement: (either compound or single) the versifier proceeded in his division of the word in use to single and compound according to the view of those who say the division is binary, and it is the correct one. The author of al-Qadiriyya said: 'The word is two divisions among them, known: single or compound composed.' And among them is one who made the division ternary: 1 - single, which is 'that whose part does not signify anything.'
Source pages p. 465

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory opening to the section on verbal investigations (mabahith al-alfaz) in al-Bannani's commentary. It begins by restating a key principle: the only signifier considered in logic is the word (lafz) that signifies by convention (wad'). The author then announces the plan for this section: to examine words from the perspective of being compound (murakkab) or single (mufrad), and within the single, whether it is universal (kulli) or particular (juz'i), etc. The ultimate aim is to lead into the discussion of the five universals (kulliyat), which are the principles of definitions (mabadi' al-ta'rifat). This is a standard logical progression: first the vehicle of meaning (words), then the meanings themselves (universals). Al-Bannani then glosses the word 'mabahith' (investigations) as a noun of source meaning 'research' (bahth) and 'thorough investigation' (taftish wa-istiqsa'). He then quotes the matn line (26): 'The words in use, wherever found, are either compound or single.' The commentary explains that this division applies only to conventional words, as indicated by the definite article 'al-' in 'al-alfaz' being for previous reference (al-'ahd) to the conventional word mentioned earlier. The passage also includes a meta-commentary: al-Bannani notes that a proper arrangement would have placed this chapter before the previous one on types of signification, and would have used 'bab' (chapter) instead of 'fasl' (section), because the previous chapter is itself part of verbal investigations. He cites al-Shaykh Qadura for this observation. The division of words is binary (compound/single) according to the correct view, as stated by the author of al-Qadiriyya. A minority made it ternary, including a definition of 'single' as that whose part does not signify anything (e.g., 'man' as a single word, where 'm' and 'n' alone have no meaning). This distinction is foundational for later discussions of terms in categorical propositions and syllogisms.

مركب
مفرد
كلي
جزئي

Classification of Expressions into Simple and Compound
Defines simple and compound expressions, presents twofold and threefold divisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between mufrad and murakkab expressions according to the twofold division, and identify the additional category in the threefold division.

Core Idea
Simple and compound expressions are distinguished by whether their parts indicate part of the meaning.

In this lesson, we examine the foundational classification of conventional linguistic expressions into simple (mufrad) and compound (murakkab), as established in Islamic logic. This classification is essential for understanding definitions and the theory of universals. The commentator explains both a twofold and a threefold division, clarifying the precise definitions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the scope
The division applies only to expressions that signify by convention (al-lafẓ al-dāll bi al-waḍ‘). Note that other types of signifiers (e.g., natural signs) are excluded.
2
Learn the twofold division
The matn states: expressions are either compound (murakkab) or simple (mufrad). Compound: its part indicates a part of its meaning. Simple: its part does not indicate a part of its meaning.
This is the correct division according to the commentator.
3
Examine the threefold division
Some scholars add a third category: mu’allaf (composed). In this division: mufrad = part indicates nothing; murakkab = part indicates not a part of meaning; mu’allaf = part indicates a part of meaning (same as murakkab in the twofold).
The threefold division redefines murakkab and introduces a new term.
4
Apply definitions to examples
Apply the definitions: 'Zaydun' is mufrad because its letters do not indicate part of its meaning. 'Zaydun qā'imun' is murakkab because 'Zaydun' and 'qā'imun' each contribute to the overall meaning.
Use Islamic examples to internalize.

قَدْ تَقَدَّمَ أَنَّ المُعْتَبَرَ عِنْدَهُمْ مِنْ أَقْسَامِ الدَّالّ إِنَّمَا هُوَ اللَّفْظُ الدَّالُ بِالوَضْعِ، وَأَرَادَ فِي هَذَا الفَصْلِ ذِكْرَ مَبَاحِثِهِ مِنْ حَيْثُ كَوْنُهُ مُرَكَّبَاً أَوْ مُفْرَداً ، وَكَوْنُ المُفْرَدِ كُلِّيَّا أَو جُزْئِيًّا ، أَوْ غَيْرُ ذَلِكَ ; لِيَنْسَاقَ بِذَلِكَ إِلَى الكَلَامِ عَلَى الكُلِّيَّاتِ الَّتِي هِيَ مَبَادِئُ التَّعْرِيفَاتِ . وَمَبَاحِثُ جَمْعُ: «مَبْحَث»، اسْمُ مَصْدَرٍ بِمَعْنَى : «البَحْثِ»، وَهُوَ: «التَّفْتِيسُ وَالِاسْتِقْصَاءُ . قَالَ : (٢٦) مُسْتَعْمَلُ الأَلْفَاظِ حَيْثُ يُوجَدُ إِمَّا مُرَكَّبٌ، وَإِمَّا مُفْرَدُ قَسَّمَ اللَّفْظَ إِلَى قِسْمَيْنِ : مُرَكَّبٍ، وَمُفْرَدٍ ; وَمُرَادُهُ : تَقْسِيمُ اللَّفْظِ الدَّالّ بِالوَضْعِ، فَـ «أَلْ» فِي الأَلْفَاظِ لِلْعَهْدِ، وَالمَعْهُودُ : اللَّفْظُ الدَّالُ بِالوَضْعِ . (فَصْلٌ فِي مَبَاحِثِ الْأَلْفَاظِ) قوله : (اسْمُ مَصْدَرٍ ) أي : لأنَّه مبدوءٌ بميم زائدة لغير المفاعلة، وما كان كذلك فهو اسم مصدر، ويصح أن يكون اسم مكان ك: «مَقْعَدٍ، وَمَقَاعِد»; أي: محلَّ البحث. ثمَّ إنَّه كان من حقِّ النَّاظم أن يقدّم هذه التَّرجمة ويأتي بها محل الباب السابق، ويستغني عن أنواع الدَّلالة، ويعبر بـ (باب) فيقولُ: بَابٌ فِي مَبَاحِثِ اللَّفْظِ» ; لأنَّ الباب السابق - أعني : «أَنْوَاعُ الدَّلَالَةِ» - مِن جملة المباحث اللفظية، وقد أشار لما ذكرنا الشيخ قدورة. قوله : (إِمَّا مُرَكَّبٌ، وَإِمَّا مُفْرَدُ درج النَّاظم في تقسيمه اللفظ المستعمل : إلى مفرد وإلى مركَّب على مذهب من يقول : إنَّ القسمة ثنائيَّةٌ ، وهو الصَّحيح، قال صاحب القادرية : اللَّفْظُ قِسْمَانِ لَدَيْهُمْ يُعْرَفُ مُفْرَد أَوْ مُرَكَّبٌ مُؤَلَّف ومنهم مَن جعل القسمة ثلاثية : ١ - مفرد، وهو: «الَّذي لا يدلُّ جزؤه على شيء». ٢ - ومركّب ، وهو : «ما يدلُّ جزؤه لا على جزء معناه». ٣ - ومؤلَّفٌ ، وهو : «ما يدلُّ جزؤه على جزء معناه». ثُمَّ أَشَارَ إِلَى تَعْرِيفِ كُلِّ وَاحِدٍ مِنَ القِسْمَيْنِ، فَقَالَ: (٢٧) فَأَوَّلٌ : مَا دَلَّ جُزْؤُهُ عَلَى جُزُءٍ مَعْنَاهُ، بِعَكْسِ مَاتَلَا
It has preceded that the considered among them from the divisions of the signifier is only the expression signifying by convention, and he intended in this chapter to mention its discussions in terms of being compound or simple, and the simple being universal or particular, or other than that; so that by that one may proceed to speak about the universals which are the principles of definitions. 'Mabāhith' is the plural of 'mabḥath', a verbal noun meaning 'research', which is 'searching and exhaustive inquiry'. He said: (26) 'The used expressions wherever they are found are either compound or simple.' He divided expression into two divisions: compound and simple; his intent is the division of the expression signifying by convention, so 'al' in 'al-alphāẓ' is for the covenant, and the covenanted is the expression signifying by convention. (Chapter on Discussions of Expressions) His words: 'a verbal noun' meaning: because it begins with an extra mīm not for the pattern of mutual action, and whatever is like that is a verbal noun, and it is correct that it be a noun of place like 'maq'ad' and 'maqā‘id', meaning 'place of research'. Then it was the right of the author to advance this heading and bring it in the place of the previous chapter, and dispense with the types of indication, and express with 'bāb' saying: 'A chapter on discussions of expression'; because the previous chapter - I mean 'Types of Indication' - is among the verbal discussions, and Sheikh Qaddūra indicated what we mentioned. His words: 'Either compound or simple' The author proceeded in his division of the used expression into simple and compound according to the doctrine of those who say the division is binary, and it is correct. The author of al-Qādiriyya said: 'Expression is two divisions according to them known: simple or compound or composed.' And among them are those who made the division threefold: 1 - Simple, which is 'that whose part does not indicate anything'. 2 - Compound, which is 'that whose part indicates not a part of its meaning'. 3 - Composed, which is 'that whose part indicates a part of its meaning'. Then he indicated the definition of each of the two divisions, and said: (27) 'The first is that whose part indicates a part of its meaning, opposite of what follows.'
Source pages p. 466

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory one from the commentary on the matn. It begins by reminding the reader that the focus is on conventional expressions (al-lafẓ al-dāll bi al-waḍ‘). The author then introduces the chapter on discussions of expressions (mabāḥith al-alfāẓ), explaining that the purpose is to study expressions from the perspectives of being compound or simple, and the simple being universal or particular, leading to the discussion of the five universals which are the foundations of definitions. The term 'mabāḥith' is defined as a plural of 'mabḥath', a verbal noun meaning research or exhaustive inquiry. The matn line (26) is quoted: 'Used expressions are either compound or simple.' The commentator explains that this division applies specifically to conventional expressions, and the definite article 'al' in 'al-alfāẓ' refers back to what was previously mentioned. There is a digression about the title: the commentator suggests it would have been better to place this chapter before the previous one on types of indication, as that also falls under verbal discussions. Then the commentary delves into the division itself: the author (al-nāẓim) divides used expressions into simple (mufrad) and compound (murakkab) following the binary division, which is considered correct. A verse from the author of al-Qādiriyya is cited to support this. However, some scholars adopt a threefold division: (1) simple (mufrad) defined as that whose part does not indicate anything, (2) compound (murakkab) defined as that whose part indicates not a part of its meaning, and (3) composed (mu’allaf) defined as that whose part indicates a part of its meaning. This threefold division is presented without endorsement. Finally, the matn line (27) is quoted, which defines the first division (i.e., compound in the binary system) as that whose part indicates a part of its meaning, with the opposite applying to the second (simple). The passage thus clarifies the two competing classifications and the core definitions.

Compound Signification: Convention vs. Reason
Debates whether compound expressions signify by convention or by reason.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two positions on the signification of compound expressions (conventional by type vs. rational) and state the evidence for each side.

Core Idea
The meaning of a compound is either conventional by type or inherently rational.

This passage from al-Bajuri's commentary on al-Bannani plunges into a pivotal debate in Islamic logic and usul al-fiqh: how do compound expressions (al-murakkab) convey meaning? While all agree that individual words are conventional, the dispute centers on the additional compositional meaning that arises from combining words with specific syntax—does this meaning depend on prior convention or is it grasped by reason alone? Understanding this debate clarifies the foundations of linguistic analysis in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Read the passage and note the two opposing views: (1) compound signification is conventional by type (wad‘iyya bi al-naw‘) – the verified position; (2) it is rational (‘aqliyya). Mark the scholars who support each side.
Al-Bajuri sides with the conventionalists, listing al-Damāmīnī, Ibn ‘Arafa, al-Subkī, and al-Qarāfī. The rationalists include Ibn al-Hājib, al-Sakkākī, Ibn Mālik, and al-Azharī.
2
Understand the agreed foundation
Notice that both camps agree on the conventionality of individual words. The disagreement starts when we consider the composite meaning that arises from the specific arrangement and inflection—for example, the attribution in "Zaydun qā'imun" (Zayd is standing).
This third meaning (attribution) is the point of dispute.
3
Examine the conventionalist evidence
The conventionalists argue that the Arabs restricted composition as they restricted singulars. We cannot arbitrarily invent new compound patterns; we must follow what has been heard. They give examples: the genitive construct cannot be fronted, nor the subject before the verb in ways unattested.
This shows that compound meaning depends on convention ('hearing').
4
Examine the rationalist evidence
The rationalists claim that once one knows the individual words and hears the inflected compound, the mind necessarily grasps the compositional meaning without needing prior exposure. For them, the structure is rationally self-evident.
They argue that if it were conventional, compounds would require hearing just like singulars, but we understand novel compounds naturally.
5
Assess the outcome and reconciliation
The commentator affirms the conventional-by-type view as the verified position, but notes that al-Fāsī suggested the two views might converge upon deeper investigation. Reflect on why this debate matters for linguistic methodology in Islam.
The debate has implications for how we interpret Qur'anic and prophetic statements, especially commands and prohibitions.

فَيُؤخَذُ مِنْهُ : أَنَّ دَلَالَةَ المُرَكَّبِ وَضْعِيَّةٌ بِالنَّوْعِ، وَهُوَ التَّحْقِيقُ كَمَا بَيْنَهُ الدَّمَامِينِيُّ وَغَيْرُهُ، خِلَافاً لِمَنْ قَالَ: «إِنَّهَا عَقْلِيَّةٌ». قوله : (فَيُؤخَذُ مِنْهُ : أَنَّ دَلَالَةَ المُرَكَّبِ) يعني : على المعنى التركيبي ; أعني : الحادث بالتركيب; إذ قولك : زَيْدٌ قَائِمٌ» يدلُّ زَيْد» منه على الذات المعيَّنة، و«قائم» على ذاتٍ مَّا متّصفة بالقيام، وهاتان الدَّلالتان وضعيّتان قطعاً، ولا خلاف في ذلك، ومجموع اللفظين بالإعراب المخصوص يدلُّ على ثبوت القيام لزيد، وهو معنى ثالث حدث بالتركيب فللمركَّب دلالة زائدة على ما تدلُّ عليه مفرداته، وهو محل النزاع. قوله : (وَضْعِيَّةٌ بِالنَّوْعِ الَّذي يؤخذ من كلام الناظم : أنَّها موضوعةٌ فقط، وأما كونها موضوعة بالنَّوع فلا يؤخذ من كلامه . ومعنى «كونها موضوعةٌ بالنَّوع : أنَّ المركَّب أنواع خبر وأمر، ونهي ونداء; وكل منها موضوع لمعناه الخاص به. قوله : (وَهُوَ التَّحْقِيقُ كَمَا بَيَّنَهُ الدَّمَامِينِيُّ وَغَيْرُهُ وكذا شهره أيضاً ابن عرفة، واختاره التَّاحُ السبكي والقرافي ; وحجتهم : أنَّ العرب حجرت في التركيب كما حجرت في المفردات، فليس لنا أن نستعمل تركيباً إلا إذا سمع له نظير في كلام العرب، فلا يقدَّم مثلاً المضاف إليه على المضاف، ولا الفاعل على الفعل; لأنَّ ذلك لم يسمع . قوله : ( خِلَافاً لِمَنْ قَالَ: «إِنَّهَا عَقْلِيَّةٌ) بمعنى : أنَّ مَن عَلِم مسمَّى «زيد»، وعلم مسمّى «قائم»، وسمع زَيْدُ قَائِمٌ» بإعرابه المخصوص، فَهِم بالضَّرورة معنى هذا الكلام، وإن لم يسمع مِنَ العرب; وحجتهم : أنَّها لو كانت وضعيّةً لتوقفت المركبات على السَّماع كتوقف المفردات، وإلى هذا القول ذهب ابن الحاجب والسكاكي، وتبعهما ابن مالك وصححه الأزهري. قال المحقق سيدي محمد بن عبد القادر الفاسي رحمهما الله تعالى : ولعل كلامهم عند التحقيق
So it is taken from it: that the signification of the compound is conventional by type, and that is the verified position as al-Damāmīnī and others explained it, contrary to those who said: 'It is rational.' His words: ('So it is taken from it: that the signification of the compound') means: upon the compositional meaning, I mean the one arising from composition; for when you say 'Zaydun qā'imun', 'Zayd' from it indicates the specific essence, and 'qā'im' indicates some essence described with standing, and these two significations are definitely conventional, and there is no disagreement about that. The totality of the two expressions with the specific inflection indicates the establishment of standing for Zayd, and that is a third meaning that occurs through composition. So the compound has an additional signification beyond what its parts signify, and that is the point of dispute. His words: ('conventional by type') which is taken from the author's speech: that it is only conventional, but that it is conventional by type is not taken from his speech. And the meaning of 'it being conventional by type' is that the compound has types: declarative, command, prohibition, and call; and each is conventionally assigned to its specific meaning. His words: ('and that is the verified position as al-Damāmīnī and others explained it') Likewise, Ibn ‘Arafa also made it famous, and al-Tāj al-Subkī and al-Qarāfī chose it. Their evidence is that the Arabs restricted composition just as they restricted singular expressions, so we may not use a composition unless a similar one is heard in the speech of the Arabs. For example, the genitive construct should not precede the governing noun, nor the subject precede the verb, because that has not been heard. His words: ('contrary to those who said: 'It is rational'') meaning that whoever knows the referent of 'Zayd' and knows the referent of 'qā'im', and hears 'Zaydun qā'imun' with its specific inflection, necessarily understands the meaning of this statement even if he has not heard it from the Arabs. Their evidence is that if it were conventional, compounds would depend on hearing as the singulars do. This opinion was held by Ibn al-Hājib and al-Sakkākī, and followed by Ibn Mālik, and al-Azharī corrected it. The investigator Sīdī Muḥammad ibn ‘Abd al-Qādir al-Fāsī, may Allah have mercy on them both, said: 'Perhaps their statement, upon investigation...'
Source pages p. 467

Commentary
This passage is a critical and polemical one from the commentary. It addresses the nature of the signification of compound expressions (al-murakkab). The commentator states that from the matn it is taken that the signification of the compound is conventional by type (wad‘iyya bi al-naw‘), which is the verified position (tahqīq), contrary to those who claim it is rational (‘aqliyya). The passage then explains the disputed point: when we say 'Zaydun qā'imun', the individual words 'Zayd' and 'qā'im' have conventional signification individually—this is agreed upon. However, the composite expression, with its specific inflection, yields a third meaning: the attribution of standing to Zayd. This additional compositional meaning is the point of contention. The commentator clarifies that the phrase 'conventional by type' means that compounds are not merely conventional in general but that each type of compound (e.g., declarative, imperative, prohibition, call) has its own conventional assignment. The verified position is supported by al-Damāmīnī, Ibn ‘Arafa, al-Tāj al-Subkī, and al-Qarāfī, who argue that the Arabs restricted composition just as they restricted singulars, meaning that we cannot arbitrarily combine words; we must follow the patterns heard from Arabs. For example, one cannot front the genitive construct in a way not attested. On the other hand, the opposing rationalist view, attributed to Ibn al-Hājib, al-Sakkākī, Ibn Mālik, and al-Azharī, holds that once one knows the meanings of the individual words and hears the inflected compound, the mind necessarily grasps the compositional meaning without needing to have heard that specific composition from the Arabs. The passage ends with a reference to the investigator al-Fāsī, who suggests that perhaps the two positions converge upon investigation, but the text cuts off.

وَضْعِيَّة
عَقْلِيَّة
النَّوْع

Key Takeaway

Definition of Murakkab (Compound) in Logic
Commentator defines murakkab with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given expression is murakkab or mufrad according to the logicians' definition.

Core Idea
A compound is an expression whose parts each signify a part of its meaning.

The logicians distinguish between simple (mufrad) and compound (murakkab) expressions because the structure of an expression affects how it conveys meaning. In this passage, the commentator clarifies the precise logical definition of murakkab: it is not just any multi-word phrase, but one where each individual part contributes a distinct part of the overall meaning. The examples from Arabic sentences and genitive constructions will help you apply this definition.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition
Find the Arabic phrasing that states what murakkab is according to the logicians. The key phrase is: 'مَا دَلَّ جُزْؤُهُ عَلَى جُزْءِ مَعْنَاهُ' (that whose part indicates a part of its meaning).
This definition is the core of the passage.
2
Analyze the first example: 'Qāma Zaydun'
Break down the sentence: 'qāma' (stood) indicates the occurrence of standing in the past; 'Zaydun' indicates the entity that performed the standing. The total meaning is the attribution of standing to Zaid. Each part supplies a distinct component, so this is murakkab.
Note that the parts correspond to parts of the meaning, not just words.
3
Analyze the second example: 'Ghulāmu Zaydin'
In this genitive construction, 'ghulāmu' indicates the attribute of being a slave, and 'Zaydin' indicates the owner. The combined meaning is a specific slave owned by Zaid. Again, each part contributes a part of the meaning.
The commentator stresses that this holds only if the construction is not used as a proper name (like 'Abdullah').
4
Contrast with mufrad expressions
A simple expression (mufrad) like 'Zaydun' or 'rajulun' has no parts that individually indicate parts of the meaning. Even multi-word expressions like 'the dog' are considered mufrad if the article does not add a distinct part of the meaning (in logical analysis).
This distinction is crucial for logical precision in definitions and syllogisms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَـ «أَوَّلُ» : مُبْتَدَأ، وَسَوَّغَ الِابْتِدَاءَ بِهِ مَعَ تَنْكِيرِهِ : كَوْنُهُ صِفَةٌ لِمُقَدَّرٍ؛ أَيْ : فَقِسْمٌ أَوَّلٌ، وَمَا بَعْدَهُ خَبَرُهُ . وَضَمَّ الزَّايَ فِي «جُزْءُ لِلوَزْنِ. وَالمَعْنَى : أَنَّ المُرَكَّبَ عِنْدَ المَنَاطِقَةِ هُوَ : مَا دَلَّ جُزْؤُهُ عَلَى جُزْءِ مَعْنَاهُ»؛ نَحْوُ : «قَامَ زَيْدٌ، فَالجُزْءُ الأَوَّلُ مِنْ هَذِهِ الجُمْلَةِ وَهُوَ قَامَ دَلَّ عَلَى قِيَامِ حَاصِلٍ فِي المَاضِي، وَجُزْؤُهَا الثَّانِي وَهُوَ زَيْدٌ دَلَّ عَلَى الذَّاتِ الَّتِي حَصَلَ مِنْهَا القِيَامُ، وَكُلٌّ مِنْ هَذَيْنِ الْمَعْنَيْنِ جُزْءٌ مِنْ مَعْنَى الجُمْلَةِ الَّذِي هُوَ حُصُولُ القِيَامِ فِيمَا مَضَى مِنْ «زَيْدِ». وَكَذَا قَوْلُنَا : «غُلَامُ زَيْدٍ وَنَحْوُهُ مِنَ المُرَكَبِ الإِضَافِيِّ الَّذِي لَمْ يُجْعَلْ عَلَماً، فَإِنَّ مَعْنَاهُ : ذَاتٌ مَوْصُوفَةٌ بِالغُلَامِيَّةِ مَنْسُوبَةٌ لِذَاتٍ مُسَمَّاةٍ بـ «زَيْدِ»، وَلَفْظُ «غُلَامُ يَدُلُّ عَلَى الأُوْلَى، وَزَيْدٍ عَلَى الثَّانِيَةِ، وَكُلٌّ مِنْهُمَا جُزْءٌ مِنْ مَجْمُوعِ المُضَافِ وَالمُضَافِ إِلَيْهِ.
Thus, 'Awwal' is a subject (mubtada'), and what permits initiating with it despite its indefiniteness is that it is a description of an estimated [noun]; i.e., 'a first division', and what follows is its predicate. He made the zay of 'juz'u' (with a damma on the zay) for the meter. And the meaning: that the compound according to the logicians is: that whose part indicates a part of its meaning; like 'Zaid stood up' (qāma Zaydun). The first part of this sentence, which is 'qāma', indicates a standing that occurred in the past, and its second part, which is 'Zaydun', indicates the entity from whom the standing originated. And each of these two meanings is a part of the meaning of the sentence, which is the occurrence of the standing in the past from Zaid. And similarly our saying: 'Ghulāmu Zaydin' (Zaid's slave) and the like of it from the genitive construction that is not made a proper name; for its meaning is: an entity described by slavery related to an entity named Zaid; the word 'Ghulāmu' indicates the first, and 'Zaydin' indicates the second, and each of them is a part of the total of the construct and the constructee.
Source pages p. 468

Commentary
This passage opens with a grammatical analysis of the word 'أَوَّلُ' in the matn, explaining why it can be the subject of a nominal sentence despite being indefinite: it is functionally a description of an estimated 'قسم' (division). Then the commentator states the definition of the compound (مركب) according to the logicians: an expression whose constituent parts each signify a part of the overall meaning. He illustrates with two examples from Arabic: the verbal sentence 'قام زيد' (Zaid stood) and the genitive construction 'غلام زيد' (Zaid's slave). In the first example, the verb 'قام' indicates the occurrence of standing in the past, and the subject 'زيد' indicates the entity that performed the standing; the sentence's meaning as a whole is the attribution of the standing to Zaid, and each word supplies a distinct component of that meaning. In the second example, 'غلام' indicates the attribute of being a slave, and 'زيد' indicates the person to whom the slave belongs; the composite meaning is a specific entity described by slavery and attributed to Zaid. This definition distinguishes compound expressions from simple (مفرد) ones, where the parts do not correspond to parts of the meaning.

Definition of Compound (Murakkab) According to Logicians
Author defines compound and illustrates with Arabic examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether an expression is a compound (murakkab) according to the logicians' definition and distinguish it from simple expressions or proper names.

Core Idea
A compound's parts each contribute a piece of the total meaning.

In this lesson, we examine the logical definition of 'compound' (murakkab) as given by the logicians. Unlike grammatical definitions which may treat any phrase as compound, logicians have a precise criterion: the parts of the expression must each indicate a separate part of the overall meaning. Understanding this distinction is foundational for analyzing definitions and arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the definition
The exact wording: 'ما دل جزؤه على جزء معناه' (that whose part indicates a part of its meaning). This is the core logical condition.
Compare with grammatical definitions of compound.
2
Analyze the first example: 'Qama Zayd'
In this verbal sentence, 'qama' indicates a past action (standing) and 'Zayd' indicates the agent. Each part gives a distinct component of the overall meaning.
The total meaning is 'standing happened from Zayd in the past'.
3
Analyze the second example: 'Ghulam Zayd'
In this genitive construct, 'ghulam' indicates a boy and 'Zayd' indicates the possessor. Each contributes a partial meaning to the whole.
This is only a compound if not used as a proper name.
4
Notice the restriction: not made a proper name
If a genitive construct like 'Abdullah' is used as a proper name, the whole is treated as a single label, not a compound. The parts no longer independently indicate parts of the meaning.
Proper names are exceptions; they are considered simple in logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالمَعْنَى : أَنَّ المُرَكَّبَ عِنْدَ المَنَاطِقَةِ هُوَ : مَا دَلَّ جُزْؤُهُ عَلَى جُزْءِ مَعْنَاهُ ; نَحْوُ : «قَامَ زَيْدٌ»، فَالجُزْءُ الأَوَّلُ مِنْ هَذِهِ الجُمْلَةِ وَهُوَ قَامَ دَلَّ عَلَى قِيَامِ حَاصِلٍ فِي المَاضِي، وَجُزْؤُهَا الثَّانِي وَهُوَ زَيْدٌ دَلَّ عَلَى الذَّاتِ الَّتِي حَصَلَ مِنْهَا القِيَامُ، وَكُلٌّ مِنْ هَذَيْنِ الْمَعْنَيْنِ جُزْءٌ مِنْ مَعْنَى الجُمْلَةِ الَّذِي هُوَ حُصُولُ القِيَامِ فِيمَا مَضَى مِنْ «زَيْدِ». وَكَذَا قَوْلُنَا : «غُلَامُ زَيْدٍ» وَنَحْوُهُ مِنَ المُرَكَّبِ الإِضَافِيِّ الَّذِي لَمْ يُجْعَلْ عَلَماً، فَإِنَّ مَعْنَاهُ : ذَاتٌ مَوْصُوفَةٌ بِالغُلَامِيَّةِ مَنْسُوبَةٌ لِذَاتٍ مُسَمَّاةٍ بـ «زَيْدِ»، وَلَفْظُ «غُلَامُ» يَدُلُّ عَلَى الأُوْلَى، وَ«زَيْدٍ» عَلَى الثَّانِيَةِ، وَكُلٌّ مِنْهُمَا جُزْءٌ مِنْ مَجْمُوعِ المُضَافِ وَالمُضَافِ إِلَيْهِ.
The meaning: the compound according to the logicians is that whose part indicates a part of its meaning, such as 'Zayd stood'; the first part of this sentence, which is 'stood', indicates a past occurrence, and its second part, which is 'Zayd', indicates the entity from which the occurrence happened, and each of these two meanings is part of the meaning of the sentence which is the occurrence of standing in the past from Zayd. Likewise our saying 'Ghulam Zayd' and similar from the genitive compound that is not made a proper name, for its meaning is an entity described by boyhood attributed to an entity named Zayd, and the word 'Ghulam' indicates the first, and 'Zayd' indicates the second, and each of the two is part of the total of the construct term and the term to which it is annexed.
Source pages p. 468

Commentary
This passage defines the compound (murakkab) as understood by the logicians (al-mantiqiyya). The definition is: a compound is that whose part indicates a part of its meaning. The author then illustrates with two types of compounds: the verbal sentence 'Qama Zayd' and the genitive construct 'Ghulam Zayd'. In the first, each word contributes a partial meaning: 'qama' conveys the occurrence of an action in the past, and 'Zayd' identifies the agent. In the second, 'Ghulam' indicates the attribute of boyhood, and 'Zayd' the possessor. Thus the total meaning is composed from the parts. The condition that the compound not be made a proper name ensures that it is truly compositional, as proper names, though formally similar, treat the whole as a single label.

Key Takeaway

Understanding 'Ma' in the Definition of the Conventional Word
Defining the scope of 'ma' in the classification of words.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the referent of 'ma' in a logical definition and determine which items are excluded based on the conditions of being a conventional composite word.

Core Idea
The word 'ma' in definition acts as a genus that excludes meaningless words and non-composite letters.

In this passage, the author clarifies what the word 'ma' refers to when used in a definition of 'lafz' (word). This is crucial for understanding how logicians classify words: they only consider conventional utterances that are composite. By understanding this, you will be able to parse the scope of any definition in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum 'ma'
Locate the word 'ma' in the Arabic text. It is a demonstrative pronoun used here as a placeholder for the term being defined. The passage says 'ما التي في التعريف' – the 'ma' in the definition.
Remember that 'ma' can have different meanings; here it functions as a noun of definition.
2
Understand the defining phrase
The phrase following 'ma' is 'اللفظ الدال بالوضع' – the word that indicates by convention. This is the complete definiens. The 'ma' refers to this entire phrase.
Convention (wad') is the act of assigning meaning.
3
Recognize the genus function
The author says 'وهي كالجنس' – 'ma' is like a genus. It serves as a category covering all subdivisions (like simple, compound, etc.).
A genus includes multiple species.
4
Identify the first exclusion: muhmal
Muhmal is a meaningless string like 'dayz'. Such utterances do not indicate anything by convention and are thus excluded from the definition.
Muhmal is not even considered simple or composite.
5
Identify the second exclusion: letters without parts
The condition 'دل جزؤه' (its part indicates) excludes letters like 'ba' and 'lam' that have no parts, as well as proper names like 'Zayd' whose parts do not contribute to the meaning.
Letters are meaningful but not composite; proper names are composite but not analytically compositional.

فصل في مباحث الألفاظ فَ «مَا الَّتِي فِي التَّعْرِيفِ : وَاقِعَةٌ عَلَى اللَّفْظِ الدَّالّ بِالوَضْعِ؛ بِقَرِينَةِ جَعْلِهِ مَوْرِدَ التَّقْسِيمِ ، وَهِيَ كَالجِنْسِ ، فَيَخْرُجُ عَنْهَا : المُهْمَلُ : دَيْز»، فَلَا يُوصَفُ بِتَرْكِيبٍ وَلَا إِفْرَادٍ. وَقَوْلُهُ : (دَلَّ جُزْؤُهُ) مُخْرِجٌ لِمَا لَا جُزْءَ لَهُ : بَاءِ الجَرِّ وَلَامِهِ، وَلِمَا لَهُ جُزْءٌ غَيْرُ دَالَّ كـ: «زَيْدِ».
Chapter on Topics of Words. The 'ma' in the definition refers to the verbal word that indicates by convention, indicated by its being made the subject of division, and it is like a genus, so it excludes the muhmal (unused word) like 'dayz', which is not described as composite or simple. And His saying 'its part indicates' excludes what has no part, like the preposition 'ba' and its 'lam', and what has a part that does not indicate, like 'Zayd'.
Source pages p. 469

Commentary
This passage explains the definition of 'lafz' (word) as used in logic. The word 'ma' in the definition is not a generic term but specifically refers to the meaningful conventional word. It functions like a genus covering all divisions. It excludes the muhmal, which is a meaningless string of letters like 'dayz' – such a word is not even considered simple or composite. The second condition 'its part indicates' further excludes two types: (1) letters that have no part, like prepositions 'ba' and 'lam', which are single letters and thus not composite; (2) words that have parts but those parts do not indicate a part of the meaning, as in the proper name 'Zayd' which, though having letters, as a whole indicates a specific person without its letters each contributing to the meaning. This clarifies the logical notion of 'compound' as opposed to 'simple'.

مهمل
دال بالوضع

Key Takeaway
The word 'ma' in the definition ensures that only conventional, composite words are considered in logical classifications.

The Condition 'On a Part of Its Meaning' in Definitions
Definition and illustration of a condition in term definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a compound proper noun satisfies the condition that its parts indicate a part of its meaning or not.

Core Idea
A term's part can indicate something outside the term's meaning, excluding such terms from the condition.

When defining a term, logicians examine whether its parts indicate parts of the term's meaning. But what if a part indicates a meaning completely foreign to the whole? This lesson unpacks that nuance using the examples of Ba'labakk and Abd Shams, and resolves a subtle objection about redundancy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Condition
The condition 'على جزء معناه' (on a part of its meaning) is used to exclude terms whose parts indicate something other than a part of the term's meaning. Recognize that this is a refinement of a broader definition.
2
Examine the Example Ba'labakk
Analyze 'بعلبك' as a proper noun for a city. Extract the parts 'بعل' (husband) and 'بك' (trituration). These meanings are not parts of the city's meaning, so the condition applies.
Ensure you see that the parts have independent meanings, but these are not components of the city's definition.
3
Extend to Other Compound Proper Nouns
Consider 'عبد شمس' and 'امرئ القيس'. Each part (e.g., 'عبد' meaning servant) indicates a meaning that is not a part of the named individual. Hence they are also excluded by the condition.
Note that the condition is about the part indicating a whole meaning, not a part of the meaning of the proper noun.
4
Resolve the Letter Objection
An objection arises: does the condition include letters like ز and ق? The author clarifies that 'جزء' means a direct component of the compound, not every letter. Letters are parts of parts, not immediate parts.
This step prevents overextension of the condition to trivial cases.
5
Address the Redundancy Objection
A further objection: if parts of proper nouns have no indication at all, they are already excluded by the earlier condition 'ما دل جزؤه', making 'على جزء معناه' redundant. The correct answer: the condition is applied by considering the isolated part's potential indication, not its actual indication in the compound.
This refinement shows the depth of the logical analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَقَوْلُهُ : (عَلَى جُزُءِ مَعْنَاهُ مُخْرِجٌ لِمَا لَهُ جُزْءٌ دَالٌ عَلَى غَيْرِ جُزْءِ مَعْنَاهُ كَـ : «بَعْلَبَكَ» عَلَماً لِبَلَدٍ، فَإِنَّ كُلًّا مِنْ بَعْلَ، وَبَكَ دَالٌ عَلَى مَعْنِّى لَكِنَّهُ غَيْرُ جُزْءٍ مِنْ مَعْنَى بَعْلَبَكَ»؛ إِذِ البَعْلُ مَعْنَاهُ : الزَّوْجُ ، وَالبَكُ : الدَّقُ مَصْدَرُ بَكَهُ . وَيَخْرُجُ بِهَذَا أَيْضاً : كُلُّ مُرَكَّب إِضَافِيٌّ جُعِلَ عَلَماً كَـ عَبْدِ شَمْسٍ، وَ امْرِئَ القَيْسِ : وَنَحْوِهِمَا ، إِذْ لَا يَدُلُّ وَاحِدٌ مِنَ الجُرْأَيْنِ عَلَى جُزْءِ مَعْنَاهُ الَّذِي هُوَ الشَّخْصُ المُسَمَّى ، فَإِنَّ عَبْدِ» يَدُلُّ عَلَى ذَاتٍ مُتَّصِفَةٍ بِالعُبُودِيَّةِ ، وَهِيَ كُلُّ المَعْنَى لَا جُزْؤُهُ، قصارة. فإن قيل : إضافة اسم الجنس إلى المعرفة في قوله : «جُزْؤُهُ» تُفيدُ العموم ؛ أي: دلَّ كلُّ جزءٍ مِن : أجزائه، ونحو: «زَيْدٌ قَائِمٌ» ليس كلُّ جزء من أجزائه دالا على جزء معناه؛ ضرورةً أنَّ مِن أجزائه ما لا دلالة له أصلاً ك: «الزَّاي» و«القاف». قلنا : المراد بـ «الجزء» - كما قال الوليُّ العراقي - : ما صار به اللفظ مركَّباً ؛ أعني : الجزء بلا واسطة، فلا يفيد العكس بالزاي والقاف؛ لأنَّ كلا منهما جزء جزء، لا جزء، وكلامنا في هذا دون ذاك . قوله : (فَإِنَّ كُلًّا مِنْ بَعْلَ، وَبَكَ دَالٌ عَلَى مَعْنى أي : موضوعٌ لمعنى يدلُّ عليه قبل المزج ؛ إلَّا أنَّه لم تعتبر هذه الدَّلالة حين التركيب. قوله : (فَإِنَّ عَبْدِ» يَدُلُّ عَلَى ذَاتٍ ... إلخ) كلامُهُ يقتضي : أنَّ العبودية ملاحظة أنَّ العبودية ملاحظة بعد التسمية، وليس كذلك؛ إذ لا دلالة لجزء العَلَم المنقول مِنَ المركب على ما يأتي، وعليه: فيخرج نحو: «عبد شمس» وكذا: «بعلبك» بقوله : «مَا دَلَّ جُزْؤُهُ»، وحينئذ يبقى هذا القيد ضائعاً؛ لأنَّ كلَّ ما خرج به خرج بما قبله؛ لأنَّه إذا لم تكن لأجزاء هذه المثل دلالة كانت خارجةً بقوله : «دَلَ جُزْؤُهُ» فيبقى قوله : عَلَى جُزْءِ مَعْنَاهُ» ضائعاً . والحقُّ في الجواب عن هذا ما أجاب به الشَّارح في حواشيه على المختصر»: بأنَّهم لم يعتبروا خصوص دلالة الجزء حال جزئيته، بل مرادهم أن ينظر إلى اللَّفظ، فإن كان لبعض أجزائه دلالة على تقدير انفراده خرج بهذا القيد، وإلا فبما قبله. قوله : (وَهِيَ كُلُّ المَعْنَى) قال شيخنا اليازغيُّ : كلُّ المعنى هو الذَّات المسماة بـ «عبد شمس» والعبودية غيرُ ملاحظةٍ فيها أصلاً .
And his saying: (On a part of its meaning) is an exclusion for what has a part that indicates something other than a part of its meaning, like 'Ba'labakk' as a proper noun for a city, for each of 'Ba'l' and 'Bakk' indicates a meaning, but it is not a part of the meaning of 'Ba'labakk'; because the meaning of 'Ba'l' is 'husband', and 'Bakk' is 'trituration', the infinitive of 'bakahu'. And by this also is excluded every compound annexed term made a proper noun like 'Abd Shams' and 'Imri' al-Qays' and the like, since neither of the two parts indicates a part of its meaning which is the named individual. For 'Abd' indicates a self qualified by servitude, and that is the whole meaning, not a part of it. Fragments. If it is said: The annexation of a common noun to a definite in his saying 'جزؤه' gives generality; i.e., every part of its parts indicates, and like 'Zayd is standing' not every part of its parts indicates a part of its meaning, necessarily, because among its parts are those that have no indication at all, like 'zay' and 'qaf'. We say: What is meant by 'part' - as al-Wali al-'Iraqi said - is that by which the utterance becomes compound; I mean the immediate part, so it does not give the reverse by 'zay' and 'qaf', because each of them is a part of a part, not a part, and our speech is about this not that. His saying: (For each of 'Ba'l' and 'Bakk' indicates a meaning) i.e., it is assigned to a meaning that it indicates before the mixing, except that this indication is not considered at the time of composition. His saying: (For 'Abd' indicates a self... etc.) His speech requires that servitude is observed after the naming, and it is not so; since there is no indication for the part of the transferred proper noun from the compound to what comes, and accordingly, examples like 'Abd Shams' and 'Ba'labakk' are excluded by his saying 'what its part indicates', and then this condition becomes wasted; because everything excluded by it is excluded by what preceded; because if the parts of these examples have no indication, they are excluded by his saying 'its part indicated', so his saying 'on a part of its meaning' remains wasted. The correct answer to this is what the commentator answered in his glosses on 'al-Mukhtasar': that they did not consider the particular indication of the part while it is a part, rather their intention is to look at the utterance, and if some of its parts have indication on the condition of its isolation, it is excluded by this condition, otherwise by what preceded. His saying: (And it is all of the meaning) Our teacher al-Yazghi said: All of the meaning is the named self by 'Abd Shams', and servitude is not considered in it at all.
Source pages p. 470

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of a previous commentator's explanation of a condition in the definition of a term. It begins by restating the condition: 'on a part of its meaning' excludes terms that have a part indicating something outside the term's meaning. The example 'Ba'labakk' is given: the parts 'Ba'l' and 'Bakk' each have meanings (husband and trituration), but these are not parts of the meaning of the city-name. The author then extends this to compound annexed proper nouns like 'Abd Shams' and 'Imri' al-Qays', where the parts do not indicate a part of the named individual. An objection is raised: the term 'جزؤه' (its part) would imply every part, including letters like zay and qaf, which have no indication. The response clarifies that 'part' here means a direct component of the compound word, not a letter. Further criticism follows: if the parts of these proper nouns have no indication at all, then the condition 'on a part of its meaning' becomes redundant because they are already excluded by the earlier condition 'its part indicated'. The correct answer, from the commentator's own glosses, is that the condition is applied by looking at the isolated part's potential indication, not its actual indication in the compound. Finally, the comment on 'Abd Shams' clarifies that 'all of the meaning' refers to the named individual, and the meaning of 'abd' (servitude) is not considered part of that.

دلالة
مركب إضافي

The condition 'على جزء معناه' excludes terms whose parts indicate meanings that are not components of the term's own meaning, refined by considering the isolated part's potential indication.

Critique of Examples: Derivation vs Composition in Definitions
Critique of examples in the definition of part indicating

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate whether a word's parts are genuine components or artifacts of derivation, and apply this criterion to test examples in a definition.

Core Idea
Derived words are not composed of their root letters; composition and derivation are distinct.

In defining the 'part indicating' (al-juz' al-dall), scholars used examples like 'Abkam' and 'Insan' to illustrate what the condition excludes. However, the commentator objects that these words are derived, not composed, so their apparent parts are not actual parts of the word. Understanding this distinction is crucial for accurate definitions in Arabic linguistics and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Recall the Definition
The definition of 'part indicating' (al-juz' al-dall) is: a part of a word that indicates a meaning from the whole meaning of the word. The condition being discussed is that the part must be a genuine component.
This definition is the backdrop of the critique.
2
Examine the Example 'Abkam'
The example 'Abkam' (mute) is claimed to be excluded because its parts 'ab' (father) and 'kam' (how many) have meanings foreign to 'Abkam' (a mute person). But the commentator argues that 'ab' and 'kam' are not actual parts of 'Abkam'.
3
Analyze Derivation of 'Abkam'
'Abkam' is on the pattern 'af'al', derived from 'bakam' (muteness). The hamza is extra. The letters 'a', 'b', 'k', 'm' are not a combination of 'ab' and 'kam' but a derivative form. Thus, 'ab' and 'kam' are not real components.
This shows that derivation changes the status of the letters.
4
Examine the Example 'Insan'
Similarly, 'Insan' (human) is said to have 'in' (if) as a part, but 'in' is a conditional particle. However, 'Insan' is derived from either 'uns' (sociability) or 'nisyan' (forgetfulness), so 'in' is not a genuine part.
The etymology is disputed but both are derivations, not compositions.
5
Apply the Criterion
To test if a word's part is genuine, check if the word is composed of those morphemes or derived from a root. Derived words have extra letters that are not independent morphemes.
This criterion helps avoid false exclusions in definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Chapter on investigations of utterances. And likewise 'Imru' meaning 'man'. And their saying: By this condition is also excluded 'Abkam' and 'Insan', because the part of 'Abkam' which is 'Ab' indicates 'father', and likewise 'Kam' is a metonymy for a number inquired about or informed of its multitude, and likewise 'In' in 'Insan' is a conditional particle, and these meanings are foreign to the meaning of 'Abkam' which is a person characterized by muteness, and 'Insan' whose concept is 'rational animal' has consideration; because neither 'Ab' nor 'Kam' can be considered a part of 'Abkam' which is with an extra hamza derived from 'bakam', and nor that 'In' is a part of 'Insan', because it is derived from 'Ins' or from 'Nisyan' according to the difference of opinion; just as it is not said in 'Rajul': Fragments. His saying: (And likewise 'Imru' meaning 'man') i.e., it indicates a self qualified by manliness, and it is all of the meaning, and that is also not apparent; since 'rajul' is an indefinite common noun, and the named 'Imri' al-Qays' is a definite individual; because the proper noun is intended to specify the named, so then the self qualified by manliness is not all of the meaning; so said our teacher mentioned, and by this you learn what is in the speech of our teacher Ibn Mansur, so look into it. His saying: (A part of 'Abkam' which is with an extra hamza) because the measure of 'Abkam' is 'af'al', so the hamza is extra, and this form does not exist in the original which is 'bakam', rather there is no composition in it at all, and you cannot consider the derivative without the derived from; because some of the first is taken from the second necessarily. His saying: (Because it is taken from 'Ins' or from 'Nisyan') The first is for the Basrans, so it is 'fa'lan' from 'Ins'; some said: 'Man is only named Insan for his sociability, and the heart only for its turning.' The second is for the Kufans, and its origin is 'Insyan', so it is 'if'ilan' from 'Nisyan', the movement of the 'ya' was transferred to the 'sin' for lightness, and the 'ya' was deleted due to two quiescents meeting, and this is evidenced by its diminutive 'Unaysiyan', and the claim of irregularity is against the principle. And some said: 'She said, when separation became inevitable and its cup had mixed the cupbearer with it and the cup, do not forget those covenants, for you are named Insan because you are forgetful.' And it is a generic individuated noun, used for the masculine and feminine singular.
Source pages p. 471

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement within a chapter on verbal investigations. It begins by noting the example 'Imru' meaning 'man' in the context of the condition. Then it discusses the claim that 'Abkam' and 'Insan' are excluded by the condition because their parts have meanings foreign to the whole: 'Ab' means father and 'Kam' is a metonymy for number, while 'Abkam' means a mute person; 'In' is a conditional particle, while 'Insan' means rational animal. However, the author objects that 'Ab' and 'Kam' are not genuine parts of 'Abkam' because 'Abkam' is derived from 'bakam' with an extra hamza, not composed from 'ab' and 'kam'. Similarly, 'Insan' is derived from 'ins' or 'nisyan', not composed from 'in' and 'san'. The author then explains the derivation of 'Insan' according to Basran and Kufan grammarians, providing poetic evidence. The passage also critiques the example 'Imru' by showing that 'imru' meaning 'man' is a common noun, but the proper noun 'Imri' al-Qays' is a specific individual, so the meaning of 'imru' is not the whole meaning of the proper noun. The author concludes by directing the reader to consult the commentary of Ibn Mansur for further clarity.

أبكم
إنسان
جزء
اشتقاق (derivation)
تركيب (composition)

Derived words are not composed of their root morphemes; evaluating examples in definitions requires distinguishing composition from derivation.

Why 'Abkam' and 'Insan' Are Not Compound Words
Commentator refutes the claim that 'abkam' and 'insan' are composite words.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a derived word (mushtaqq) and a compound word (murakkab) in Arabic by analyzing morphological patterns and rejecting false segmentation.

Core Idea
A word can be derived from a root without being composed of independent morphemes.

In this passage, the commentator refutes a mistaken claim that the words 'abkam' (dumb) and 'insan' (human) are compounds of meaningful parts like 'ab' (father) and 'kam' (how many). He shows that they are derived forms with extra letters, not composites. Understanding this distinction is crucial for avoiding errors in linguistic analysis and logical definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Claim Being Refuted
The claim is that 'abkam' is composed of 'ab' (father) and 'kam' (how many), and 'insan' includes 'in' as a conditional particle. These parts have meanings foreign to the actual words.
2
Analyze the Morphological Pattern of 'Abkam'
Note that 'abkam' follows the pattern 'af'al' (أفعل). The hamza is an extra letter, not a root letter. The word is derived from 'bikam' (dumbness), not composed of 'ab' and 'kam'.
Pattern recognition is key: the word is a derived form (mushtaqq) on a standard pattern.
3
Analyze the Etymology of 'Insan'
According to Basrans, 'insan' comes from 'ins' (familiarity) on pattern 'fa'lan'; according to Kufans, from 'nisyan' (forgetfulness) on pattern 'if'ilan'. In both cases, 'in' is part of the root or pattern, not a separate particle.
The commentator mentions disagreement among grammarians, but both views support derivation, not composition.
4
Conclude: Derivation vs. Composition
Both words are derived (mushtaqq) from a root, not compound (murakkab) from independent words. The extra letters are morphological markers, not independent morphemes.
This principle applies to many Arabic words; we must not confuse derivative morphology with compounding.

And likewise 'imru' meaning 'rajul'. And their saying: 'by this condition also is excluded 'abkam' and 'insan', because the part of 'abkam' which is 'ab' indicates the father, and likewise 'kam' is a metonym for a number inquired about or informed of its multitude, and likewise 'in' in 'insan' is a conditional particle, and these meanings are foreign to the meaning of 'abkam' which is a person characterized by dumbness, and 'insan' whose concept is 'rational animal' has a problem; because neither 'ab' nor 'kam' can correctly be a part of 'abkam' which, with the addition of the hamza, is derived from 'bikam' (dumbness), nor is 'in' a part of 'insan', because it is taken from 'ins' or from 'nisyan' according to the disagreement; just as it is not said in 'rajul'. Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) that explains and expands upon a statement in the matn regarding the definition of 'word' and the exclusion of certain terms from being considered compound words. The commentator first mentions that 'imru'' meaning 'rajul' is similar. He then addresses the claim that 'abkam' and 'insan' are excluded by a certain condition because they appear to have parts ('ab' and 'kam' in 'abkam', and 'in' in 'insan') that have meanings (father, how many? and conditional particle) foreign to the actual meanings of the words (a dumb person, and rational animal). The commentator refutes this by showing that 'abkam' is derived from 'bikam' (dumbness) on the pattern 'af'al', so the hamza is extra and not a separate morpheme; similarly, 'insan' is derived from 'ins' (familiarity) or 'nisyan' (forgetfulness), so 'in' is not a separate part but part of the root. He supports this with linguistic arguments and poetry. The passage thus clarifies the distinction between derivation (ishtiqaq) and composition (tarkib), and warns against treating a derived form as if it were a compound of two independent words.

Key Terms
أبكم (abkam): A person characterized by dumbness (al-bakam); derived from 'bakama' (to be dumb) on the pattern 'af'al'. [Note: No significant shift; this is standard Arabic morphology.]
إنسان (insan): A human being, defined as 'rational animal'; morphologically derived from 'ins' (familiarity) according to Basrans or from 'nisyan' (forgetfulness) according to Kufans. [Note: No significant shift; the etymological debate is classical.]
مشتق (mushtaqq): Derived from a root; here, 'abkam' and 'insan' are derived (mushtaqq) from 'bikam' and 'ins'/ 'nisyan' respectively, not composed of independent words. [Note: Stable term in Arabic linguistics and logic.]

A word's apparent parts may be morphological inflections, not independent morphemes; derivation must be distinguished from composition.

Refining the Definition of a Proper Noun: Intended Signification and Purity
Commentary adds 'intended signification' and 'pure' to proper noun definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why al-Sanusi added the conditions of 'intended signification' and 'pure' to the definition of a proper noun and identify which types of proper nouns each condition excludes.

Core Idea
A proper noun's purpose is pure specification; any intended meaning contaminates it.

The definition of a proper noun (علم) seems straightforward—it is a noun that specifies its referent. However, some proper nouns are derived from compound phrases whose parts still carry meaning. Does that affect their status as proper nouns? This commentary explores refinements to the definition to handle cases like 'al-hayawan al-natiq' used as a name, and relational proper nouns like 'Abu al-Su'ud' where the meaning is intentionally invoked.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base definition
Recall the standard definition of a proper noun (e.g., 'what specifies its referent without being shared'). The passage builds on this definition by adding conditions.
2
Understand the first problem case
Consider 'al-hayawan al-natiq' (the rational animal) used as a proper noun for a human. Each part indicates a part of the meaning (animal, rational), but this signification is not intended by the speaker; the purpose is only to specify the individual.
This case shows how a compound proper noun can accidentally carry meaning.
3
Analyze the first addition: 'intended signification'
Al-Sanusi added 'intended signification' (dalalah maqsudah) to the definition to exclude cases where the parts signify unintentionally, like the above example. The proper noun is for specification, not for conveying compositional meaning.
4
Analyze the second addition: 'pure'
Al-Sanusi further added 'pure' (khalisah) to exclude relational proper nouns (al-alam al-idafi) such as 'Abu al-Su'ud' or 'Hujjat al-Islam', where the speaker intentionally uses the compositional meaning for praise or boasting. Such proper nouns are not 'pure' because they carry intended meaning.
5
Consider the commentator's own view
The commentator argues that the parts of a proper noun derived from a compound do not signify anything at all, whether intended or not. Therefore, the addition of 'intended signification' is unnecessary; the base definition suffices.
This reflects a stricter position on the definition.
