⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَزَادَ الشَّيْخُ السَّنُوسِيُّ وَغَيْرُهُ فِي التَّعْرِيفِ : دَلَالَةٌ مَقْصُودَةٌ»؛ قَالُوا : لِلاحْتِرَازِ بِهِ عَنْ نَحْوِ : «الحَيَوَانِ النَّاطِقِ إِذَا جُعِلَ عَلَماً لإِنْسَانٍ، فَكُلٌّ مِنْ جُزْأَيْهِ دَالٌ عَلَى جُزْءِ مَعْنَى ( الإِنْسَانِ ، لَكِنْ هَذِهِ الدَّلَالَةَ غَيْرُ مَقْصُودَةٍ مِنَ العَلَمِ ; لِأَنَّ القَصْدَ مِنْهُ تَعْيِينُ مُسَمَّاهُ، وَالمَعْنَى الأَصْلِيُّ التَّرْكِيبِيُّ غَيْرُ مَلْحُوظ فِيهِ البَيَّةَ . وَاسْتَحْسَنَ الشَّيْخُ السَّنُوسِيُّ أَنْ يُزَادَ فِيهِ بَعْدَ مَقْصُودَةٌ» : «خَالِصَةٌ»؛ أَيْ : مِنْ شَوَائِبِ العَلَمِيَّةِ ; لِلتَّحَرُّزِ بِهِ عَنِ العَلَمِ الإِضَافِيِّ الَّذِي يُقْصَدُ بِهِ مَعَ تَعْيِينِ مُسَمَّاهُ إِفَادَةَ مَعْنَاهُ التَّرْكِيبِيِّ لِغَرَضِ تَفَاوُلٍ، أَوْ مَدْحٍ، أَوْ غَيْرِهِ، كَمَا فِي «أَبِي السُّعُودِ» وَ «حُجَّةِ الإِسْلَام» وَنَحْوِهِمَا، وَكَأَنَّ القَوْمَ لَمْ يَغْتَبِرُوا هَذِهِ الدَّلَالَةَ؛ لِأَنَّ المَقْصُودَ بِالذَّاتِ مِنَ العَلَمِ إِنَّمَا هُوَ تَعْيِينُ المُسَمَّى، قوله : (مِنْ) أَمْرَيْنِ مِنَ الرُّؤيَةِ وَالجَوَلَانِ) الأمر مِنَ الرُّؤية» : «رَه» بفتح الراء، ومِنَ «الجولان»: «جُل» بضم الجيم وسكون اللَّام . قوله : «دَلَالَةٌ مَقْصُودَةٌ» الحقُّ : أنَّه لا دلالة لجزء العَلَم المنقول مِنَ المركب على جزء معناه، لا دلالة مقصودة ولا غير مقصودةً، فالصَّوابُ : ترك هذا القيد كما في النَّظم . قوله : (لِغَرَضِ تَفَاوُلٍ، أَوْ مَدْحٍ مِن ذلك قول أبي العلاء المعري : سَأَلْنَ فَقُلْتُ مَقْصِدُنَا سَعِيدٌ فَكَانَ اسْمُ الأَمِيرِ لَهُنَّ فَالَا وَقَالَ آخَرُ فِي رَجُلٍ اسْمُهُ أَبُو المَحَاسِنِ: قَصَدْتُ أَبَا المَحَاسِنِ كَيْ أَرَاهُ بِشَوْقٍ كَادَ يَجْذُبنِي إِلَيْهِ فَلَمَّا أَنْ أَتَيْتُ رَأَيْتُ فَرْداً وَلَمْ أَرَ مِنْ بَنِيهِ ابْناً لَدَيْهِ
And Shaykh al-Sanusi and others added to the definition: 'an intended signification'; they said: to guard against something like 'the rational animal' when it is made a proper noun for a human, for each of its two parts indicates a part of the meaning of 'insan', but this signification is not intended from the proper noun, because the intention from it is to specify its named entity, and the original compositional meaning is not considered in it at all. And Shaykh al-Sanusi preferred that after 'intended' one should add 'pure', i.e., from the admixtures of proper noun-ness, to guard against the relational proper noun (al-ilm al-idafi) which intends, along with specifying its named entity, to convey its compositional meaning for a purpose of boasting, or praise, or other, such as 'Abu al-Su'ud' and 'Hujjat al-Islam' and similar. And it is as if the people did not consider this signification, because the primary intention of the proper noun is only the specification of the named. His saying: ('from' two things: from ru'yah and jawalan) the imperative from ru'yah is 'rah' with fathah on the ra, and from jawalan is 'jul' with dammah on the jim and sukun on the lam. His saying: 'an intended signification' The truth is: there is no signification of the part of a proper noun transmitted from a compound on a part of its meaning, neither intended nor unintended. So the correct course is to leave this condition as in the versification. His saying: (for a purpose of boasting or praise) from that is the saying of Abu al-'Ala' al-Ma'arri: 'They asked, so I said: our destination is Sa'id, so the name of the prince was for them a successful one.' And another said of a man named Abu al-Mahasin: 'I intended Abu al-Mahasin that I might see him, with a longing that almost drew me to him. Then when I came, I saw a solitary man and I did not see among his children a son before him.'
Source pages p. 472

Commentary
This passage is a commentary expanding on the definition of a proper noun (علم). The commentator discusses additions made by al-Sanusi and others to the definition. The issue is that if a proper noun is originally a compound phrase (e.g., 'al-hayawan al-natiq' used as a name for a human), its parts may still indicate parts of the meaning (animal and rational), but this signification is not intended; the purpose of a proper noun is solely to specify the named entity. To exclude such cases, al-Sanusi added 'intended signification' (dalalah maqsudah). However, this still excludes compound proper nouns that are intentionally meaningful, like 'Abu al-Su'ud' or 'Hujjat al-Islam', where the speaker intends both to specify the person and to convey a meaning (e.g., praise). Therefore al-Sanusi further preferred adding 'pure' (khalisah) to exclude even these relational proper nouns where the meaning is intended. The commentator then notes that some scholars hold that the parts of a proper noun derived from a compound do not signify at all, whether intended or not, and thus the addition of 'intended' is unnecessary. The passage also includes linguistic remarks on the imperative forms and poetic examples to illustrate the issue of compound proper nouns where the speaker intends the literal meaning (like 'Abu al-Mahasin' meaning 'father of virtues' but the person actually has no sons). This shows that the signification can be intended but the proper noun may still be misleading.

Key Terms
دلالة مقصودة
خالصة
العلم الإضافي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did al-Sanusi add the word 'khalisah' (pure) after 'maqsudah' in the definition of a proper noun?
A) To exclude proper nouns that are not originally Arabic.
B) To exclude proper nouns that are derived from compounds but whose meaning is intended, like 'Abu al-Su'ud'.
C) To exclude proper nouns that are too long.
D) To include proper nouns that are purely for specification.

Key Takeaway
The definition of a proper noun is refined by adding 'intended signification' to exclude accidental meaning, and 'pure' to exclude intended meaning, ensuring that a proper noun serves only to specify its referent.

Definition of Compound Word: Conditions of Intended and Pure Indication
Bannani defines compound word and discusses the necessity of adding 'intended indication' and 'pure'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate whether the additions of 'intended indication' (دلالة مقصودة) and 'pure' (خالصة) are necessary conditions for a compound word in Islamic logic.

Core Idea
A compound word must have parts that intentionally indicate parts of its meaning, not incidentally.

In this lesson, we delve into the technical definition of 'compound word' (مركب) in the tradition of Islamic logic. The basic definition holds that parts indicate parts of meaning by convention. However, later scholars like al-Sanusi proposed refinements to exclude cases where composition is incidental, such as proper names. We will examine these additions and Bannani's assessment of their necessity.

Guided Path
1
Recall the base definition of compound word
From earlier parts of al-Sullam, a compound word is one whose parts indicate parts of its meaning according to convention. This is the starting point. Note that the passage mentions the two components 'رؤية' and 'جولان' as examples from the poem, but they are excluded by a prior condition.
The base definition is assumed from prior text.
2
Identify the problem with proper names
Consider the phrase 'الحيوان الناطق' when used as a proper name for a person. Each part (حيوان, ناطق) does indicate a part of meaning (animal, rational), but this indication is not intended from the proper name. The purpose of a proper name is to specify a referent, not to convey compositional meaning. Thus, without a condition, such a phrase would wrongly be considered a compound.
Proper names are considered single words in logic despite having meaningful parts.
3
Apply the 'intended indication' condition
Al-Sanusi and others added the phrase 'intended indication' (دلالة مقصودة) to the definition to exclude proper names like the above. The indication must be intended from the utterance itself, not merely incidental. This is a refinement to guard against such cases.
This addition is found in later commentaries, not in the original matn.
4
Examine the further addition of 'pure'
Al-Sanusi further approved adding 'pure' (خالصة) to exclude additive proper names (العلم الإضافي) like 'أبو السعود' or 'حجة الإسلام'. In these names, the speaker intends both to specify the referent and to convey meaning (praise, etc.). The indication is intended but not pure – it is mixed with naming. The 'pure' condition ensures that the indication is solely for the meaning, not for naming.
This refinement is specific to al-Sanusi's commentary and not universally adopted.
5
Evaluate the necessity of these additions
Bannani notes that the scholars (القوم) did not consider such indications in proper names at all, because the primary purpose of a proper name is only to specify the referent, not to convey meaning. This suggests that the additions may be unnecessary – the base definition already excludes these cases by virtue of the purpose of proper names. Reflect on whether the refinements are needed or whether they overcomplicate the definition.
Bannani's own view leans towards the additions being superfluous.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِنَّهُ مُرَكَّبٌ مِنْ أَمْرَيْنِ مِنَ الرُّؤيَةِ وَالجَوَلَانِ وَالصَّوَابُ : أَنَّهُمَا خَرَجًا بِمَا قَبْلَ هَذَا القَيْدِ . وَزَادَ الشَّيْخُ السَّنُوسِيُّ وَغَيْرُهُ فِي التَّعْرِيفِ : دَلَالَةٌ مَقْصُودَةٌ»؛ قَالُوا : لِلاحْتِرَازِ بِهِ عَنْ نَحْوِ : «الحَيَوَانِ النَّاطِقِ إِذَا جُعِلَ عَلَماً لإِنْسَانٍ، فَكُلٌّ مِنْ جُزْأَيْهِ دَالٌ عَلَى جُزْءِ مَعْنَى ( الإِنْسَانِ ، لَكِنْ هَذِهِ الدَّلَالَةَ غَيْرُ مَقْصُودَةٍ مِنَ العَلَمِ ; لِأَنَّ القَصْدَ مِنْهُ تَعْيِينُ مُسَمَّاهُ، وَالمَعْنَى الأَصْلِيُّ التَّرْكِيبِيُّ غَيْرُ مَلْحُوظ فِيهِ البَيَّةَ . وَاسْتَحْسَنَ الشَّيْخُ السَّنُوسِيُّ أَنْ يُزَادَ فِيهِ بَعْدَ مَقْصُودَةٌ» : «خَالِصَةٌ» ; أَيْ : مِنْ شَوَائِبِ العَلَمِيَّةِ ; لِلتَّحَرُّزِ بِهِ عَنِ العَلَمِ الإِضَافِيِّ الَّذِي يُقْصَدُ بِهِ مَعَ تَعْيِينِ مُسَمَّاهُ إِفَادَةَ مَعْنَاهُ التَّرْكِيبِيِّ لِغَرَضِ تَفَاوُلٍ، أَوْ مَدْحٍ، أَوْ غَيْرِهِ، كَمَا فِي : «أَبِي السُّعُودِ وَ: «حُجَّةِ الإِسْلَام وَنَحْوِهِمَا، وَكَأَنَّ القَوْمَ لَمْ يَغْتَبِرُوا هَذِهِ الدَّلَالَةَ؛ لِأَنَّ المَقْصُودَ بِالذَّاتِ مِنَ العَلَمِ إِنَّمَا هُوَ تَعْيِينُ المُسَمَّى، لَا مَعْنَاهُ الأَصْلِيَّ.
Indeed, it (the compound) is composed of two things: from 'رؤية' (sight) and 'جولان' (roaming). The correct view is that both are excluded by what precedes this condition. And al-Sanusi and others added to the definition: 'intended indication'; they said: to guard against something like 'الحيوان الناطق' when made a proper name for a human, for each of its two parts indicates a part of the meaning of 'human', but this indication is not intended from the proper name, because the purpose of it is to specify its referent, and the original compositional meaning is not at all regarded in it. And al-Sanusi approved that after 'intended' there be added 'pure', meaning: from the impurities of proper-nameness, to guard against the additive proper name like 'أبو السعود' and 'حجة الإسلام' and similar, where with specifying its referent, conveying its compositional meaning is intended for a purpose of boasting or praise or otherwise. And it is as if the scholars did not consider this indication, because the primary purpose of a proper name is only to specify the referent, not its original meaning.
Source pages p. 472

Commentary
This passage is a definitional expansion within Bannani's commentary. The author explains the definition of a compound word (مركب) as given in the preceding text of al-Sullam. The compound is said to be composed of two things: sight (رؤية) and roaming (جولان) — which are words from the rhyme of the poem. The correct view, as Bannani states, is that these two are already excluded by the condition mentioned earlier (that the parts indicate parts of meaning). Then he discusses the addition of 'intended indication' (دلالة مقصودة) proposed by al-Sanusi and others. The purpose is to exclude cases like the phrase 'الحيوان الناطق' when it is used as a proper name for a specific human. In that case, each part (حيوان, ناطق) indicates a part of the meaning (animal, rational), but this indication is not intended because the main purpose of a proper name is to specify a referent, not to convey the compositional meaning. Al-Sanusi further approved adding 'pure' (خالصة) to exclude additive proper names (العلم الإضافي) like 'أبو السعود' and 'حجة الإسلام', which are intended both to specify and to convey praise or meaning. Bannani concludes that the scholars (القوم) disregarded this kind of indication in proper names, because the essential purpose of a proper name is only to specify the referent, not its original meaning. Thus, the additions may be unnecessary.

Key Terms
مُرَكَّب
دَلَالَة مَقْصُودَة
خَالِصَة
عَلَم

B) To exclude additive proper names that convey both naming and meaning.

Key Takeaway
The definition of compound word in Islamic logic requires that parts indicate parts of meaning intentionally and purely, excluding proper names where composition is incidental or mixed with naming.

Definition of the Single Word and Its Four Types
Bannani defines the single word by negating the compound property and lists four types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four types of single words (mufrad) and justify why each fits the definition.

Core Idea
The single word is defined by what its parts do not indicate, not by having no parts.

In logic, a 'single word' is not simply a word with no parts. Bannani defines it negatively: it is an utterance whose parts do not intentionally indicate parts of its meaning. This seemingly simple definition covers four distinct types, from a single letter to a descriptive phrase used as a name. Understanding these types saves you from confusing compounds with single words in logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Recall the definition of a compound
A compound word has parts that intentionally indicate parts of its meaning. The single word is its opposite: its parts do NOT intentionally indicate parts of its meaning.
2
Understand the four conditions satisfying the definition
The definition covers: (1) words with no parts at all, (2) words with non-indicative parts, (3) words whose parts indicate something outside the meaning, (4) words whose parts are indicative but not intentionally so.
3
Match examples to each type
For each of Bannani's examples (باء, زيد, بعلبك, حيوان ناطق as a name), explain why it fits only one type.
4
Apply the definition to new examples
Given a new utterance, determine whether it is a single word or compound by checking if any part intentionally indicates a part of the meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في مباحث الألفاظ بِعَكْسٍ مَا تَلَا بِعَكْسِ بِالتَّنْوِينِ : خَبَرٌ مُقَدَّمٌ، وَمَا تَلَا»: مُبْتَدَأَ مُؤخَّرٌ، وَمَا : وَاقِعَةٌ عَلَى المُفْرَدِ، وَالمَعْنَى : أَنَّ تَعْرِيفَ المُفْرَدِ الَّذِي تَلَا المُرَكَّبَ كَائِنٌ بِعَكْسِ تَعْرِيفِ المُرَكَّبِ. وَالمُرَادُ : العَكْسُ اللُّغَوِيُّ ، أَيْ: بِخِلَافِهِ؛ فَيُقَالُ فِي تَعْرِيفِهِ : هُوَ : اللَّفْظُ الَّذِي لَا يَدِلُّ جُزُوهُ عَلَى جُزْءِ مَعْنَاهُ ، أَيْ: دَلَالَةٌ مَقْصُودَةٌ، فَيَدْخُلُ فِيهِ : - مَا لَا جُزْءَ لَهُ : بَاءِ الجَرِّ وَلَامِهِ ; لِأَنَّ السَّالِيَةَ تَصْدُقُ مَعَ نَفْيِ المَوْضُوعِ . كَـ - وَمَا لَهُ جُزْءٌ غَيْرُ دَالٍ بِالوَضْعِ كَـ: «زَيْدِ ، وَمِثْلُهُ: أَبْكَمُ، وَإِنْسَانٌ [ج/ ٩]، وَرَجُلٌ ; عَلَى مَا هُوَ الحَقُّ . - وَمَا لَهُ جُزْءٌ دَالٌ عَلَى غَيْرِ جُزْءِ مَعْنَاهُ كَ : بَعْلَبَكَ، وَامْرِئ القَيْسِ . - وَمَا لَهُ جُزْءٌ دَالٌ عَلَى جُزْءِ مَعْنَاهُ دَلَالَةٌ غَيْرَ مَقْصُودَةٍ كَـ: «حَيَوَانٍ نَاطِقٍ عَلَماً عَلَى إِنْسَانٍ مُعَيَّنٍ.
Chapter on investigations of utterances. By the opposite of what followed, 'بِعَكْسٍ' with tanwin: a predicate fronted, and 'ما تلا' is a subject delayed, and 'ما' stands for the singular. The meaning: the definition of the singular [word] that followed the compound is by the opposite of the definition of the compound. The intended is the linguistic opposite, i.e., contrary to it. So it is said in its definition: it is the utterance whose part does not indicate a part of its meaning, that is, with intended indication. Thus it includes: (1) what has no part: like the preposition 'باء' and its 'لام' because the negative statement [that there is no indication] holds true even with the negation of the subject. (2) And what has a part that does not indicate conventionally: like 'زيد', and similar: 'أبكم', and 'إنسان', and 'رجل' according to the correct view. (3) And what has a part that indicates something other than a part of its meaning: like 'بعلبك' and 'امرئ القيس'. (4) And what has a part that indicates a part of its meaning with unintentional indication: like 'حيوان ناطق' as a proper name for a specific human.
Source pages p. 473

Commentary
This passage is a definitional classification. Bannani explains the definition of the single word (المفرد) as given in the poem by its logical opposite: if the compound word is defined as having parts that indicate parts of its meaning, then the single word is defined as not having that property. The definition: a single word is an utterance whose part does not indicate a part of its meaning with intended indication. This definition covers four types of single words. First: words with no parts at all, like the preposition 'باء' and its 'لام' (e.g., the Arabic letter ب or ل), because the statement 'no part indicates' holds even though there is no subject. Second: words that have parts but they are not conventionally indicative, like the proper name 'زيد', and similar words like 'أبكم' (dumb), 'إنسان' (human), and 'رجل' (man) according to the correct view — because their parts (like the letters) do not indicate any part of their meaning by convention. Third: words whose parts indicate something not part of the meaning, like the place name 'بعلبك' (Baalbek) and the personal name 'امرئ القيس' (Imru' al-Qays), because 'بعل' and 'بك' are not parts of the meaning of 'Baalbek', and 'امرؤ' and 'قيس' are not parts of the meaning of the person. Fourth: words whose parts do indicate parts of the meaning but unintentionally, like the phrase 'حيوان ناطق' when used as a proper name for a specific human; each part indicates a part of the meaning (animal, rational) but this indication is not intended from the proper name. Thus, all these are considered single words in the logical sense.

Key Terms
مُفْرَد
جُزْء
دَلَالَة مَقْصُودَة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of the fourth type of single word (parts indicate parts of meaning but unintentionally)?
A) 'زيد'
B) 'بعلبك'
C) 'حيوان ناطق' used as a name
D) 'باء'

Key Takeaway
The single word is not defined by its length or number of letters, but by whether its parts carry intended meaning about its own meaning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Examine the utterance 'بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم'. Identify which parts are single words and which are compounds, justifying each according to Bannani's definition.
?
Provide an original example for each of the four types of single word from an Islamic text (e.g., from the Qur'an or Hadith) and explain why it qualifies.
